Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf on Traveling Oath-Taker: Actual Cohabitation Required
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath-breaking condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether an oath not to cohabit is broken when the oath-taker travels, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
An oath not to cohabit is not broken unless the oath-taker is actually cohabiting — mere residence of the other does not suffice.

In oaths of abstention, the key question is whether the prohibited action has occurred. This lesson examines a specific scenario: a man swears not to cohabit with someone, then travels while the other person resides with his family. Does the oath break? The Hanafi school has two views, with the fatwa on Abu Yusuf's side. We will analyze the reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the oath
The oath-taker (الحالف) swears not to cohabit (يساكن) with someone (فلان). Then the oath-taker travels, while the other person settles with his family in the same place. Determine what the oath prohibits and what actually occurred.
The oath is about 'musakanah' – sharing a dwelling or cohabiting.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken (يحدث). He likely considers that the other's residence alone constitutes cohabitation in a general sense, even if the oath-taker is absent.
Full reasoning not given in this text, but it emphasizes a broader understanding of cohabitation.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is not broken. His reasoning: the oath-taker is not actually cohabiting (لم يساكنه حقيقة) because he is traveling. Actual cohabitation requires physical presence.
Abu Yusuf insists on literal, physical cohabitation (حقيقة).
4
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The text explicitly states: 'وعليه الفتوى' — the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. This is the relied-upon position for issuing legal opinions.
Tarjih authority: The text itself (likely from later Hanafi works).
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
According to the mufta bihi view, if the oath-taker travels, he does not break his oath. According to Abu Hanifa, he would break. The practical consequence: expiation (kaffarah) is not required under the fatwa, but would be under Abu Hanifa's view.
This affects how a mufti advises someone who took such an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سافر الحالف فسكن فلان أهله ؛ قال أبو حنيفة يحنث، وقال أبو يوسف لا وعليه الفتوى لأن الحالف لم يساكنه حقيقة ا هـ .
And if the oath-taker travels and so-and-so settles with his family; Abu Hanifa said he breaks the oath, and Abu Yusuf said no, and upon it is the fatwa because the oath-taker did not actually cohabit with him.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath not to cohabit. The situation: the oath-taker travels (so he leaves the place), but the other person (فلان) settles with his family in the same place. Does the oath-taker break his oath? Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken because the concept of 'cohabitation' can be realized even when one party is absent? The text does not give his full reasoning. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that since the oath-taker is not actually cohabiting (he is traveling), the oath is not broken. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf. This shows that in Hanafi fatwa, actual physical cohabitation is required; mere residence of the other party does not break the oath.

Key Terms
الحالف (al-halif): The person who takes the oath
المساكنة حقيقة (al-musakanah haqiqah): Actual cohabitation; literally sharing a dwelling
الفتوى (al-fatwa): The operative, relied-upon legal opinion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, if a man swears not to cohabit with someone and then travels while the other resides with his family, does the oath-taker break his oath?
A) Yes, because the other is residing.
B) Yes, because the oath was absolute.
C) No, because the oath-taker is not actually cohabiting.
D) No, unless he returns.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, an oath not to cohabit is only broken when the oath-taker is actually cohabiting; mere temporary absence prevents the breach.

Oaths on Dwelling, Staying, and Cohabitation: Verb Distinctions and Definiteness
Rulings on oaths related to residing, staying, and cohabitation with distinctions in duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for oaths using the verbs أقام, ساكن, سكن, بات, and قعد, and apply the definite/indefinite distinction to determine whether the oath is broken by a brief action or requires the full period.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oath law, the verb determines if a moment breaks the oath or the full period is required; definite vs indefinite wording further refines the ruling.

This lesson examines a series of rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on oaths related to dwelling, staying, cohabitation, spending the night, and sitting. The key is the verb and the definiteness of the time period. Understanding these nuances is essential for correct fatwa in everyday disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb and its legal connotation
Determine whether the verb used in the oath implies continuity or not. For example, 'أقام' (stay) requires the full period, whereas 'ساكن' (cohabitate) and 'سكن' (reside) do not require continuity—an hour breaks the oath.
The verb 'بات' (spend the night) is tied to the night period, and 'قعد' (sit) is literal unless the person is a resident.
2
Check the wording for definiteness
If the time period is definite (e.g., 'شهر كذا' meaning a specific month), the oath is tied to that month. If indefinite (e.g., 'شهراً'), the full month is required for verbs that imply continuity, but for non-continuous verbs like 'ساكن', even an hour breaks it.
Custom and intention can override the apparent meaning; the swearer may be believed in private.
3
Apply the ruling to the specific verb
For 'لا أقيم شهراً': not broken until full month. For 'لا أساكن شهراً': broken with an hour. For 'لا أسكن شهراً': broken with an hour (like ساكن). For 'لا أبيت': consider if the person leaves but family stays; oath is on self. For 'لا يجمعني سقف': broken by sitting together in any shared space, but not by praying in same congregation without sitting together.
Note the exception for residence oaths: some Hanafi scholars differ on whether 'سكنى' requires full month; the author prefers the majority view.
4
Consider intention and custom
If the swearer has a specific intention, it is considered in private but not in court unless there is supporting custom. The definite/indefinite distinction can be clarified by customary usage.
Al-Ramli and the author discuss that custom should be followed if it clarifies interpretation.
5
Understand the intra-madhhab dispute on residence
For an oath like 'If I reside in this house for Ramadan, my slave is free', there is a dispute: some Hanafis require the whole month, others (like al-Qadi al-Amiri) hold that an hour breaks it. The author rules that the whole month is required unless the swearer intended an hour, which is the Shafi'i position.
This dispute is reflected in the madhhab dispute map.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في يمينه ولكن ذكر داراً على التنكير وباقي المسألة بحالها لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يساكن فلاناً شهر كذا فساكنه ساعة في ذلك الشهر حنث لأن المساكنة مما لا يمتد، ولو قال لا أقيم بالرقة شهراً لا يحنث ما لم يقم جميع الشهر، ولو حلف لا يسكن الرقة شهراً فسكن ساعة حنث، ولو حلف لا يبيت الليلة في هذا المنزل فخرج بنفسه وبات خارج المنزل وأهله ومتاعه في المنزل لا يحنث، وهذه اليمين تكون على نفسه لا على المتاع ولو حلف لا يبيت على سطح هذا البيت وعلى البيت غرفة وأرض الغرفة سطح هذا البيت يحنث إن بات عليه، ولو حلف لا يبيت على سطح فبات على هذا لا يحنث ولو قال والله لا أبيت في منزل فلان غداً فهو باطل إلا أن ينوي الليلة الجائية وكذا لو قال بعد ما مضى أكثر الليلة، ولو قال لا أكون غداً في منزل فلان فهو على ساعة من الغد ا هـ وفي الخلاصة : لو قال والله لا أسكن هذه الدار ثلاثين يوماً أو قال لأسكنن هذه الدار ثلاثين يوماً له أن يفرق، ولو حلف لا يسكن هذه القرية فذهب على ما هو الشرط ثم عاد وسكن يحنث ؛ هذا في الفتاوى الصغرى. وأفتى القاضي الإمام أنه إن نوى الفور لا يحنث إذا عاد وسكن وكذا إذا كان هناك مقدمة الفور. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يقعد في هذه الدار ولا نية له قالوا إن كان ساكناً فيها فهو على السكني، وإن لم يكن ساكناً فهو على القعود حقيقة، ولو قال والله لا يجمعني وإياك سقف بيت فهذا على المجالسة، فإن جالسه في بيت أو فسطاط أو سفينة أو خيمة حنث، وإن صلى في مسجد جماعة فصلى الآخر معه في القوم لم يحنث، وإن كان أحدهما في المسجد فجاء الآخر فجلس إليه فقد حنث، وإن جلس بعيداً منه ولم يجلس إليه لم يحنث ا هـ . الرملي : الفرق بين الفرعين هو التعريف والتنكير إذ مع التعريف معناه في شهر كذا ومع التنكير معناه مدة شهر وإلا فكل من المساكنة وإلا إقامة مما يمتد إذ يقال سكنت في الدار شهراً وأقمت فيه شهراً تأمل. أقول : أيضاً عندي في الأول نظر إذا المتبادر من قوله لا أساكنه شهر كذا توقيت الحلف بالشهر فينبغي أن لا يحنث إذ معناه لا أساكنه مدة شهر .كذا ثم رأيت في الخانية والتتارخانية أنه تصح نيته في ذلك ويدين في كل من مسألتي التعريف والتنكير والظاهر الاحتمال لكل منهما، فإذا كان العرف يقضي بشيء منهما أتبع فظهر بحمد الله تعالى صحة ما بحثته. وفي التتارخانية : فإن كان الحالف في مسألة المساكنة قال عنيت مساكنة فلان جميع شهر رمضان على سبيل الدوام دين ولا يدين في القضاء. وكان الفقيه أبو بكر الأعمش والبخاري يقول : ينبغي أن يدين في القضاء والصحيح الأول. هذا إذا عقد يمينه على المساكنة وإن عقد يمينه على السكنى بأن قال إن سكنت هذه الدار شهر رمضان فعبدي حر لم يذكر محمد هذه المسألة في الجامع، وقد اختلف فيها المشايخ فبعضهم قال لا يحنث ما لم يسكن فيها جميع الشهر، وبعضهم قال يحنث إذا سكن فيها ساعة وإلى هذا مال القاضي العامري اهـ :أقول فتحرر أن فيها اختلاف الرواية والذي يقتضيه النظر الفقهي أن لا يحنث إلا بسكنى الجميع ما لم ينو سكنى ساعة منه وهو مذهب الشافعي بخلاف لا أسكن في هذا الشهر أو في هذه السنة فإنه يحنث بسكنى ساعة . اهـ ملخصاً
Regarding his oath, but he mentioned a house indefinitely, and the rest of the issue as it stands, he does not break it. And if he swore, 'I will not cohabitate with so-and-so for a certain month,' and he cohabitated with him for an hour in that month, he breaks it because cohabitation does not require continuity. And if he said, 'I will not stay in al-Raqqa for a month,' he does not break it unless he stays the entire month. And if he swore, 'I will not reside in al-Raqqa for a month,' and he resided for an hour, he breaks it. And if he swore, 'I will not spend the night in this house,' and he himself went out and stayed outside the house while his family and belongings are in the house, he does not break it. And this oath is about himself, not about belongings. And if he swore, 'I will not spend the night on the roof of this house,' and the house has an upper room and the floor of the upper room is the roof of this house, he breaks it if he spends the night on it. And if he swore, 'I will not spend the night on a roof,' and he spent the night on this [particular roof], he does not break it. And if he said, 'By Allah, I will not spend the night in so-and-so's house tomorrow,' it is invalid unless he intends the coming night. Similarly, if he said after most of the night had passed. And if he said, 'I will not be tomorrow in so-and-so's house,' it refers to an hour of tomorrow. End quote. And in al-Khulasa: If he said, 'By Allah, I will not reside in this house for thirty days,' or he said, 'I will surely reside in this house for thirty days,' he may split [the days]. And if he swore, 'I will not reside in this village,' and he left as required, then returned and resided, he breaks it; this is in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. And al-Qadi al-Imam gave the fatwa that if he intended immediacy, he does not break it when he returns and resides, and likewise if there was a precondition of immediacy. And in al-Muhit: He swore, 'I will not sit in this house,' and he had no intention; they said: If he was a resident, it is about residence; if he was not a resident, it is about sitting literally. And if he said, 'By Allah, no roof of a house shall bring me and you together,' this refers to sitting together. If he sits with him in a house, tent, ship, or tent, he breaks it. If he prayed in a mosque in congregation and the other prayed with him in the congregation, he does not break it. If one was in the mosque and the other came and sat with him, he has broken it. If he sat far from him and did not sit with him, he does not break it. End quote. Al-Ramli: The difference between the two branches is definite and indefinite articles. With the definite, it means 'in a certain month'; with the indefinite, it means 'a period of a month'. Otherwise, both cohabitation and staying require continuity, as one says, 'I resided in the house for a month' and 'I stayed in it for a month.' Consider. I say: Also, I have doubt about the first. The apparent meaning of his statement, 'I will not cohabitate with him for a certain month,' is the oath is tied to the month, so he should not break it because it means 'I will not cohabitate with him for a period of a certain month.' Then I saw in al-Khaniyya and al-Tatar Khaniyya that his intention is valid and he is believed in private in both the definite and indefinite questions. The apparent is both possibilities. So if custom dictates one, it is followed. So praise be to Allah, what I researched is correct. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If the swearer in the cohabitation question said, 'I meant cohabitation with so-and-so for the entire month of Ramadan continuously,' he is believed in private but not in court. And Faqih Abu Bakr al-A'mash and al-Bukhari say he should be believed in court, but the correct is the first. This is if he made his oath about cohabitation. But if he made his oath about residence, such as saying, 'If I reside in this house for the month of Ramadan, my slave is free,' Muhammad did not mention this issue in al-Jami'. The scholars differed: some said he does not break it unless he resides the entire month; some said he breaks it if he resides for an hour, and al-Qadi al-Amiri inclined to this. End quote. I say: It is established that there is difference in transmission, and what legal reasoning requires is that he does not break it unless he resides the whole month, unless he intended residence for an hour, and this is the Shafi'i school, contrary to 'I will not reside in this month' or 'in this year' where he breaks it with an hour of residence. End summary.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a series of rulings on oaths involving residence, cohabitation, and staying. The author first distinguishes between verbs: 'ساكن' (cohabitate) does not require continuity, so a moment breaks the oath; 'أقام' (stay) requires the full period. 'سكن' (reside) like 'ساكن' breaks with a moment. For oaths of spending the night (بيتوتة), leaving the house alone does not break the oath if family and belongings remain, as the oath is on oneself. On roofs, if the roof is also the floor of an upper room, it is considered a roof. If one swears not to spend the night on a roof and sleeps on this specific roof, it does not break the oath because the oath is general. For oaths about 'tomorrow', if one swears not to spend the night tomorrow, it is invalid unless the coming night is intended; if said after most of the night has passed, it also refers to the coming night. 'I will not be tomorrow in so-and-so's house' refers to an hour of tomorrow. From al-Khulasa: an oath not to reside for thirty days allows splitting, while an oath not to reside in a village is broken upon returning after leaving. Al-Qadi al-Imam ruled that if intent of immediacy is present, returning does not break the oath. In al-Muhit: 'لا يقعد' (not to sit) in a house is interpreted based on whether he is a resident or not. The oath 'لا يجمعني وإياك سقف بيت' concerns sitting together; it is broken by sitting in any shared space like house, tent, ship, or tent, but not by praying in the same mosque congregation unless they sit together. The commentary by al-Ramli and the author discusses the difference between definite and indefinite wording in oaths like 'شهر كذا' vs 'شهراً'. Custom governs interpretation. The author concludes that on the issue of 'سكنى' (residence) for a month, the relied-upon view is that the oath is not broken unless the entire month is resided, unless the swearer intended an hour, which is the Shafi'i position. However, for 'لا أسكن في هذا الشهر' (I will not reside in this month), an hour breaks the oath.

Key Terms
المساكنة (al-musākanah): Cohabitation with someone, meaning sharing a dwelling; in this passage it is distinguished from إقامة (staying) and سكنى (residence) in that a moment of cohabitation breaks the oath because it does not require continuity.
التعريف والتنكير (al-taʿrīf wa al-tankīr): Definite and indefinite articles; in oaths, the definite (e.g., 'في شهر كذا') means a specific named month, while indefinite (e.g., 'شهراً') means any month-long period. This affects whether the oath is broken by a part of the period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'I will not spend the night in this house,' and leaves the house but his family and belongings remain, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because the house is occupied.
B) No, because the oath is on himself, not his belongings.
C) No, if he returns before dawn.
D) Yes, if he intended to stay away.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, the verb and definiteness of the time period are decisive: 'إقامة' requires the full period, while 'ساكن' and 'سكن' break with a moment; definite wording ties to the named period, indefinite to the full duration.

Oaths on Exit: Command, Consent, and Compulsion
Ruling on oaths and being carried out: command, consent, compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an oath not to exit is broken if one is carried out by command, consent, or compulsion, and explain the attribution principle that distinguishes each case.

Core Idea
An oath breaks if you command, not if compelled; the action follows the commander.

This lesson examines the nuances of oaths regarding exit and entry when the swearer is physically moved by another. The Hanafi school distinguishes between being carried out by command (which breaks the oath), by consent without command (does not break), and by compulsion (does not break). Understanding this hinges on the principle that an action is attributed to the one who commands it. We also explore whether the oath dissolves after such an act, a point of difference with Sayyid Abu Shuja'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is being carried out of a place after swearing not to exit. Three scenarios are distinguished: (1) carried out by the swearer's command, (2) carried out with his consent but without command, (3) carried out under compulsion. Identify the ruling for each: commanded → breaks; consent without command → does not break; compulsion → does not break.
The term 'break' here means the oath is violated (ḥanatha).
2
Locate the Dalīl (Proof)
The author uses an analogy: riding an animal that exits is attributed to the rider; similarly, commanding another to carry one out is attributed to the commander. This analogy is the rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī).
The source says: 'like when he rode an animal and it exited.' The illa is the presence of command.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalālah
The linking factor (illa) is that the action of the commanded is legally attributed to the commander. Since the swearer commanded the carrying, the exit is considered his action, thus breaking the oath. Without command, the action is not attributed to him.
Consent alone does not transfer the action; only command does.
4
Note the Ikhtilāf (Dispute)
Sayyid Abū Shujāʿ held that after being carried out (even without command), the oath dissolves (inḥilāl), so a later voluntary exit does not break it. The majority (including the author) maintain the oath does not dissolve.
The dispute is about dissolution, not about breaking at the moment of being carried. The majority view is that the oath remains intact.
5
Evaluate the Tarjīḥ (Preference)
The author declares the view that the oath does not dissolve as correct (al-ṣaḥīḥ), citing Fatḥ al-Qadīr. Thus, if later the person voluntarily exits, he breaks the oath and must expiate.
This is the relied-upon (muftā bihī) position in the Hanafi school.

قوله : (لا يخرج فاخرج محمولاً بأمره حنث وبرضاه لا بأمره أو مكرهاً لا) أي لا يحنث وهو شروع في بعض مسائل الحلف على الخروج. فإذا حلف لا يخرج من المسجد مثلاً فأمر إنساناً فحمله وأخرجه حنث لأن فعل المأمور مضاف إلى الآمر فصار كما إذا ركب دابة فخرجت، ولو أخرجه مكرهاً لم يحنث لأن الفعل لم ينتقل اليه لعدم الأمر، ولو حمله برضاه لا بأمره لا يحنث في الصحيح لأن الانتقال بالأمر لا بمجرد الرضا، وإذا لم يحنث فيهما لا تنحل في الصحيح لعدم فعله. وقال السيد أبو شجاع تنحل وهو أرفق بالناس، ويظهر أثر هذا الاختلاف فما لو دخل بعد هذا الإخراج هل يحنث ؟ فمن قال انحلت قال لا يحنث وهذا بيان كونه أرفق بالناس، ومن قال لا تنحل قال حنث ووجبت الكفارة وهو الصحيح؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وصوابه إن كان الحلف بأنه لا يخرج أن يظهر فيما لو دخل بعد هذا الإخراج ثم خرج، وإن كان الحلف بأنه لا يدخل فنعم. قيد بكونه أخرج مكرهاً أي حمله المكره وأخرجه لأنه لو خرج بنفسه مكرهاً وهو الإكراه المعروف وهو أن يتوعده حتى يفعل فإنه رف أن الإكراه لا يعدم الفعل عندنا ونظيره ما لو حلف لا يأكل هذا الطعام فأكره عليه حتى أكله حنث، ولو أوجر في حلقه لا يحنث ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . وبهذا ظهر أن هذا الحكم لا يختص بالحلف على الخروج لأنه لو حلف لا يدخل فأدخل محمولاً بأمره حنث وبر برضاه لا بأمره أو مكرهاً لا . وفي المجتبى : لو هبت به الريح وأدخلته لم يحنث وفي الانحلال كلام وفيمن زلق فوقع فيها أو كان راكباً دابة فانفلتت ولم يستطع حينئذ يحنث لما عرف أن الإكراه لا يعدم الفعل عندنا اعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأنه مناقض لما مر قبل هذا بنحو ورقتين من أن للإكراه تأثيراً في إعدام الفعل وقد يجاب بأن قوله هنا لا يعدم الفعل» أي لا يرفعه بعد وجوده وصدوره من فاعله وقوله هناك إن له تأثيراً في إعدامه» أي في إعدام نسبته إلى فاعله حيث كان مفوتاً للاختيار والحاصل أن الإكراه إن أثر في إعدام الاختيار لا ينسب إلى فاعله والأنسب كما في مسألتنا فإنه ما خرج إلا باختياره. نعم الإكراه أبطل رضاه بخلاف مسألة الإيجار فإنه لم يوجد الرضا ولا الاختيار، وكذا مسألة السكنى السابقة وعبارة الخانية في تعليلها هكذا لأن
His statement: (He does not exit, so if he is carried out by his command, he breaks the oath; by his consent without command, or by compulsion, he does not). That is, he does not break the oath. This is the beginning of some issues on oaths regarding exit. So if he swore, 'I will not exit the mosque,' for example, and he commanded a person and he carried him and brought him out, he breaks the oath because the action of the commanded is attributed to the commander, so it is as if he rode an animal and it went out. And if he brought him out under compulsion, he does not break the oath because the action did not transfer to him due to lack of command. And if he carried him with his consent but not by his command, he does not break the oath in the correct view because the transfer is by command, not merely by consent. And if he does not break the oath in both cases, the oath is not dissolved in the correct view due to lack of his action. Sayyid Abu Shuja' said it dissolves, which is more convenient for people. The effect of this difference appears when he enters after this exit: does he break the oath? Those who said it dissolves say he does not break it, and this shows it is more convenient. Those who said it does not dissolve say he breaks it and must make expiation, which is correct; as in Fath al-Qadir. The correct understanding: if the oath was that he will not exit, the effect appears when he enters after this exit and then exits; if the oath was that he will not enter, then yes. The condition that he was brought out under compulsion means that the one compelling carried him and brought him out, because if he himself went out under compulsion—which is the known compulsion, i.e., he threatens him until he does it—then know that compulsion does not negate the action in our school. An analogy: if he swore not to eat this food and was compelled to eat it, he breaks it; if it was poured down his throat, he does not break it; as in Fath al-Qadir. From this it is clear that this ruling is not specific to oaths about exiting, because if he swore not to enter and was carried in by his command, he breaks it; with his consent without command or under compulsion, he does not. In al-Mujtaba: If the wind blew him and brought him in, he does not break it. There is discussion about dissolution. And regarding one who slipped and fell into it, or was riding an animal that bolted and he could not [control it], then he breaks it, due to what is known that compulsion does not negate the action in our school. A certain virtuous person objected that this contradicts what was mentioned about two pages earlier that compulsion has an effect in negating the action. It may be answered that his statement here 'does not negate the action' means it does not remove it after its existence and occurrence from its doer, and his statement there 'has an effect in negating it' means negating its attribution to its doer where it eliminates choice. The sum: if compulsion eliminates choice, it is not attributed to its doer, and the appropriate [interpretation] is as in our issue, for he did not exit except by his choice. Yes, compulsion nullified his consent, unlike the case of pouring, where neither consent nor choice existed. Similarly, the earlier issue of residence, and the statement of al-Khaniyya in its reasoning is: because...
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths on exiting and entering. The key principle is that an action carried out by the swearer's command is attributed to him; thus, if he orders someone to carry him out, he breaks the oath. In contrast, if he is carried out under compulsion (ikrāh) without his command, the action is not attributed to him, so the oath is not broken. Consent alone (without command) also does not attribute the action to him. The correct view is that such acts do not dissolve the oath; it remains intact and if he later exits voluntarily, he breaks it. Sayyid Abu Shujā' held that the oath dissolves after such an act, but the relied-upon view is that it does not, as upheld in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. The discussion extends to being carried in (by analogy), and to cases like being blown by wind or slipping. The objection that this contradicts earlier statements about compulsion negating action is resolved by distinguishing between negating the existence of the act (which compulsion does not do) and negating its attribution to the agent (which compulsion can do if it removes choice). In the case of being carried out by command, the swearer's choice is present, so the act is attributed to him.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compulsion that leaves no choice; in this passage, it is distinguished from mere lack of command. Being carried out under compulsion (ikrāh) does not break the oath because the action is not attributed to the swearer.
انحلال اليمين (inḥilāl al-yamīn): The dissolution or expiration of an oath; the debate is whether an oath not to exit dissolves when the swearer is removed from the place by another's action, so that no further expiation is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone swears, 'By Allah, I will not exit this mosque.' He then orders someone to carry him out. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break the oath because he was carried, not walking.
B) He breaks the oath because the action is attributed to him through command.
C) He does not break the oath because he was compelled.
D) The oath dissolves and he does not need expiation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath not to exit is broken only if the swearer commands the act of exiting; being carried by consent or compulsion does not break it, and the oath remains intact.

The Oath Not to Exit: Attribution and the Effect of Coercion
Explaining the ruling for an oath not to exit when carried out by order, consent, or coercion, with an intra-madhhab dispute on dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking an oath when carried out by order, consent, or coercion, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute on dissolution of the oath.

Core Idea
The oath is only broken by a voluntary act; being carried out without command or under coercion does not trigger hanath, and the oath remains unless voluntarily broken.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle point in oaths: if a person swears not to exit and is then carried out by another, does he break his oath? The answer depends on whether he commanded the act, merely consented, or was coerced. The commentator also explores whether the oath dissolves if no breach occurs, introducing an intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the matn and commentary. There are three ways to be carried out: (1) by explicit order (amr), (2) with consent but no order, (3) under coercion (ikrah). Determine the ruling for each.
The matn says: 'by his order: he breaks; with his consent without order: no; coerced: no.'
2
Understand the principle of attribution
The commentary states that the action of the one commanded is attributed to the commander (al-mamur yudaf ila al-amir). This is why an order leads to hanath, while mere consent does not transfer the act.
Analogy: riding an animal that exits – the rider's command causes the exit.
3
Examine the dispute on dissolution (inhilal)
If no hanath occurs, does the oath dissolve? The sahih position says no; Sayyid Abu Shuja' says yes, for public benefit. Note the practical consequence: if the person later enters and exits again, the dissolution view would not require kaffarah, while the sahih view would.
The author of Fath al-Qadir confirms the sahih position is relied upon.
4
Analyze the definition of coercion
The passage distinguishes being carried out by a coercer (mukrih) from exiting under threat (ikrah). Under threat, the oath-taker still acts with some choice, so hanath occurs. But if the food is poured down his throat, no hanath.
Coercion annuls consent but not the act itself; only physical compulsion without any choice negates hanath.
5
Apply the ruling to analogous cases
The passage extends the ruling to oaths about entering, and gives examples: wind blowing him in (no hanath), slipping into a place (hanath if he caused it), riding an animal that bolts (hanath if he could control it).
Attribution depends on causation and choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وهو شروع في بعض مسائل الحلف على الخروج. فإذا حلف لا يخرج من المسجد مثلاً فأمر إنساناً فحمله وأخرجه حنث لأن فعل المأمور مضاف إلى الآمر فصار كما إذا ركب دابة فخرجت، ولو أخرجه مكرهاً لم يحنث لأن الفعل لم ينتقل اليه لعدم الأمر، ولو حمله برضاه لا بأمره لا يحنث في الصحيح لأن الانتقال بالأمر لا بمجرد الرضا، وإذا لم بحنث فيهما لا تنحل في الصحيح لعدم فعله. وقال السيد أبو شجاع تنحل وهو أرفق بالناس، ويظهر أثر هذا الاختلاف فما لو دخل بعد هذا الإخراج هل يحنث ؟ فمن قال انحلت قال لا يحنث وهذا بيان كونه أرفق بالناس، ومن قال لا تنحل قال حنث ووجبت الكفارة وهو الصحيح؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وصوابه إن كان الحلف بأنه لا يخرج أن يظهر فيما لو دخل بعد هذا الإخراج ثم خرج، وإن كان الحلف بأنه لا يدخل فنعم. قيد بكونه أخرج مكرهاً أي حمله المكره وأخرجه لأنه لو خرج بنفسه مكرهاً وهو الإكراه المعروف وهو أن يتوعده حتى يفعل فإنه رف أن الإكراه لا يعدم الفعل عندنا ونظيره ما لو حلف لا يأكل هذا الطعام فأكره عليه حتى أكله حنث، ولو أوجر في حلقه لا يحنث ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . وبهذا ظهر أن هذا الحكم لا يختص بالحلف على الخروج لأنه لو حلف لا يدخل فأدخل محمولاً بأمره حنث وبر برضاه لا بأمره أو مكرهاً لا . وفي المجتبى : لو هبت به الريح وأدخلته لم يحنث وفي الانحلال كلام وفيمن زلق فوقع فيها أو كان راكباً دابة فانفلتت ولم يستطع

بسكنى الجميع ما لم ينو سكنى ساعة منه وهو مذهب الشافعي بخلاف لا أسكن في هذا الشهر أو في هذه السنة فإنه يحنث بسكنى ساعة . اهـ ملخصاً قوله : وهذا بيان كونه أرفق بالناس) ذكر الرملي أنه أخبره بعض من يثق به عن المؤلف أنه أفتى بهذا ثم قال أقول : الظاهر أنه مال إلى ما هو بالناس مع كونه خلاف الصحيح من المذهب وقد نقرت في فتاواه التي هي واقعاته فلم أجد هذه الفتيا فيها بل رأيت ما يعكر عليها في أثناء كلامه في مثلها فإنه قال : لا يحنث وإذا لم يحنث لا تنحل اليمين فهي باقية والله تعالى أعلم .اهـ. قلت قد رأيت ذلك الذي أفتى به صاحب البحر في فتاواه المرتبة ثم نقل مرتبها عبارة البحر ثم قال : لعل شيخنا أفتى بانحلالها لكونه أرفق بالناس قوله : (لما عرف أن الإكراه لا يعدم الفعل عندنا اعترضه بعض الفضلاء بأنه مناقض لما مر قبل هذا بنحو ورقتين من أن للإكراه تأثيراً في إعدام الفعل وقد يجاب بأن قوله هنا لا يعدم الفعل» أي لا يرفعه بعد وجوده وصدوره من فاعله وقوله هناك إن له تأثيراً في إعدامه» أي في إعدام نسبته إلى فاعله حيث كان مفوتاً للاختيار والحاصل أن الإكراه إن أثر في إعدام الاختيار لا ينسب إلى فاعله والأنسب كما في مسألتنا فإنه ما خرج إلا باختياره. نعم الإكراه أبطل رضاه بخلاف مسألة الإيجار فإنه لم يوجد الرضا ولا الاختيار، وكذا مسألة السكنى السابقة وعبارة الخانية في تعليلها هكذا لأن
Book of Oaths, Chapter: The Oath Regarding Entering and Exiting. And its family: breaking of oath: if one swears not to exit, then he is carried out by his order, he breaks his oath; and with his consent without his order, no; or coerced, no. His statement: '(He does not exit, then he is carried out by his order: he breaks his oath; with his consent without his order, no; or coerced, no)' meaning he does not break his oath. This is a beginning of some issues of the oath on exit. So if he swears not to exit from the masjid, for example, then orders a person to carry him and take him out, he breaks his oath because the action of the one commanded is attributed to the commander, so it becomes as if he rode an animal and it exited. And if he takes him out coerced, he does not break his oath because the action is not transferred to him due to the lack of command. And if he carries him with his consent but without his order, he does not break his oath in the correct position because transfer is by command, not by mere consent. And if he does not break his oath in both cases, the oath is not dissolved in the correct position due to the lack of his action. Sayyid Abu Shuja' said it dissolves, and it is kinder to people. The effect of this difference appears in the case where he enters after this expulsion: does he break his oath? One who said it dissolves said he does not break his oath, and this is an explanation of it being kinder to people. And one who said it does not dissolve said he breaks his oath and expiation becomes obligatory, and that is the correct position; as in Fath al-Qadir. And the correct [answer] is that if the oath was that he would not exit, then it appears in the case where he enters after this expulsion and then exits. And if the oath was that he would not enter, then yes. He restricted it to being coerced, meaning the one compelling carried him and took him out, because if he himself exits coerced—which is the known coercion, that he threatens him so that he does it—then it is established that coercion does not annul the act according to us. An analogy is if he swears not to eat this food and is coerced until he eats it, he breaks his oath. And if it is poured into his throat, he does not break his oath; as in Fath al-Qadir. And thereby it appears that this ruling is not specific to the oath on exit, because if he swears not to enter and is carried in by his order, he breaks his oath; with his consent without order, no; or coerced, no. In al-Mujtaba: if the wind blows him and brings him in, he does not break his oath; but regarding dissolution there is discussion. And regarding one who slips and falls into it, or was riding an animal that bolted and he could not [control it], then he breaks his oath because... It is kinder regarding residence for all as long as he did not intend residence of an hour from it, and this is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i, unlike 'I will not reside in this month or this year' for he breaks his oath by residence of an hour. End summarized. His statement: 'And this is an explanation of it being kinder to people.' al-Ramli mentioned that a trusted person told him from the author that he gave fatwa with this. Then he said: I say, apparent is that he inclined to what is kinder to people despite it being contrary to the correct of the madhhab. And I have examined in his fatawa which are his waqi'at, and I did not find this fatwa in them, but I saw what contradicts it in during his speech in similar cases, for he said: he does not break his oath, and when he does not break his oath, the oath does not dissolve, so it remains. And Allah knows best. End. I said: I have seen that which the author of al-Bahr gave fatwa with in his arranged fatawa, then the arranger quoted the wording of al-Bahr and then said: perhaps our shaykh gave fatwa with its dissolution because it is kinder to people. His statement: ('Because it is known that coercion does not annul the act according to us') some learned ones objected to it as contradictory to what passed about two pages before that coercion has an effect in annulling the act. And it can be answered that his statement here 'does not annul the act' means it does not remove it after its existence and occurrence from its doer, and his statement there that 'it has an effect in annulling it' means in annulling its attribution to its doer, as it deprives [him] of choice. The outcome is that coercion, if it affects annulment of choice, is not attributed to its doer, and what is appropriate is as in our case, for he did not exit except by his choice. Yes, coercion invalidated his consent, unlike the case of pouring into the throat, for there was neither consent nor choice. And likewise the previous case of residence, and the wording of al-Khaniyya in its reasoning is thus because
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn statement regarding an oath not to exit. It begins by clarifying the ruling when the oath-taker is carried out of a place like a mosque. If he orders someone to carry him out, he breaks his oath because the commanded act is attributed to the commander. This is analogized to riding an animal that exits—he caused the exit indirectly. If he is coerced (e.g., forced by another person against his will), he does not break his oath because the coerced act is not attributed to him under command. If he consents to being carried without giving an order, the correct position is that he does not break his oath, because attribution requires command, not mere consent. The passage then discusses whether the oath is dissolved (inhalal) when no breach occurs. The relied-upon position (sahih) is that the oath remains, but Sayyid Abu Shuja' held it dissolves, being kinder to people. The practical difference appears if he later enters and exits again—according to the dissolution view, he does not break his oath on re-entry, while according to the sahih view, he does. The author then clarifies that coercion here means being carried by the coercer; if he exits himself under threat, that is defined as duress (ikrah), which in the Hanafi school does not annul the act itself but only the attribution? Actually the text explains that ikrah does not annul the act (i.e., the act still occurs), but it may affect attribution. For example, if he swears not to eat food and is forced to eat, he breaks his oath; but if it is poured down his throat without his action, he does not. This ruling applies analogously to oaths about entering as well. Further examples are given: if wind blows him in, he does not break his oath; if he slips and falls in, or if his animal bolts and he cannot control it, he does break his oath. The passage then discusses the application of the 'kinder to people' principle and records a dispute about the author's own fatwa, concluding that the correct and relied-upon position is that the oath is not dissolved.

Key Terms
الحنث (al-hanath): Breaking of an oath, resulting in the obligation of kaffarah (expiation).
مكرهاً (mukrahan): Being compelled by another person against one's will, such that the coerced act is not attributed to the oath-taker for the purpose of hanath.
الانحلال (al-inhilal): Dissolution of the oath; the oath becomes null without needing to be broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to exit his house, and his wife (without his order) carries him out with his consent, does he break his oath according to the correct Hanafi position?
A) Yes, because he consented.
B) No, because he did not order her.
C) Yes, because consent implies agency.
D) No, because coercion negates the act.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, breaking an oath requires a voluntary act attributable to the oath-taker; being carried out without command or under coercion does not break the oath, and the oath remains unless voluntarily violated.

Defining Exit (Khuruj) in Hanafi Oath Law
Definition and first ruling from al-Bada'i on the nature of exit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khuruj and apply it to determine when an oath of not exiting is breached.

Core Idea
Exit is separation from interior to exterior; staying after exit is not a new exit.

The Hanafi school defines 'khuruj' (exit) precisely to resolve oath disputes. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, quoting al-Bada'i, establishes that exit means separating from the interior to the exterior. It clarifies that remaining in the new place after exit does not constitute another exit. This definition affects oaths regarding leaving houses, cities, and specific locations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
Consider an oath like 'I will not exit except to a funeral.' The core issue is what counts as exit and how exceptions operate.
Focus on the literal act of exiting, not intention unless specified.
2
Extract the definition of khuruj
From al-Bada'i: khuruj is 'al-infisal min al-hisn ila al-‘awra' – separation from the fortress to the open land. This is a physical change of location.
This definition applies to all places with a boundary: houses, cities, tents, ships.
3
Distinguish exit types
Exit from a house requires taking self, possessions, and family; exit from a city only requires one's body. The type affects what fulfills the oath condition.
This differentiation prevents overly broad violations.
4
Apply the exception principle
An excepted exit (e.g., to a funeral) does not break the oath. Subsequent movement after the excepted act is not considered a new exit because 'staying after exit is not exit'.
The exception covers the entire act; later actions are separate.
5
Note the role of intention
If someone swears not to exit while in a room and goes to the courtyard, it is not a violation unless they intended to exit the entire house. Intent is considered only if explicit in the oath.
Unspecified intentions are not accepted judicially.

كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج يخرج إلا إلى جنازة فخرج إليها ثم أتى حاجة لا يخرج أو لا يذهب إلى مكة فخرج إمساكها فأدخلته خلاف ا هـ . وفي البدائع : الخروج هو الانفصال من الحصن إلى العورة على مضادة الدخول فلا يكون المكث بعد الخروج خروجاً كما لا يكون المكث بعد الدخول دخولاً، ثم الخروج كما يكون في البلدان والدور والمنازل والبيوت تكون من الأخبية والفساطيط والخيم والسفن لوجود حده، والخروج من الدور المسكونة أن يخرج الحالف بنفسه ومتاعه وعياله كما إذا حلف لا يسكن، والخروج من البلدان والقرى أن يخرج الحالف ببدنه خاصة. ولو قال والله لا أخرج وهو في بيت من الدار فخرج إلى صحن الدار لم يحنث إلا أن ينوي، فإن نوى الخروج إلى مكة أو خروجاً من البلد لم يصدق قضاء ولا ديانة لأن غير المذكور لا يحتمل التخصيص. ولو قال إن خرجت من هذه الدار فأنت طالق فخرجت منها من الباب أي باب كان ومن أي موضع كان من فوق حائط أو سطح أو نقب حنث لوجود الشرط وهو الخروج من الدار ، ولو قيد بباب هذه الدار لم يحنث بالخروج من غير الباب قديماً كان الباب أو حادثاً، ولو عين باباً في اليمين تعين ولا يحنث بالخروج من غيره
هذه
قوله : (كلا يخرج إلا إلى جنازة فخرج إليها ثم أتى حاجة يعني لا يحنث لأن الموجود خروج مستثنى والمضي بعد ذلك ليس بخروج. وفي البدائع : لو قال إن خرجت من الدار إلا إلى المسجد فأنت طالق فخرجت تريد المسجد ثم بدا لها فذهبت إلى غير المسجد لم تطلق لما ذكرنا . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو قال إن خرجت من هذه الدار فلان فأنت طالق مع فخرجت وحدها أو مع فلان آخر ثم خرج فلان ولحقها فإنه لا يحنث لأن كلمة «مع» للقران فيقتضي مقارنتها للخروج ولم يوجد لأن المكث بعد الخروج ليس بخروج كما في البدائع أيضاً : ولو خرج في مسألة الكتاب لغير الجنازة فإنه يحنث لوجود الشرط والاعتبار للقصد
The Book of Oaths, Chapter on the Oath Concerning Entering and Exiting. He does not exit except to a funeral, so he exited to it then came to a need. He does not exit or does not go to Mecca, so he exited to her? He entered her contrary to the exception etc. In al-Bada'i': Exit is the separation from the fortress to the open land, opposite of entry. So staying after exit is not exit, just as staying after entry is not entry. Then exit occurs in countries, houses, dwellings, tents, pavilions, tents, and ships because their boundary exists. Exit from inhabited houses is that the oath-taker goes out with himself, his possessions, and his family, as if he swore not to reside. Exit from cities and villages is that the oath-taker goes out with his body alone. If he said, 'By Allah, I will not exit' while he is in a room of the house, then he exited to the courtyard, he does not break his oath unless he intended. If he intended exit to Mecca or exit from the city, he is not believed judicially or morally because the unspecified does not admit specification. If he said, 'If you exit this house, you are divorced,' and she exited from it through the door, any door, and from any place, from over a wall or roof or a breach, she breaks the oath due to the existence of the condition, which is exiting the house. If he restricted to the door of this house, she does not break the oath by exiting from other than the door, whether the door was old or new. If he specified a door in the oath, it is specified, and she does not break the oath by exiting from other than it. This is the text. He said: (Kalla: He does not exit except to a funeral, so he exited to it then came to a need) meaning he does not break the oath because what occurred is an excepted exit, and proceeding after that is not exit. In al-Bada'i': If he said, 'If you exit the house except to the mosque, you are divorced,' and she exited intending the mosque, then it occurred to her to go to other than the mosque, she is not divorced for what we mentioned. And the author indicated that if he said, 'If you exit this house with so-and-so, you are divorced,' and she exited alone or with someone else, then so-and-so exited and caught up with her, he does not break the oath because the word 'with' implies conjunction, requiring simultaneity with the exit, which did not occur because staying after exit is not exit. Also in al-Bada'i': If he exited in the book's issue for other than the funeral, he breaks the oath due to the existence of the condition, and consideration is for the intention.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage consists of a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq ('لا يخرج إلا إلى جنازة') followed by its commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The initial line seems garbled but the commentary clarifies: an oath that forbids exit except for a specific purpose (like a funeral) is not broken by that excepted exit. The author then provides a comprehensive definition of 'exit' from al-Bada'i': it is the separation from the interior to the exterior. Crucially, remaining in the place after the exit does not constitute a new exit. This principle is applied: if someone swears not to exit except to the mosque and goes to the mosque, then changes her mind and goes elsewhere, she is not in violation because the exception was fulfilled and the subsequent movement is not considered exit. The author also addresses conditional divorce: if a man says 'If you exit this house, you are divorced,' any means of exit (door, wall, roof, breach) triggers the divorce because the condition of exiting the house is met. But if he specifies a particular door, only that door counts. The key legal maxim is: 'al-mukth ba‘da al-khuruj laysa bi-khuruj' (staying after exit is not exit).

Key Terms
الخروج (al-khuruj): الانفصال من الداخل إلى الخارج (separation from interior to exterior)
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Excluding a specific case from a general oath
الحنث (al-hanith): Breaking the oath, requiring expiation (kaffarah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following is NOT considered an exit for the purposes of an oath?
A) Moving from one room to another within the same house
B) Stepping out of the front door of the house
C) Leaving the city limits while carrying all belongings
D) Exiting a tent by removing the flap

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the definition of exit is literal: complete separation from the interior, and staying after exit is not exit.

Exceptions and Conditional Divorce in Exit Oaths
Applications of the exit definition to conditional divorce and exception scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mukth ba‘da al-khuruj laysa bi-khuruj' to determine when an oath with an exception is violated and when a conditional divorce takes effect.

Core Idea
An exception in an oath prevents violation; exit through any means triggers divorce unless a specific door is specified.

This passage continues the commentary on oaths, applying the definition of exit to conditional divorce and exceptions. Key scenarios: a husband's conditional divorce 'if you exit this house' is triggered by any exit, but specifying a door restricts it. Also, an oath with an exception (e.g., except for a funeral) is not violated even if additional actions follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of oath
Determine whether it is a conditional divorce (talīq) or a general oath (yamīn). The ruling differs: divorce takes effect immediately upon condition, while oath violation requires expiation.
Conditional divorce is binding irrespective of intention.
2
Determine the condition scope
If the condition is 'if you exit this house' without restriction, any exit (door, wall, roof) triggers the condition. If a specific door is mentioned, only that door counts.
The rule: general conditions are fulfilled by any means; specifications limit the means.
3
Apply the exception principle
If the oath includes an exception (e.g., 'except to the mosque'), the excepted exit does not violate. Subsequent actions are not considered a new exit because of the principle 'staying after exit is not exit.'
The exception covers the entire act; later movement does not reset the violation.
4
Analyze the 'with' (ma‘a) case
If the condition is 'exit with so-and-so,' simultaneous exit is required. If one exits alone and the other catches up later, the condition is not met because staying after exit is not exit.
The word 'ma‘a' implies conjunction, so the exit must be at the same time.
5
Compare with breaking the oath
If the exit is for a non-excepted purpose, the oath is broken. The intention behind the exit matters if the exception is based on purpose (e.g., 'except for a funeral').
Only the stated exception protects; any other purpose triggers violation.

A) Yes, because she exited the house.
B) No, because the condition specified a specific door.
C) Yes, because any exit triggers divorce.
D) No, unless she intended the front door.

What Does 'Exit' Mean in Oaths? House vs City
Clarifies what constitutes exit from a house (with belongings) vs city (by body alone)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'exit' for oaths about houses (al-naqla) versus cities and apply it to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Exiting a house for oaths means full relocation; exiting a city means just the body.

In oaths about leaving places, the meaning of 'exit' can vary. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a house, where exit implies moving family and belongings, and a city, where only bodily exit matters. This lesson unpacks this distinction from the text of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and shows how it applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two location types
Note that the passage distinguishes between inhabited houses (al-dur al-maskunah) and cities/belad. Each has a different standard for what constitutes 'exit'.
Houses are where the oath-taker resides with family; cities are larger geographic areas.
2
State the definition for each
For a house: exit requires the oath-taker to move himself, his possessions, and his family (al-naqla). For a city: exit is simply leaving with one's body.
The source cites al-Khaniya and al-Zahiriyya for this ruling.
3
Note the intra-school nuance
There is discussion about whether the example of a mosque (in the matn) is a restriction. Some scholars say the rule applies generally, others say it is specific to mosques.
Ghayat al-Bayan states the mosque is not a restriction, so the same logic applies to houses.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a hypothetical oath about a house or a city, determine if the action described breaks the oath. Use the definitions: for house, check if family and belongings moved; for city, check if the body left.
Remember that the oath-taker's intention is interpreted according to these default meanings unless evidence suggests otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والخروج من الدور المسكونة أن يخرج الحالف بنفسه ومتاعه وعياله عزاه في الذخيرة والتتارخانية إلى القدوري، وقد قيد في النهر مسألة المتن بقوله حلف لا يخرج من هذا المسجد مثلاً فاخرج محمولاً الخ» ثم نقل عبارة البدائع هذه المسألة قال : وعلى هذا فمن صور في البيت يحمل كلامه على أن الحالف كان تبعاً لغيره في السكنى كما مر اهـ . قلت : وقد وقع تقييد المسألة أيضاً بالمسجد في كلام الإمام محمد في الجامع الصغير لكن قال في غاية البيان: إنه ليس بقيد .اهـ. ويدل عليه ما في الخانية والظهيرية : رجل قال والله لا أخرج من بلد كذا فهو على أن يخرج ببدنه ولو قال لا أخرج من هذه الدار فهو على النقلة منها بأهله إن كان ساكناً فيها ! دل الدليل على أنه أراد به الخروج ببدنه اهـ. فمن صور المسألة بالبيت مراده حيث دل الدليل على أن المراد الخروج ببدنه لكن التصوير بالمسجد كما فعل
His statement: (And exit from inhabited houses is that the oath-taker goes out with himself, his possessions, and his family) — he attributed it in al-Dhakhira and al-TatarKhaniyya to al-Quduri. In al-Nahr, he restricted the issue of the matn by saying, 'He swore not to exit from this mosque, for example, then he was carried out, etc.' Then he transmitted the phrase from al-Bada'i' on this issue. He said: According to this, if one depicts the issue as a house, his words are interpreted as meaning that the oath-taker was dependent on others in residence, as previously. I say: The issue has also been restricted to the mosque in the words of Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, but in Ghayat al-Bayan it is said that it is not a restriction. Evidence for this is what is in al-Khaniya and al-Zahiriyya: A man said, 'By Allah, I will not exit from such a city' — this is interpreted as his exiting with his body. If he said, 'I will not exit from this house' — then it is interpreted as moving from it with his family if he is a resident in it! Evidence indicates that he intended by it exiting with his body. So whoever depicts the issue as a house, his intention is that evidence indicates that the intended meaning is exiting with his body, but the depiction as a mosque is as he did.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of 'exit' from a house versus a city in the context of oaths. The author quotes al-Dhakhira and al-TatarKhaniyya attributing to al-Quduri that exit from an inhabited house requires the oath-taker to move himself, his possessions, and his family. This is analogous to swearing not to reside — residence implies settling with family and belongings. In contrast, exit from a city or village is simply leaving with one's body; one can be said to have left the city even if family remains. The passage then discusses a subtlety: if someone swears not to exit a mosque and is carried out, does that break the oath? The matn in Kanz al-Daqa'iq depicts the issue with a mosque, but Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir also uses the mosque, though Ghayat al-Bayan says it is not a restriction. The key principle from al-Khaniya and al-Zahiriyya is that in an oath about a city, only bodily exit counts; in an oath about a house, the intention is usually to move out entirely (transfer of residence). This is because 'exit' from a house is understood in the custom as relocating, whereas from a city it is simply leaving its boundaries.

Key Terms
النقلة (al-naqla): Moving from one place to another with possessions and family; complete relocation.
الدور المسكونة (al-dur al-maskunah): Inhabited houses where the oath-taker resides with his family and possessions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not exit this city.' He walks out of the city gate alone to the neighboring village, leaving his family and belongings in the city. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he left the city.
B) No, because exit from a city requires moving his family and belongings.
C) No, because exit from a city is only with the body, and he did that.
D) Yes, but only if he intended to relocate permanently.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'exit' has two distinct meanings: for an inhabited house it means full relocation (naqla), for a city it means bodily departure; the mosque example in the source does not restrict this general rule.

Distinction Between Exiting, Going, and Coming in Oaths to Mecca
Differentiates verbs and their legal effect on oath-breaking with travel to Mecca

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of the verbs 'exit', 'go', and 'come' in oaths concerning travel to Mecca, and apply the condition of passing the built-up area to determine oath-breaking.

Core Idea
In oaths to Mecca, 'exit' and 'go' break upon departure; 'come' breaks upon arrival; 'exit' only breaks after leaving the city limits.

Oaths involving travel require careful linguistic analysis because the choice of verb determines when the oath is broken. The Hanafi school differentiates between exiting, going, and coming, and also conditions the breaking of an oath to exit on passing the built-up area of one's city. This lesson unpacks the distinctions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three verbs and their default meanings
In oaths, 'exit' (خروج) means separating from interior to exterior, 'go' (ذهب) implies moving away, and 'come' (إتيان) means arrival. Recognize that each verb triggers a different moment of oath-breaking.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule for 'exit' and 'go'
For 'exit' and the relied-upon view of 'go', the oath is broken upon departure (separation from the starting point) with the intention to reach the destination. For 'come', the oath breaks only upon actual arrival.
The Hanafi position on 'go' is that it is like 'exit' because it signifies removal (زوال).
3
Note the dispute over 'go' and its tarjih
Some scholars equated 'go' with 'come' (arrival). The Hanafi school declared the departure view as the most correct (الأصح) because 'go' indicates moving away, not reaching.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
4
Understand the implicit condition of travel for 'exit' to a distant city
When the oath involves exiting to a distant city (e.g., Mecca, Baghdad), the word 'exit' implicitly means 'travel'. A traveler is only considered to have exited after passing the built-up area (عمران المصر) of his own city.
This is based on the concept that a person is not a traveler until leaving inhabited areas.
5
Differentiate between travel and local outings
For local destinations (e.g., a funeral at a nearby mosque), the condition of passing the built-up area does not apply; leaving the house suffices to break the oath. The passage illustrates this via the example of exiting to a funeral vs. exiting to Baghdad.
The key is whether the destination requires travel (safar) or is local.

قوله : (لا يخرج أو لا يذهب إلى مكة فخرج يريدها ثم رجع يحنث وفى لا يأتيها (لا أي لا يحنث والفرق بين الخروج والإتيان أن الخروج على قصد مكة قد وجد وهو الشرط إذا الخروج هو الانفصال من الداخل إلى الخارج، وأما الإتيان فعبارة عن الوصول قال الله تعالى فأتيا فرعون فقولا [الشعراء: ١٦] واختلف في الذهاب فقيل هو كالاتيان، وقيل كالخروج وهو الأصح لأنه عبارة عن الزوال. أطلق في الحنث بالخروج وهو مقيد بما إذا جاوز عمران مصره على قصدها، فلو خرج قاصداً مكة ولم يجاوز عمرانه لا يحنث كما في الظهيرية ،وغيرها وكأنه ضمن لفظ أخرج معنى أسافر للعلم بأن المضي إليها سفر لكن على هذا لو لم يكن بينها وبينه مدة سفر ينبغي أن يحنث بمجرد انفصاله من الداخل كما في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يخرج إلى بغداد اليوم فخرج من باب داره يريد بغداد ثم بداله فرجع لا يحنث ما لم يجاوز عمران مصره بهذه النية بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يخرج إلى جنازة فلان والمسألة بحالها يحنث والفرق أن الخروج إلى بغداد سفر والمرء لا يعد مسافراً ما لم يجاوز عمران ،مصره ولا كذلك في الخروج إلى الجنازة. ولو كان في منزل من داره في المسألة الثانية فخرج إلى صحن الدار ثم رجع لا يحنث ما لم يخرج من باب الدار لأنه لا يعد خارجاً في جنازة فلان ما دام في داره كما لا يعد خارجاً إلى بغداد ما دام في مصره فاستوت المسألتان معنى ا هـ .
وفي البدائع قال عمر بن أسد سألت محمداً عن رجل حلف ليخرجن من الرقة ما الخروج ؟ قال : إذا جعل البيوت خلف ظهره لأن من حصل في هذه المواضع جاز له القصر فالحاصل أن الخروج إن كان من البلد فلا يحنث حتى يجاوز عمران مصره، سواء كان إلى مقصده مدة سفر أو لا، وإن لم يكن خروجاً من البلد فلا يشترط مجاوزة العمران. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف أن لا يخرج إلى مكة ماشياً فخرج من أبيات المصر ماشياً يريد به مكة
اهـ.
الإمام محمد أولى لظهور الدليل على أن المراد ذلك والله تعالى أعلم قوله: (والخروج من البلدان والقرى أن يخرج الحالف ببدنه خاصة قال في الذخيرة بعده زاد في المنتقى : إذا خرج ببدنه فقد أراد سفر أو لم يرد اهـ. وفي حاشية الرملي : فائدة الارتحال والانتقال بعامة المتاع بحيث يقال فلان

His statement: (He does not exit or does not go to Mecca, so he exited intending it then returned, he breaks the oath; and in [the oath] 'he does not come to it,' he does not break the oath). The difference between exit and coming is that exit with the intention of Mecca has occurred, and that is the condition, because exit is the separation from the interior to the exterior. As for coming, it is an expression of arrival. Allah said: 'So go to Pharaoh and say' [26:16]. And there is disagreement about 'going': it is said it is like coming, and it is said it is like exiting, and this is the most correct because it is an expression of removal. He generalized in breaking the oath by exit, but it is restricted by the condition that he passes the built-up area of his city with the intention. So if he exited intending Mecca but did not pass the built-up area, he does not break the oath, as in al-Zahiriyya and others. It is as if he implied in the word 'exit' the meaning of 'travel' because it is known that going to it is a journey. But on this, if there is no travel distance between him and it, it would be appropriate that he breaks the oath by mere separation from the interior, as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Muhit: He swore not to exit to Baghdad today, so he exited from his house door intending Baghdad, then he changed his mind and returned; he does not break the oath until he passes the built-up area of his city with this intention. Unlike if he swore not to exit to the funeral of so-and-so and the situation is the same; he breaks the oath. The difference is that exit to Baghdad is travel, and a person is not considered a traveler until he passes the built-up area of his city, and not so in exit to a funeral. And if he was in a room of his house in the second issue and exited to the courtyard then returned, he does not break the oath until he exits the door of the house, because he is not considered exiting for the funeral of so-and-so as long as he is in his house, just as he is not considered exiting to Baghdad as long as he is in his city. So the two issues are equal in meaning. End. In al-Bada'i': 'Umar ibn Asad said: I asked Muhammad about a man who swore to exit from al-Raqqah: What is exit? He said: When he puts the houses behind his back, because whoever is in these places is allowed to shorten prayer. So the conclusion is that if the exit is from the city, he does not break the oath until he passes the built-up area of his city, whether his destination is a travel distance or not. And if it is not an exit from the city, then passing the built-up area is not required. And the author indicated that if he swore not to exit to Mecca walking, and he exited from the houses of the city walking intending Mecca, then end. Imam Muhammad is more appropriate because evidence shows that what is intended is that. And Allah knows best. His statement: (And exit from cities and villages is that the oath-taker goes out with his body alone). In al-Dhakhira after it: He added in al-Muntaqa: If he goes out with his body, whether he intended travel or not. In Hashiyat al-Ramli: The benefit of departure and moving with the majority of possessions such that it is said 'so-and-so departed' or 'so-and-so moved' — refer to what we wrote in the margin of al-TatarKhaniyya, which is frequently occurring. And what he wrote in the margin of al-TatarKhaniyya, he strengthened it until it is said 'so-and-so has moved' etc. Evidence that moving cannot be except with the majority of his possessions.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage compares three verbs used in oaths related to travelling to Mecca: 'exit' (خروج), 'go' (ذهب), and 'come' (إتيان). The key ruling: if someone swears 'I will not exit to Mecca' and then leaves with that intention, he breaks the oath only upon departing (separation). If he swears 'I will not come to Mecca', he breaks the oath only upon arrival. The verb 'ذهب' (go) is debated: some equate it with 'come' (arrival), but the correct Hanafi position (al-asahh) is that it is like 'exit' (departure) because it signifies moving away. The passage then introduces a crucial condition: for oaths involving travel to a distant city like Mecca or Baghdad, the oath is not broken until the person passes the built-up area (عمران المصر) of his own city. This is because the word 'exit' in such contexts implicitly means 'travel', and a traveler is only considered one after leaving the inhabited area. For local exits like to a funeral, passing the city limits is not required; exiting the house door suffices. The discussion from al-Bada'i' and the question of 'Umar ibn Asad to Imam Muhammad confirms that exit from a city is defined by putting the houses behind one's back, analogous to the distance for shortening prayer. The passage ends by reaffirming that exit from a city is with the body alone, while moving (naqla) from a house requires taking the majority of one's possessions.

Key Terms
الإتيان (al-ityan): Coming to a place; actual arrival.
الذهاب (al-dhahab): Going; moving away from the current location.
عمران المصر (umran al-misr): The built-up area of a city; the inhabited part including suburbs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will not go to Mecca.' He packs his bags, leaves his house, and drives to the airport within the same city. His flight is cancelled and he returns home. Did he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he intended to go and started travelling.
B) No, because 'go' means arrive and he did not reach Mecca.
C) Yes, because 'go' is like exit and he departed from his house.
D) No, because he did not pass the built-up area of his city.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between exit/going (departure) and coming (arrival) in oaths, and conditions the breach of an oath to exit a distant city on leaving the built-up area.

The Core Distinction: Oaths of Going Out vs. Coming
Distinguishes legal criteria for breaking oaths based on the verb used.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when an oath about going out (khuruj) is broken and when an oath about coming (ityān) is broken, and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
For oaths of going out, intention at departure is decisive; for oaths of coming, reaching the destination suffices.

In Hanafi fiqh, the legal consequences of an oath (birr or hanth) depend on the nature of the action sworn about. This lesson examines the key distinction between khuruj (going out) and ityān (coming). The classical Hanafi works—from al-Zahiriyya to al-Muhit and al-Dhakhira—consistently apply this principle: intention matters when the action is ambiguous, but not when it is unambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the Two Actions
Identify that the passage distinguishes between khuruj (going out, leaving) and ityān (coming, arriving). Note that khuruj can have multiple possible destinations, while ityān has a clear target.
Also includes dhahab (going) as synonymous with khuruj.
2
Analyze the Legal Consequence
For khuruj, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended at the moment of departure to go to the named place. For ityān, the oath is broken upon reaching the destination, regardless of intention at the start.
The text says: 'في الخروج والذهاب تشترط النية عند الانفصال للحنث وفي الإتيان لا يشترط بل إذا وصل إليها يحنث، ونوى أو لم ينو'.
3
Examine the Rationale (Talil)
The reason is that khuruj is ambiguous (can be to many places), so intention specifies which departure triggers the oath. In contrast, ityān is unambiguous—reaching the place is the clear event.
The passage states: 'لأن الخروج متنوع يحتمل الخروج إليها وإلى غيرها'.
4
Apply to Examples
Work through the examples: going to Mecca (requires intention to pass through Kufa), leaving Baghdad (with funeral – breaks oath), wife's outing (intention not needed if cause is moving). See how the principle determines hanth.
The multiple examples from al-Muhit, al-Dhakhira, al-Muntqa all reinforce the same distinction.
5
Note the Exception for Visiting
For visiting the sick (iyadah) and ziyarah, reaching the door is considered arrival; the principle of ityān applies. Similarly, departure (rihlah) requires actual leaving of the town.
The author adds: 'قيّد بالإتيان لأن العبادة والزيارة لا يشترط فيهما الوصول' meaning the act of visiting is fulfilled by coming to the door.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج وفي القنية : إلى حيث كنا ثم ركب حنث، ولو خرج راكباً ثم نزل فمشى لا يحنث كذا في الظهيرية. وفيها أيضاً: رجل قال والله لأخرجن مع فلان العام إلى مكة إذا خرج مع فلان حتى جاوز البيوت وصار بحيث يباح له قصر الصلاة برّ في يمينه، وإن بداله أن يرجع رجع من غير ضرر، ولو حلف أن لا يخرج من بغداد فخرج مع جنازة والمقابر خارجة من بغداد يحنث، ولو قال لامرأته إن خرجت من ها هنا اليوم فإن رجعت إلى سنة فأنت طالق ثلاثاً فخرجت اليوم إلى الصلاة أو غيرها ثم رجعت فإن كان سبب اليمين خروج الانتقال أو السفر لا تطلق ا هـ . انتقل الزوجان من الرستاق إلى قرية فلحقه رب الديون فقال لها اخرجي معي. فيه فأبت إلى الجمعة فقال إن لم تخرجي معي فكذا فإن كان قد تأهب للخروج فهو على الفور وإلا فلا. وإن خرجت معه في الحال إلى درب القرية ثم رجعت برّ في يمينه، وإن أراد زوجها الخروج أصلاً ا هـ. وفي المحيط : ولو حلف لا يخرج من الري إلى الكوفة فخرج من الري يريد مكة وجعل طريقه إلى الكوفة ينظر ؛ إن كان حيث خرج نوى أن يمر بالكوفة حنث، وإن نوى أن لا يمر بالكوفة ثم بداله بعد ما خرج فصار إلى موضع آخر تقصر فيه الصلاة فقصد أن يمر بالكوفة لا يحنث ا هـ . ثم في الخروج والذهاب تشترط النية عند الانفصال للحنث كما قدمناه وفي الإتيان لا يشترط بل إذا وصل إليها يحنث، ونوى أو لم ينو، لأن الخروج متنوع يحتمل الخروج إليها وإلى غيرها، وكذا الذهاب فلا بد من النية عند ذلك كالخروج إلى الجنازة بخلاف الإتيان لأن الوصول غير متنوع وفي المحيط : ليأتينه فأتاه فلم يأذن له لا يحنث وفي الذخيرة : إذا حلف الرجل أن لا تأتي امرأته عرس فلان فذهبت قبل العرس وكانت ثمة حتى مضى العرس لا يحنث ؛ هكذا ذكر في المنتقى وعلله فقال : لأنها ما أتت العرس بل العرس أتاها ولو حلف لا يأتي فلاناً فهو على أن يأتي منزله أو حانوته لقيه أو لم يلقه، وإن أتى مسجده لم يحنث ؛ رواه ابراهيم عن محمد. وفي المنتقى : رجل لزم رجلاً وحلف الملتزم ليأتينه غداً فأتاه في الموضع الذي لزمه فيه لا يبرّ حتى يأتي منزله، فإن كان لزمه في منزله فحلف ليأتينه غداً فتحول الطالب من منزله فأتى الحالف المنزل الذي كان فيه الطالب فلم يجده لا يبر حتى يأتي المنزل الذي تحول إليه. ولو قال إن لم آتك في موضع كذا فعبدي حر فأتاه فلم يجده فقد برّ إنما هذا على إتيان ذلك الذخيرة إذا حلف ليعودن فلاناً أو ليزورنه فأتى بابه فلم يؤذن له وأقول : والرحلة كذلك. قال في القاموس : ارتحل القوم من المكان انتقلوا. وبه يعلم الجواب عما يقع كثيراً أن الرجل يحلف على الرحيل من بلده فاستفد ذلك اهـ.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths Concerning Entering and Exiting. In al-Qunya: 'to where we were then he rode and broke the oath; if he went out riding then dismounted and walked, he does not break the oath' as in al-Zahiriyya. Also in it: A man said, 'By Allah, I will surely go out with so-and-so this year to Mecca.' If he went out with him until he passed the houses and reached a place where shortening prayer is permitted, his oath is fulfilled. If he changes his mind and returns, he returns without harm. If he swore not to go out from Baghdad and he went out with a funeral, and the graves are outside Baghdad, he breaks the oath. If he said to his wife, 'If you go out from here today, then if you return within a year, you are divorced three times,' and she went out today for prayer or otherwise and then returned, if the cause of the oath was a move or travel, she is not divorced. The spouses moved from a village to another, and a debt claimant pursued him, so he said to her, 'Go out with me.' She refused until Friday, so he said, 'If you do not go out with me, then such and such.' If he had prepared to go out, it is immediate; otherwise not. If she went out with him immediately to the village lane and then returned, his oath is fulfilled. If her husband intended to go out altogether, end quote. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to go out from al-Ray to Kufa, then he went out from al-Ray intending Mecca and made his route through Kufa, it is considered: if when he went out he intended to pass through Kufa, he breaks the oath; if he intended not to pass through Kufa, then changed his mind after going out and reached a place where prayer is shortened, then intended to pass through Kufa, he does not break the oath. Then in going out and going, intention at the time of departure is required for breaking the oath, as we mentioned; but in coming, intention is not required; rather, when he reaches it he breaks the oath, whether he intended or not, because going out has multiple possibilities: it could be to that place or to another. Similarly, going requires intention at that time, like going to a funeral, unlike coming, because arrival is not multiple. In al-Muhit: 'He will come to him, and he came but he did not permit him, he does not break the oath.' In al-Dhakhira: If a man swore that his wife would not come to so-and-so's wedding, and she went before the wedding and remained there until the wedding passed, he does not break the oath. Thus it is mentioned in al-Muntqa, and he explained: because she did not come to the wedding, rather the wedding came to her. If he swore not to come to so-and-so, it means to come to his house or his shop, whether he meets him or not; if he comes to his mosque, he does not break the oath. It was narrated by Ibrahim from Muhammad. In al-Muntqa: A man adhered to a man, and the one who adhered swore that he would come to him tomorrow. He came to him at the place where he adhered to him; he does not fulfill until he comes to his house. If he adhered to him at his house and swore to come to him tomorrow, then the seeker moved from his house, and the swearer came to the house where the seeker had been and did not find him, he does not fulfill until he comes to the house to which he moved. If he said, 'If I do not come to you at such a place, my slave is free,' and he came and did not find him, he has fulfilled, because this is about coming to that place. This is unlike if he said, 'If I do not pay you tomorrow at such a place,' and the swearer came to that place and did not find him, where he breaks the oath, because this is about the two of them meeting. End quote. He restricted with 'coming' because visiting the sick and visiting (ziyarah) do not require reaching [the person]. For this reason, al-Dhakhira said: If he swore to visit someone sick or to visit him, and he came to his door and was not given permission... And I say: Departure is the same. Al-Qamus said: 'The people departed from the place, they moved.' With this, the answer is known for what often occurs: a man swears to depart from his town. So benefit from that.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof section from al-Bahr al-Raiq, commenting on a matn in Kanz al-Daqaiq about oaths concerning entering and exiting. The author presents numerous cases from earlier Hanafi sources (al-Qunya, al-Zahiriyya, al-Muhit, al-Dhakhira, al-Muntqa) to illustrate the key distinction: In oaths involving going out (khuruj) or going (dhahab), the oath is broken only if the swearer had the specific intention at the time of departure to go to the named place, because going out is ambiguous—one could go anywhere. In contrast, in oaths involving coming (ityan), the oath is broken as soon as the swearer reaches the destination, regardless of intention, because arrival is unambiguous. The text then applies this to visiting the sick (iyadah) and visiting (ziyarah), noting that these acts do not require reaching the person himself; reaching the door suffices. The author adds that the same applies to departure (rihlah) from a town: if one swears to depart, he must physically leave the town. The multiple examples serve to clarify the operative principle that the mode of action determines the conditions for breaking an oath.

Key Terms
خروج (khuruj): Going out, leaving a place; in oaths, requires intention at departure to break the oath.
إتيان (ityan): Coming to a place; in oaths, breaking occurs upon arrival regardless of intention.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath, incurring legal consequences (expiation or divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the key difference between an oath about going out (khuruj) and an oath about coming (ityan)?
A) Both require intention at the moment of action.
B) Going out requires intention at departure; coming requires reaching the destination regardless of intention.
C) Coming requires intention; going out does not.
D) Neither requires intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi school, the decisive factor for breaking an oath of movement depends on whether the action is ambiguous (khuruj – needs intention) or unambiguous (ityan – reaching suffices).
