Oath to Visit: Reaching Door vs Reaching Person
Refines ruling on oaths to visit: door suffices, but permission needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fulfilling an oath to visit the sick by reaching the door, and apply the qiyas principle to distinguish between prevention and failure to seek permission.

Core Idea
In oaths to visit the sick, reaching the door fulfills the objective, but failing to ask permission breaks the oath.

We previously learned that swearing to visit a sick person requires actually going to the person. Here, the Hanafi school refines that: reaching the door is sufficient for the oath to be fulfilled, provided you seek permission to enter. The passage then draws an analogy from another case involving prevention from leaving a house to justify that failure to seek permission breaks the oath. This refinement clarifies the delicate balance between literal fulfillment and legal objective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) the swearer returns without reaching the sick person, and (2) he comes to the door but does not ask permission. Note the different rulings for each.
The first scenario does not break the oath; the second does.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The oath to visit (iyadah) is considered fulfilled when the swearer reaches the door because the essence of 'visiting' is coming to the place of the sick person. Seeking permission is an additional obligation to complete the visit.
Reaching the door satisfies the movement component; permission is needed to actualize the visit.
3
Analyze the qiyas from al-Muhit
The text quotes an analogy: if someone swears 'I will not go out of this house today' and is prevented (e.g., tied up), he breaks the oath because the prevention does not excuse him. Similarly, in the visiting case, failing to ask permission is a minor obstacle that does not excuse the oath.
The illa (effective cause) is that the swearer had the ability to fulfill the oath but did not complete a necessary step.
4
Recognize the mufta bihi position
The author states that this ruling—breaking the oath in both returning without reaching (if he could have reached) and coming without permission—is the chosen (mukhtar) position of later Hanafi scholars.
This is the authoritative fatwa position based on the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض فرجع ولم يصل إليه لا يحنث وإن أتى بابه ولم يستأذن حنث قال في المحيط : وعلى قياس من قال إن لم أخرج من هذا المنزل اليوم فمنع أو قيد حنث فيجب أن يحنث هنا في الوجهين وهو المختار لمشايخنا ا.هـ.
Some returned and did not reach him, he does not break the oath; if he came to his door and did not ask permission, he breaks the oath. Al-Muhit said: By analogy with the one who said 'If I do not go out of this house today' and he was prevented or tied up, he breaks the oath, so he must break the oath here in both cases, and this is the chosen position of our scholars.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from al-Bahr al-Raiq, commenting on a matn regarding oaths to visit the sick. It clarifies a nuance: if someone swears to visit a sick person, he fulfills his oath by reaching the door, even if he does not actually see the person. However, if he reaches the door but does not ask permission to enter, he breaks the oath (because he had the opportunity to fulfill but failed to complete the visit). The text then draws an analogy (qiyas) from a similar case in al-Muhit: if someone swears 'If I do not go out of this house today' and is prevented by an obstacle or tied up, does he break the oath? The ruling is that he does break it because the prevention does not excuse him. By analogy, in the visiting case, both coming to the door without permission and failing to go out due to prevention result in breaking the oath. The author notes that this is the chosen (mufta bihi) position of Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
عيادة (iyadah): Visiting the sick; in oaths, reaching the door suffices, but seeking permission is required.
فرجع (faraja‘a): To return; here, returning without reaching the person means the oath is not broken.
مختار (mukhtar): Chosen, preferred position of the school, relied upon for fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an oath to visit a sick person, when does the swearer break his oath according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) If he reaches the door but does not ask permission.
B) If he returns without reaching the person, but he intended to visit.
C) Only if he enters the house and sees the person.
D) If he reaches the door and asks permission but is refused entry.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath to visit the sick is broken if the swearer reaches the door but fails to ask permission, by analogy with the case of prevention from leaving a house.

Fulfilling Oaths: Sending, Bringing, and Accompanying
Three rulings on oath fulfillment in sending and accompanying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an oath is broken when goods are lost after sending, and distinguish between cases based on the oath-taker's intention.

Core Idea
Sending fulfills the oath even if goods are lost; intention determines when personal delivery is required.

In Hanafi fiqh, the fulfillment of an oath often hinges on the precise wording and the underlying intention. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines three scenarios involving oaths to send money, bring goods, or accompany companions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of yamin (oaths). The key concept is that the act of sending (irsal) is fulfilled by dispatching, regardless of receipt, and that the oath-taker's intention can qualify what action is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and the Act Sworn To
For each of the three scenarios, determine what the oath-taker swore to do: send maintenance, bring goods, or go with companions. Note the exact wording.
The wording determines the expectations.
2
Determine the Outcome (Hanth or Not)
Based on the text, decide whether the oath is broken. In the first scenario, it is not broken despite loss. In the second, it depends on intention. In the third, prevention by authorities excuses.
Loss of goods does not invalidate the act of sending.
3
Identify the Key Legal Concept
The concept of irsal (sending) is fulfilled by the act of dispatching, not by receipt. Also, the role of niyyah (intention) can qualify the required action.
Al-ummur bi-maqasidiha also applies here.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
In the third scenario, Abu Ja'far rules no divorce due to prevention, but al-Sadr al-Shahid chose a different view. Understand that there is ikhtilaf within the school.
The dispute centers on whether external prevention excuses an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال إن لم أرسل إليك نفقتك هذا الشهر فأنت طالق فأرسل بها على يد إنسان وضاعت من يد الرسول لا يحنث لأنه قد أرسل، وكذا إذا قال إن لم أبعث إليك نفقة هذا الشهر. ولو قال إن لم تجيئيني غداً بمتاع كذا فأنت طالق فبعثت به مع إنسان قال : إن كان مراده وصول عين المتاع إليه لا يحنث وإن كان غرضه أن تحمل بنفسها يحنث . ولو قال الرجل لأصحابه إن لم أذهب بكم الليلة إلى منزلي فامرأته طالق فذهب بهم الطريق فأخذهم العسس فحبسهم لا تطلق امرأته ؛ هكذا حكي عن الفقيه أبي جعفر. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : هذا الجواب يوافق قولهما في مسألة الكوز وقد مر في أول النوع اختيار الصدر الشهيد في جنس هذه المسائل بخلاف هذا ا هـ . ما في الذخيرة،
If he said, 'If I do not send you your maintenance this month, then you are divorced,' and he sent it via a person and it was lost from the messenger's hand, he does not break the oath because he has sent. Similarly, if he said, 'If I do not dispatch to you maintenance this month.' If he said, 'If you do not bring me such-and-such goods tomorrow, you are divorced,' and she sent them with a man: if his intention was that the actual goods should reach him, he does not break the oath; if his intention was that she should carry them herself, he breaks the oath. If a man said to his companions, 'If I do not take you tonight to my house, then my wife is divorced,' and he took them on the road, but the watchmen seized them and imprisoned them, his wife is not divorced. So it is narrated from al-Faqih Abi Ja‘far. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This answer agrees with their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) position in the issue of the jug, and it has passed in the beginning of the chapter that the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid in such matters is contrary to this.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from al-Dhakhira on specific oath situations. The first ruling: a man swears to send (arsala) maintenance to his wife within the month; he hands it to a messenger who loses it. The oath is not broken because the act of sending (irsal) has been accomplished, even if the goods do not arrive. The second ruling distinguishes between two intentions: if the husband swears that his wife should bring goods, and she sends them with a messenger, if his intention was merely that the goods reach him, the oath is fulfilled; if he intended that she carry them herself, the oath is broken. The third ruling involves an oath to take companions to his house; they are detained by authorities on the way. According to Abu Ja‘far, the wife is not divorced because the prevention is beyond his control. Abu al-Layth notes that this aligns with the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the similar case of the jug (where being prevented excuses), but al-Sadr al-Shahid chose a different view. The passage ends with 'that is what is in al-Dhakhira'.

Key Terms
إرسال (irsal): Sending; the act of dispatching, not necessarily the arrival.
بعث (ba‘th): Dispatch, similar to irsāl; used interchangeably here.
عسس (asas): Watchmen, police; external authority that prevents movement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a wife is sworn to bring goods and she sends them with a messenger, if the husband's intention was that she bring them herself, the ruling is:
A) He does not break the oath because the goods arrived.
B) He breaks the oath because she did not bring them herself.
C) The oath is invalid because 'bringing' is ambiguous.
D) It depends on whether the goods were lost.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers the act of sending (irsal) sufficient to fulfill an oath of sending, regardless of receipt; intention determines when personal delivery is required.

Oaths of Going in Evening and Despair of Fulfillment
Defines 'rawah' as going and explains the despair principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the meaning of rawah in oaths and apply the principle that absolute future oaths are broken only upon despair.

Core Idea
Rawah means any going, not just evening; an oath to act in the future is broken only when fulfillment becomes impossible.

Oaths often use terms whose precise meanings determine when they are broken. This passage clarifies that 'rawah' (evening going) simply means 'going' in classical Arabic, so an oath to refrain from rawah is broken by any departure with that intent. Furthermore, for oaths to perform an action in the future without a specified time, the oath remains intact until despair of fulfillment sets in—such as the death of one party. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi commitment to linguistic precision and the principle of avoiding premature conclusion of oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Define rawah linguistically
Identify the meaning of 'rawah' as clarified by al-Azhari and confirmed by al-Nawawi: it is synonymous with 'going' (dhahab) at any time of night, not specifically the evening.
This linguistic ruling is crucial because it determines the scope of the oath.
2
Apply the definition to an oath
Consider an oath like 'I will not go (yaruh) to his house.' According to the definition, the oath is broken as soon as the person leaves with the intention to go, regardless of whether he reaches the house.
This is because 'rawah' equals 'dhahab' (going out), and going out with intent triggers the breaking.
3
Understand the despair principle
For oaths to perform an action in the future without a time limit (e.g., 'I will beat Zayd'), the oath is not broken immediately if the action is not done. It remains valid until fulfillment becomes impossible—this is called 'ya's' (despair).
The classic example is the death of one party; then the oath is broken at the moment of death.
4
Identify examples of despair
Text gives examples: swearing to beat someone, give something, or divorce; despair occurs when the person dies or the action becomes impossible. The oath is broken at that final moment.
This shows the Hanafi principle that an absolute future oath continues in effect until impossibility arises.
5
Note the refinements
The passage also touches on visiting (iyadah) requiring reaching the door but not the person, and the effect of coercion on positive vs. negative conditions. Later scholars like al-Sharnblali suggest that the ruling on rawah should be based on custom.
These details show that even after establishing the rule, the school considers contextual variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم أر من صرح بلفظ الرواح من أئمتنا وهو كثير الوقوع في كلام المصريين وفي أيمانهم لكن قال الأزهري : لغة العرب أن الرواح الذهاب، سواء كان أول الليل أو آخره أو في الليل. قال النووي في شرح مسلم من كتاب الجمعة بعد نقله : وهذا هو الصواب ا هـ . فعلى هذا إذا حلف لا يروح إلى كذا فهو بمعنى لا يذهب وهو بمعنى الخروج يحنث بالخروج عن قصده صل أو لا. قوله : (ليأتينه فلم يأته حتى مات حنث في آخر حياته لأن البرّ قبل ذلك موجود ولا خصوصية للإتيان بل كل فعل حلف أنه يفعله في المستقبل وأطلقه ولم يقيده بوقت لم يحنث حتى يقع الإياس عن البر مثل ليضربن زيداً أو ليعطين فلانة أو ليطلقن زوجته وتحقق اليأس عن البر يكون بفوت أحدهما فلذا قال في غاية البيان وأصل هذا أن الحالف في اليمين يروح قوله : (لأن العيادة والزيارة لا يشترط فيهما الوصول فيه نظر لأن الوصول المنفي في عبارة الذخيرة التي استشهد بها هو الوصول إلى الشخص المعاد والمزور، أما الوصول إلى باب داره فهو شرط، وكذا في الإتيان فقد قال في الذخيرة أيضاً : لو حلف لا يأتي فلاناً فهو على أن يأتي منزله أو حانوته لقيه أو لم يلقه، وإن أتى مسجده لم يحنث رواه ابراهيم عن محمد رحمه الله تعالى اهـ. فقد اشترك الإتيان والعيادة والزيارة في اشتراط الوصول إلى المنزل دون الوصول إلى صاحبه بل زادت العيادة والزيارة اشتراط الاستئذان قوله : (وعلى قياس من قال الخ) قد يقال هذا قياس مع لأن الشرط في «إن لم أخرج منفي وفي ليعودن فلاناً مثبت والإكراه يؤثر في المثبت لا في المنفي تأمل. قوله : (ولو قال الرجل لاصحابه إن لم أذهب بكم الخ) قال الرملي؛ هذا يتأتى على القول بأن الذهاب كالإتيان لا على أنه كالخروج وقد تقدم أنه الأصح تأمل قوله : (فعلى هذا إذا حلف لا يروح إلى كذا الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية : الدليل خاص بالذهاب ليلاً والمدعي أعم فينبغي أن يبني على العرف اهـ. قلت: وفي المصباح ما هو أوضح مما نقله المؤلف حيث قال فيه : وقد يتوهم بعض كما مر
I have not seen any of our imams explicitly mention the word 'rawah' (evening going), though it frequently occurs in the speech of Egyptians and in their oaths. But al-Azhari said: In the Arabic language, 'rawah' means going, whether at the beginning of the night, the end, or during the night. Al-Nawawi in his commentary on Sahih Muslim, Book of Friday, after quoting him, said: This is correct. Based on this, if he swears 'I will not go in the evening to such-and-such', it means 'I will not go', and it is like going out; he breaks the oath by going out with the intention to that place, whether he arrives or not. His statement (the matn): 'He will come to him, and he did not come until he died; he breaks the oath at the end of his life because fulfillment (birr) existed earlier; and there is no specificity to 'coming' (ityan); rather, every action that one swears to do in the future, if he gives it absolutely and does not restrict it to a time, he does not break the oath until despair of fulfillment sets in. For example, 'I will surely beat Zayd', 'I will surely give so-and-so', or 'I will surely divorce his wife'. Despair of fulfillment occurs by the death of one of them. Therefore, al-Ghayah al-Bayan said: The origin of this is that the swearer in the oath... His statement: 'Because visiting the sick and visiting do not require reaching' is debatable, because the reaching negated in the wording of al-Dhakhira is reaching the person being visited or visited; as for reaching the door of his house, it is a condition. Similarly, in 'coming' (ityan), al-Dhakhira also said: If he swore not to come to so-and-so, it means to come to his house or his shop, whether he meets him or not; if he comes to his mosque, he does not break the oath. Narrated by Ibrahim from Muhammad. So 'coming' (ityan), 'visiting the sick' (iyadah), and 'visiting' (ziyarah) all share the condition of reaching the house, but not reaching the occupant; indeed, visiting the sick and visiting additionally require seeking permission. His statement: 'And by analogy with the one who said...' It may be said that this is an analogy with a difference because the condition in 'If I do not go out' is negative, while in 'I will surely visit so-and-so' it is positive, and coercion affects the positive, not the negative. Contemplate. His statement: 'If a man said to his companions...' Al-Ramli said: This is consistent with the view that going (dhahab) is like coming (ityan), not like going out (khuruj); and it has preceded that the more correct view is... Contemplate. His statement: 'Based on this, if he swears not to go in the evening...' Al-Sharanblali said: The evidence is specific to going at night, but the general claim is broader, so it should be based on custom. I say: In al-Misbah, there is clearer evidence than what the author quoted, where he said: 'And some may imagine, as has passed.'
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a composite commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq that addresses several points. First, it clarifies the meaning of 'rawah' (evening going) in oaths, citing al-Azhari and al-Nawawi to establish that it simply means 'going' (dhahab), regardless of the time. Therefore, if one swears not to go in the evening to a place, he breaks the oath by leaving with that intention, even if he does not arrive. Second, it comments on a matn statement: 'He will come to him, and he did not come until he died; he breaks the oath at the end of his life'. The commentator explains the general principle: in oaths to perform a future action without a time limit, the oath is not broken until there is despair (ya's) of fulfillment, e.g., through the death of one party. Third, it revisits the issue of visiting (iyadah/ziyarah), refining the earlier statement by clarifying that reaching the door is a condition, not just reaching the person. Fourth, it discusses an analogy about coercion in negative vs. positive conditions. Fifth, it notes al-Ramli's view that the case of taking companions aligns with considering 'going' (dhahab) as 'coming' (ityan) rather than 'going out' (khuruj). Finally, al-Sharnblali suggests that the ruling on 'rawah' should be based on custom (urf). The passage ends with a reference to al-Misbah.

Key Terms
رواح (rawah): Going in the evening; according to al-Azhari, it simply means 'going' (dhahab) at any time of night.
يأس (ya's): Despair; in oath context, the point at which fulfillment becomes impossible, e.g., death of the person involved.
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion; debated in oaths: affects positive conditions (e.g., to do something) but not negative conditions (e.g., not to do).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is an oath to do an action in the future (without a time limit) broken?
A) Immediately if the action is not done.
B) Only when despair of fulfillment occurs, such as the death of the person involved.
C) When the swearer changes his intention.
D) Only if a specific time passes without action.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'rawah' as any night-time going and holds that absolute future oaths are only broken upon impossibility, ensuring oaths are not prematurely voided.

Rulings on Oaths of Movement and Sending
Rulings on various oaths related to movement and sending

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings on oaths of entering, exiting, and sending goods by determining whether the literal action constitutes fulfillment and when prevention or intention alters the ruling.

Core Idea
An oath is fulfilled by the literal act sworn; prevention or intention may or may not cause breach depending on context.

This lesson from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines several distinct scenarios involving oaths about movement and sending items. The key principle is that the fulfillment of an oath depends on the literal performance of the sworn action, but exceptions arise when external factors prevent the action or when the intent of the oath-holder differs from the literal wording. We will walk through the cases of turning back before reaching a destination, sending maintenance via a messenger, requiring a person to bring an item, and being prevented by authorities. Understanding these rulings helps clarify when an oath is considered broken (hanth) and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sworn action
Read the oath and determine the exact verb and object sworn upon. For example, 'If I do not send you your maintenance' or 'If I do not take you to my house.'
The wording must be taken literally unless there is a clear intention to the contrary.
2
Assess literal fulfillment
Determine whether the literal action was performed. If the person did the act (e.g., sent the money, left the house), the oath is fulfilled unless prevention or qualifying intention intervenes.
In Hanafi fiqh, fulfillment is based on the actual occurrence of the sworn action, not the outcome.
3
Consider prevention and intention
If the person was prevented from completing the action (e.g., by imprisonment or loss of goods), or if the oath-holder's intention was more specific than the literal wording, the ruling may change. The text cites analogies and disputes for such cases.
Prevention that is beyond the oath-holder's control may or may not constitute a breach, as seen in the dispute about the chained person.
4
Apply the Hanafi ruling
For each scenario, state the ruling: does the oath break (hanth) or not? The passage provides the answers: e.g., sending maintenance via messenger is sufficient; bringing an item by messenger suffices if only the item's arrival was intended; prevention by authorities does not break the oath in the view cited.
Note the intra-Hanafi difference in the prevention case, where al-Muhit and al-Sadr al-Shahid have differing opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج بعض فرجع ولم يصل إليه لا يحنث وإن أتى بابه ولم يستأذن حنث قال في المحيط : وعلى قياس من قال إن لم أخرج من هذا المنزل اليوم فمنع أو قيد حنث فيجب أن يحنث هنا في الوجهين وهو المختار لمشايخنا ا.هـ. ولو قال إن لم أرسل إليك نفقتك هذا الشهر فأنت طالق فأرسل بها على يد إنسان وضاعت من يد الرسول لا يحنث لأنه قد أرسل، وكذا إذا قال إن لم أبعث إليك نفقة هذا الشهر. ولو قال إن لم تجيئيني غداً بمتاع كذا فأنت طالق فبعثت به مع إنسان قال : إن كان مراده وصول عين المتاع إليه لا يحنث وإن كان غرضه أن تحمل بنفسها يحنث . ولو قال الرجل لأصحابه إن لم أذهب بكم الليلة إلى منزلي فامرأته طالق فذهب بهم الطريق فأخذهم العسس فحبسهم لا تطلق امرأته ؛ هكذا حكي عن الفقيه أبي جعفر. قال الفقيه أبو الليث : هذا الجواب يوافق قولهما في مسألة الكوز وقد مر في أول النوع اختيار الصدر الشهيد في جنس هذه المسائل بخلاف هذا ا هـ . ما في الذخيرة
Book of Oaths, Chapter on Oaths on Entering and Exiting. Some: He turned back and did not reach him, he does not break his oath. And if he came to his door and did not ask permission, he breaks his oath. He said in al-Muhit: And by analogy with the one who said 'If I do not leave this house today' and he was prevented or chained, he breaks his oath, so here he should break his oath in both cases, and that is the chosen view of our scholars. End. And if he said: 'If I do not send you your maintenance this month, then you are divorced,' and he sent it via a person and it was lost from the messenger's hand, he does not break his oath because he has sent. Similarly if he said 'If I do not dispatch to you the maintenance of this month.' And if he said: 'If you do not come to me tomorrow with such-and-such an item, then you are divorced,' and she sent it with a person, he said: If his intention was the arrival of the item itself, he does not break his oath; if his purpose was that she bring it herself, he breaks his oath. And if a man said to his companions: 'If I do not take you tonight to my house, then my wife is divorced,' and he went with them on the road and the night watch took them and imprisoned them, his wife is not divorced. Thus it is narrated from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This answer agrees with their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view in the case of the pitcher (مسألة الكوز) and it has passed at the beginning of the chapter that the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid in these types of issues is contrary to this. End. What is in al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several distinct rulings on oaths related to movement and sending goods. The first ruling concerns a person who swore to go to someone but turned back before reaching him: he does not break his oath unless he reached the door and did not seek permission to enter. However, according to al-Muhit, there is an analogy with a case where someone swore to leave his house but was physically prevented; in both cases the oath is broken. The passage then records a case of an oath to send maintenance: if the husband sends it via a messenger and it is lost, he is still considered to have sent it and does not break his oath. Similarly, if a wife is sworn to bring an item, the ruling depends on the husband's intention: if he intended the item to reach him, sending it with someone else suffices; if he intended her to bring it personally, it does not. Finally, an oath to take companions to a house is not broken if they are taken by the authorities on the way, according to a narration from Abu Ja'far. Abu al-Layth notes that this agrees with the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the case of the pitcher (a well-known legal analogy), although al-Sadr al-Shahid preferred a different opinion. The passage concludes by citing al-Dhakhira.

Key Terms
حنث (hanath): Breaking of an oath, i.e., committing the act sworn against or failing to do what was sworn.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, a solemn vow invoking Allah or a conditional statement that triggers a legal effect upon breach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband swears, 'If you do not come to me tomorrow with the package, then you are divorced.' The wife sends the package with a friend. According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does the wife not break the oath?
A) Always not break, because sending suffices.
B) Always break, because the wife did not come personally.
C) Not break if the husband intended only the arrival of the package; break if he intended her personal presence.
D) Break only if the husband explicitly said 'in person'.

Key Takeaway
In oaths involving movement and sending, the key is the literal act sworn and any qualifying intention; prevention may or may not cause hanth depending on the preferred scholarly view.

The Linguistic Meaning of 'Rawah' in Oaths
Definition of 'رواح' and its legal effect in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic meaning of 'رواح' and apply it to determine when an oath sworn with 'يروح' is broken.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'رواح' means any going – day or night – so an oath using it is broken by exiting, not arrival.

This lesson explores the term 'رواح' (rawah) as it appears in oaths. While classical Hanafi texts did not explicitly define it, later scholars like al-Azhari clarified that linguistically it means 'going' at any time, not just evening. This has a direct legal effect: if someone swears 'لا أروح إلى مكان' (I will not go to a place), the oath is broken as soon as he exits towards that place, even if he never reaches it. Understanding this prevents common misinterpretations in everyday oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in question
Locate the word 'رواح' in the passage and note its Arabic context. The author says it is frequently used in Egyptian speech for oaths but was not explicitly addressed by earlier Hanafi imams.
The term appears in the phrase 'لفظ الرواح' (the word rawah).
2
Understand the linguistic definition
Al-Azhari defines 'رواح' as simply 'الذهاب' (going), without restriction to a specific time. He explicitly says it can be at the beginning of the night, end of the night, or during the night – implying it applies to any time.
This is a quotation from outside the Hanafi school but adopted here.
3
Connect to the legal ruling in oaths
The author then applies the definition: if someone swears 'لا أروح إلى كذا' (I will not go to such-and-such), it means the same as 'لا أذهب' (I will not go) and 'لا أخرج' (I will not exit). Therefore, the oath is broken by the mere act of exiting towards the place, even if the person does not arrive.
The key is that the oath focuses on the departure, not the arrival.
4
Apply to a sample oath
Consider an oath: 'والله لا أروح إلى المسجد' (By Allah, I will not go to the mosque). If the person leaves his house heading to the mosque, his oath is broken immediately upon exiting, even if he turns back or never enters the mosque.
Confirm that 'going' equals 'exiting with intention to head towards'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم أر من صرح بلفظ الرواح من أئمتنا وهو كثير الوقوع في كلام المصريين وفي أيمانهم لكن قال الأزهري : لغة العرب أن الرواح الذهاب، سواء كان أول الليل أو آخره أو في الليل. قال النووي في شرح مسلم من كتاب الجمعة بعد نقله : وهذا هو الصواب ا هـ . فعلى هذا إذا حلف لا يروح إلى كذا فهو بمعنى لا يذهب وهو بمعنى الخروج يحنث بالخروج عن قصده وصل أو لا.
I have not seen any of our imams explicitly mention the word 'rawah' [in this context], and it happens frequently in the speech of the Egyptians and in their oaths. But al-Azhari said: In the language of the Arabs, 'rawah' means going, whether at the beginning of the night, the end, or during the night. Al-Nawawi said in his commentary on Muslim from the Book of Jumu'ah after quoting it: 'This is correct.' End. So based on this, if someone swears 'I will not go (لا أروح) to such-and-such,' it means 'I will not go' and it is equivalent to 'I will not exit'; he breaks his oath by exiting, whether he reaches his intended place or not.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the linguistic meaning of the word 'رواح' (rawah) as it appears in oaths. The author notes that while this term is commonly used in Egyptian speech for oaths, earlier Hanafi scholars had not explicitly addressed it. He then quotes al-Azhari, a renowned lexicographer, who states that 'رواح' simply means 'going' (الذهاب) at any time of the night, not specifically the evening. Al-Nawawi confirms this as correct. Consequently, if a person swears 'لا أروح إلى كذا' (I will not go to such-and-such), the oath is equivalent to 'لا أذهب' (I will not go) and also to 'لا أخرج' (I will not exit). Thus, he breaks his oath by the mere act of exiting towards the place, regardless of whether he actually arrives. This ruling is important for understanding the scope of an oath involving the term 'رواح'.

Key Terms
الرواح (al-rawah): Going, traveling; in the context of oaths, it means departing or heading towards a place, at any time of night or day.
اليمين بالرواح (al-yamin bi al-rawah): An oath where the condition is phrased using the verb 'يروح' (to go) or its negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Azhari, what does the word 'رواح' mean in the Arabic language?
A) Going only at the beginning of the night.
B) Going at any time of the night or day.
C) Going only at the end of the night.
D) Going only during the day.

Key Takeaway
An oath using 'يروح' (rawah) is broken by the act of exiting towards the intended place, regardless of arrival, because 'رواح' linguistically means any form of going.

Absolute Future Oaths and the Condition of Ability
Rulings on unqualified oaths to do future acts and the effect of ability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for absolute future oaths: state that the oath is not broken until despair of fulfillment (typically death), and identify the default condition of ability as physical health.

Core Idea
An absolute oath remains binding until fulfillment is impossible, most commonly by death.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies when an unqualified (absolute) oath to perform a future act is considered broken. Many assume that failure to act immediately triggers expiation, but Hanafi law holds that the oath remains intact as long as performance is physically possible. The key is 'despair of fulfillment' (al-iyas), which typically occurs only upon the death of one party. The passage also explains that the default ability required is physical health (استطاعة الصحة), and obstacles like forgetfulness or insanity prevent the oath from being broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of oath
Determine whether the oath is absolute (unqualified) or time-bound. Only absolute oaths follow this rule.
2
Apply the rule of despair
For an absolute oath, the oath is not broken as long as fulfillment is conceivable. The only event that makes fulfillment impossible is death (of either the oath-taker or the subject). Note that forgetfulness, insanity, or prevention by authority are barriers that prevent the oath from being broken.
3
Examine the condition of ability
If the oath was conditioned on ability, the default meaning is physical ability (soundness of limbs and removal of obstacles). If the oath-taker intended a narrower ability (e.g., immediate convenience), that is accepted only between him and Allah but does not change the legal ruling.
4
Derive the practical consequence
As long as the oath-taker and the subject are alive, the oath remains in effect. Only upon death does it become impossible and thus the oath is broken (hanth), requiring expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ليأتينه فلم يأته حتى مات حنث في آخر حياته لأن البرّ قبل ذلك موجود ولا خصوصية للإتيان بل كل فعل حلف أنه يفعله في المستقبل وأطلقه لم يقيده بوقت لم يحنث حتى يقع الإياس عن البر مثل ليضربن زيداً أو ليعطين فلانة أو ليطلقن زوجته وتحقق اليأس عن البر يكون بفوت أحدهما فلذا قال في غاية البيان وأصل هذا أن الحالف في اليمين يراح ليأتينه إن استطاع فهي استطاعة الصحة وإن نوى القدرة دين لا تخرجي إلا بإذني شرط المطلقة لا يحنث ما دام الحالف والمحلوف عليه قائمين لتصور البر، فإذا فات أحدهما فإنه يحنث ا هـ. وبهذا ظهر أن الضمير في قوله حتى مات يعود إلى أحدهما أيهما كان، سواء كان الحالف أو المحلوف عليه، لا أنه خاص بالحالف كما هو المتبادر من العبارة. وقيد باليمين المطلقة لأنها لو كانت مقيدة كقوله إن لم أدخل هذه الدار اليوم فعبده حر فإن الحنث معلق بآخر الوقت حتى إذا مات الحالف قبل خروج الوقت ولم يدخل الدار لا يحنث، وأما إذا مضى الوقت قبل دخوله وهو حي عتق العبد ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. ثم اعلم أن اليمين المطلقة لا تكون على الفور إلا بقرينة ففي الظهيرية في الفصل السابع: ولو حلف إن رأى فلاناً ليضربنه فالرؤية على القريب والبعيد والضرب متى شاء إلا أن يعني الفور. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : رجل أراد أن يواقع امرأته وكانت امرأته على باب الدار فقال لها إن لم تدخلي معي في الدار فأنت طالق فدخلت بعد ما سكنت شهوته وقع الطلاق عليها، وإن دخلت قبل ذلك لم تطلق : وفي الفصل الخامس : حلف ليضر بن غلامه في كل حق وباطل وليس له نية فهو على أن يضربه كل ما شكى إليه بحق أو باطل ولا يكون يمينه على فور الشكاية ما لم ينو وسيأتي تمام مسائل الفور إن شاء الله تعالى قريباً قوله : (ليأتينه إن استطاع فهي استطاعة الصحة) لأنها المرادة في العرف وهي سلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب. وفسرها محمد رحمه الله بقوله : إذا لم يمرض ولم يمنعه السلطان ولم يجىء أمر لا يقدر على إتيانه فلم يأته حنث ا هـ. فعلى هذا المراد بسلامة الآلات صحة الجوارح فالمريض ليس بمستطيع، والمراد بصحة الأسباب تهيئة لإرادة الفعل على وجه الاختيار فخرج الممنوع ولذا ذكر في الاختيار أنها سلامة الآلات ورفع الموانع. وفي المبسوط : الاستطاعة رفع الموانع ا هـ. فينبغي أنه إذا نسي اليمين سلامة الآلات ورفع الموانع في المبسوط : الاستطاعة رفع الموانع ا هـ. فينبغي أنه إذا نسي اليمين لا يحنث لأن النسيان مانع ، وكذا لو جن فلم يأته حتى مضى الغد كما لا يخفى ولذا قال في غاية البيان : وحدها التهيؤ لتقييد الفعل على إرادة المختار قوله: (وإن نوى القدرة دين) أي صدق فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لأن حقيقتها فيما يقارن الفعل. ويطلق الاسم على سلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب في المتعارف فعند الإطلاق ينصرف إليه، وتصح نية ذلك اهـ . الناس أن الرواح لا يكون إلا في آخر النهار وليس كذلك بل الرواح والغدة عند العرب يستعملان في المسير أي وقت كان من ليل أو نهار قاله الأزهري وغيره. وعليه قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام من راح إلى الجمعة في أول النهار فله كذا أي من ذهب ثم قال الأزهري : وأما راحت الإبل فلا يكون إلا بالعشي إذا أراحها على أهلها يقال سرحت الإبل بالغداة إلى المرعى وراحت بالعشي على أهلها أي رجعت من المرعى إليهم فهي رائحة اهـ.
His saying: 'If he swore that he would come to him and he did not come until he died, he breaks his oath at the end of his life because before that the fulfillment was possible, and there is no particularity to 'coming'; rather, every action that one swears to do in the future absolutely without specifying a time, he does not break his oath until despair of fulfillment occurs, such as 'I will surely strike Zaid' or 'I will surely give to so-and-so' or 'I will surely divorce my wife,' and the realization of despair of fulfillment occurs by the demise of one of them. Hence, it is said in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'The origin of this is that the one who swears an oath...' ... 'He swore to come to him if he is able, and this ability is the ability of health (استطاعة الصحة). And if he intends [only] the ability [at the time of performance], he is truthful in his intention [before Allah].' ... 'Do not go out except with my permission' is a condition for a divorced woman. He does not break his oath as long as the one swearing and the one sworn about are alive, because fulfillment is conceivable. If one of them dies, then he breaks his oath. End. And by this it is clear that the pronoun in 'until he died' refers to either one, whichever it may be, whether the one swearing or the one sworn about; it is not specific to the one swearing as apparent from the wording. And it is restricted to the absolute oath because if it were restricted, like saying 'If I do not enter this house today, then my slave is free,' then breaking is suspended until the end of the time. If the one swearing dies before the time expires without entering, he does not break; but if the time passes without his entry while he is alive, the slave is freed. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. Then know that the absolute oath is not immediate except with a context. In al-Zahiriyyah in the seventh chapter: If he swore 'If I see so-and-so, I will surely strike him,' the seeing is for near and far, and the striking may be whenever he wishes unless he intends immediacy. In the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: A man wanted to have intercourse with his wife while she was at the door of the house; he said to her, 'If you do not enter with me into the house, you are divorced.' She entered after his desire subsided; the divorce occurs. But if she entered before that, she is not divorced. In the fifth chapter: He swore to beat his slave for every right and wrong, and he has no intention; he is to beat him whenever he complains to him with right or wrong, and his oath is not for immediacy of the complaint unless he intends it. And the complete issues of immediacy will come soon, Allah willing. His saying: 'If he swore to come to him if he is able, this is the ability of health' because that is what is meant customarily, which is soundness of limbs and validity of means. Muhammad (rahimahullah) interpreted it as: if he is not ill, the ruler does not prevent him, and no matter occurs that he cannot come, and he does not come, he breaks his oath. End. So by this, what is meant by soundness of limbs is health of the organs; the sick person is not able. And what is meant by validity of means is the preparedness to bring about the action by choice, so the prevented is excluded. Hence it is mentioned in al-Ikhtiyar: it is soundness of limbs and removal of obstacles. And in al-Mabsut: ability is removal of obstacles. End. So it should be that if he forgets the oath, he does not break his oath because forgetfulness is an obstacle. Likewise if he becomes insane and does not come until the next day passes, as is not hidden. Hence it is said in Ghayat al-Bayan: Its definition is preparedness to bring about the action with free choice. His saying: 'And if he intends ability (power at the time of action), he is truthful (between him and Allah)' because its reality is what accompanies the action. And the name is applied commonly to soundness of limbs and validity of means, so when unqualified it applies to that, and the intention of that is valid. End. The people [think] that 'rawah' is only at the end of the day, but it is not so; rather, 'rawah' and 'ghadwa' (morning) in the language of the Arabs are used for journeying at any time, night or day, as al-Azhari and others said. And based on it is his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever goes (راح) to Jumu'ah at the beginning of the day, for him is such-and-such,' meaning whoever goes. Then al-Azhari said: As for 'راحت الإبل' (the camels returned), it is only in the evening when they return to their owners; it is said: 'سرحت الإبل بالغداة إلى المرعى' (the camels set out in the morning to pasture) and 'راحت بالعشي على أهلها' (they returned in the evening to their owners) i.e. they came back from pasture to them. So they are 'رائحة'. End.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the ruling for an absolute oath to perform an action in the future. The key principle is that if a person swears to do something without specifying a time limit, the oath is not broken as long as it is still possible to fulfill it. The only thing that definitively breaks such an oath is the death of either the oath-taker or the person to whom the oath relates, because after death, fulfillment becomes impossible. The passage also discusses the condition of ability (استطاعة). When an oath is conditioned on ability, the default meaning is physical ability (استطاعة الصحة), i.e., being healthy and free from obstacles. If the oath-taker intends a narrower ability (such as being able to do it at the exact moment), his intention is accepted between him and Allah but does not change the legal ruling. The commentary further explores the implications of forgetting the oath or insanity as obstacles. It also touches on the immediacy of absolute oaths, clarifying that they are not immediate unless there is a context indicating so, and gives examples from Fatawa. Finally, the passage returns to the definition of 'رواح' to confirm that it means going at any time, not just evening, which aligns with the earlier discussion.

Key Terms
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamin al-mutlaqah): An oath that is not restricted by a time limit or condition; it is open-ended until fulfillment becomes impossible.
الاستطاعة (al-istita'ah): Ability or capacity to perform the action; in oaths, it defaults to physical ability (soundness of limbs and absence of obstacles).
الإياس (al-iyas): Despair of fulfillment; it occurs when it becomes impossible for the oath to be fulfilled, typically by the death of one party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears an absolute oath to visit his friend (no time limit), when does he break his oath?
A) Immediately if he does not visit the next day.
B) After one year has passed without visiting.
C) Only when either he or his friend dies, because before that it is still possible.
D) When his friend moves away and can no longer be visited.

Key Takeaway
An absolute oath to perform a future act is not broken until fulfillment becomes impossible, most commonly by the death of a party.

Definition of Istita'ah in Oaths and Effect of Intention
Explaining the legal meaning of istita'ah and the ruling on intending a different meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of istita'ah in oaths and explain why intending a different meaning (qudra) is accepted only religiously, not judicially.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, istita'ah means physical health and absence of obstacles, not the ability to act at the moment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the legal meaning of 'istita'ah' (ability) in oaths. The Hanafi school grounds its definition on customary usage, distinguishing between apparent ability and the philosophical concept of power simultaneous with action. Understanding this nuance is critical for correctly evaluating oath conditions and the role of intention in court versus before God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal element
The passage focuses on the condition 'if he is able' in an oath. Identify that the ruling concerns the validity of the oath condition.
The act is swearing an oath with a condition of ability.
2
State the Hanafi definition of istita'ah
From the text, istita'ah is defined as physical health and absence of obstacles (salama al-alat wa sihha al-asbab). Understand that this is the customary meaning.
Imam Muhammad's interpretation: not sick, not prevented by ruler, no impossible matter.
3
Differentiate between dinan and qada'an
If the swearer intends 'qudra' (capacity at the moment), the intention is valid religiously (dinan) but not accepted in court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent meaning.
The judge rules based on the customary meaning, not the secret intention.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis said the intention is accepted judicially too. The Ash'ariyya define power as concurrent with action. The Hanafi response is that obligation is based on apparent ability.
Fath al-Qadir preferred the preponderant view that intention is not judicially accepted.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a person swears 'I will come if I am able' and then is sick, or intends qudra but is healthy, determine the outcome.
Sick: no breach. Healthy but intended qudra: judge rules breach because customary meaning prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليأتينه إن استطاع فهي استطاعة الصحة وإن نوى القدرة دين لا تخرجي إلا بإذني شرط المطلقة لا يحنث ما دام الحالف والمحلوف عليه قائمين لتصور البر، فإذا فات أحدهما فإنه يحنث ا هـ. وبهذا ظهر أن الضمير في قوله حتى مات يعود إلى أحدهما أيهما كان، سواء كان الحالف أو المحلوف عليه، لا أنه خاص بالحالف كما هو المتبادر من العبارة. وقيد باليمين المطلقة لأنها لو كانت مقيدة كقوله إن لم أدخل هذه الدار اليوم فعبده حر فإن الحنث معلق بآخر الوقت حتى إذا مات الحالف قبل خروج الوقت ولم يدخل الدار لا يحنث، وأما إذا مضى الوقت قبل دخوله وهو حي عتق العبد ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. ثم اعلم أن اليمين المطلقة لا تكون على الفور إلا بقرينة ففي الظهيرية في الفصل السابع: ولو حلف إن رأى فلاناً ليضربنه فالرؤية على القريب والبعيد والضرب متى شاء إلا أن يعني الفور. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : رجل أراد أن يواقع امرأته وكانت امرأته على باب الدار فقال لها إن لم تدخلي معي في الدار فأنت طالق فدخلت بعد ما سكنت شهوته وقع الطلاق عليها، وإن دخلت قبل ذلك لم تطلق : وفي الفصل الخامس : حلف ليضر بن غلامه في كل حق وباطل وليس له نية فهو على أن يضربه كل ما شكى إليه بحق أو باطل ولا يكون يمينه على فور الشكاية ما لم ينو وسيأتي تمام مسائل الفور إن شاء الله تعالى قريباً قوله : (ليأتينه إن استطاع فهي استطاعة الصحة) لأنها المرادة في العرف وهي سلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب. وفسرها محمد رحمه الله بقوله : إذا لم يمرض ولم يمنعه السلطان ولم يجىء أمر لا يقدر على إتيانه فلم يأته حنث ا هـ. فعلى هذا المراد بسلامة الآلات صحة الجوارح فالمريض ليس بمستطيع، والمراد بصحة الأسباب تهيئة لإرادة الفعل على وجه الاختيار فخرج الممنوع ولذا ذكر في الاختيار أنها سلامة الآلات ورفع الموانع. وفي المبسوط : الاستطاعة رفع الموانع ا هـ. فينبغي أنه إذا نسي اليمين سلامة الآلات ورفع الموانع في المبسوط : الاستطاعة رفع الموانع ا هـ. فينبغي أنه إذا نسي اليمين لا يحنث لأن النسيان مانع ، وكذا لو جن فلم يأته حتى مضى الغد كما لا يخفى ولذا قال في غاية البيان : وحدها التهيؤ لتقييد الفعل على إرادة المختار قوله: (وإن نوى القدرة دين) أي صدق فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى لأن حقيقتها فيما يقارن الفعل. ويطلق الاسم على سلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب في المتعارف فعند الإطلاق ينصرف إليه، وتصح نية ذلك اهـ. لكل خروج أذن بخلاف إلا ان وحتى ولو أرادت الخروج فقال إن خرجت أو ضرب ألفاظ الأول ديانة لأنه حقيقة كلامه وظاهر كلامه أنه لا يصدق قضاء لأنه خلاف الظاهر، وقيل يصدق قضاء أيضاً لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وإذا صدق لا يتصور حنثه أبداً لأنها لا تسبق الفعل. ورجح في فتح القدير الأول بأنه أوجه لأنه وإن كان مشتركاً بينهما لكن تعورف استعماله عند الإطلاق عن القرينة لأحد المعنيين بخصوصه وهو سلامة آلات الفعل وصحة أسبابه فصار ظاهراً فيه بخصوصه فلا يصدقه القاضي في خلاف الظاهر ا هـ. وقد أظهر الزاهدي في المجتبى اعتزاله في هذا المحل كما أظهره في القنية في موضعين من التكفير عبارته في المجتبى قلت وفي قوله حقيقة الاستطاعة فيما يقارن الفعل نظر قوي لأنه بناه على مذهب الأشعرية والسنية أن القدرة تقارن الفعل وأنه باطل إذ لو كان كذلك لما كان فرعون وهامان وسائر الكفرة الذين ماتوا على الكفر قادرين على الإيمان وكان تكليفهم بالإيمان تكليفاً بما لا يطاق، وكان إرسال الرسل والأنبياء وإنزال الكتب والأوامر والنواهي والوعد والوعيد ضائعة في حقهم ا.هـ. وهو غلط لأن التكليف ليس مشروطاً بهذه القدرة حتى يلزم ما ذكره وإنما هو مشروط بالقدرة الظاهرة و ة وهي سلامة الآلات وصحة الأسباب كما عرف في الأصول.
Let him come to him if he is able, so it is the ability of health. And if he intended (to mean) capacity, it is (valid) religion-wise. 'Do not go out except with my permission' is a condition for the divorced woman. She does not break the oath as long as the swearer and the sworn about exist, because the possibility of fulfillment is present. If one of them ceases, he breaks the oath. End. By this it appears that the pronoun in 'until he dies' refers to either of them, not specifically the swearer. It is restricted to the absolute oath; if it were conditional, the breaking is suspended until the end of the time. If the swearer dies before time ends and hasn't entered, he does not break; if time passes before entry while he is alive, the slave is freed. In Ghayat al-Bayan. Then know that the absolute oath is not immediate except with a context. In al-Zahiriyya chapter seven: If he swore upon seeing someone to beat him, the seeing is relative to near and far, beating whenever except if he meant immediacy. In Fatawa Abi al-Layth: A man wanted intercourse, his wife at door said 'If you don't enter with me you are divorced' – she entered after his desire subsided, divorce occurs; if before, not. Chapter five: He swore to beat his slave in right and wrong without intention – it is upon each complaint, not immediate unless intended. He said: 'Let him come if he is able – it is ability of health' because it is customary, which is soundness of limbs and validity of causes. Muhammad interpreted: if not sick, not prevented by ruler, no impossible matter, then if he doesn't come he breaks oath. By this, soundness of limbs means health of organs, so sick not able; validity of causes means readiness for voluntary act, so prevented excluded. In al-Ikhtiyar: soundness of limbs and removal of obstacles. In al-Mabsut: istita'ah is removal of obstacles. So if he forgets the oath, he does not break because forgetfulness is an obstacle, also if insane. In Ghayat al-Bayan: its limit is readiness to restrict action by free will. He said: 'And if he intended capacity, it is religion-wise' – he is truthful between himself and God because its reality is concomitant with the act. The name is applied to soundness of limbs and causes customarily, so upon absolute use it refers to that, and the intention is valid. End. For every exit there is permission, unlike 'illa an' and 'hatta'. If she wanted to exit and said 'If I exit' or 'beat' – words, the first is religiously because it is the truth of his speech, but he is not believed judicially because it contradicts the apparent. It is said he is believed judicially too because he intended the truth of his speech; if believed, his breaking is never conceived because it does not precede the act. Fath al-Qadir preponderated the first because although the word is shared, its customary use without context for one meaning (soundness of limbs and causes) made it apparent for that specific meaning, so the judge does not accept him against the apparent. Al-Zahidi in al-Mujtaba dissented, saying his statement that the reality of istita'ah is concomitant with the act is questionable because it builds on the Ash'ari view that power is concomitant, which is false – otherwise Pharaoh, Haman, and all disbelievers who died on disbelief would be incapable of faith and obligation would be impossible, making missions and books futile. This is erroneous because obligation is not conditioned on this power but on apparent power, which is soundness of limbs and causes as known in usul.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, analyzing the meaning of 'istita'ah' (ability) in oaths. The Hanafi position is that the term, when used without qualification, refers to physical health and the absence of external obstacles (salama al-alat wa sihha al-asbab), not to the actual capability at the moment of action (qudra). The author explains that Imam Muhammad interpreted 'ability' as the absence of sickness, state prevention, or an impossible matter. Thus, if the person is healthy and unhindered, the condition is fulfilled. The text also addresses the effect of intention: if the swearer intends by 'istita'ah' the meaning of 'qudra' (capability at the moment), this is valid religiously (dinan) – he is truthful before God – but the judge does not accept it in court because the customary meaning prevails. A minority view holds that the intention is accepted even judicially, but the preponderant view (as in Fath al-Qadir) rejects this. The passage then refutes a criticism by al-Zahidi, who argued that the Ash'ari definition of power leads to absurdities. The Hanafi response is that legal obligation is based on apparent ability (al-qudra al-zahira), i.e., health and absence of obstacles, not on the power simultaneous with the act. This holds for all schools within Hanafism.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة (al-istita'ah): In oaths, it means physical health and absence of obstacles.
القدرة (al-qudra): Capability to act at the moment of action.
اليمين المطلقة (al-yamin al-mutlaqa): An absolute oath without time limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant Hanafi view, what is the judicial (qada'an) ruling if a person swears 'If I am able, I will come' and then, being healthy, does not come, but later claims he intended 'qudra' (capability at the moment)?
A) He does not break the oath because his intention is valid.
B) He breaks the oath because the judge interprets 'able' according to the customary meaning.
C) The oath is void due to ambiguity.
D) He must pay kaffarah for breaking the oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'istita'ah' defaults to physical health and absence of obstacles; a different intention is valid only before God, not in court.

Difference between 'illa bi-idhni' and 'illa an adhana' in Oaths of Exit
Clarifying the ruling on permission for exit when the oath uses different phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two oath formulations and state the resulting ruling for each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'except with my permission' requires repeated permission; 'except that I permit' ends with one.

The Hanafi school pays close attention to the precise wording of oaths. The difference between 'illa bi-idhni' and 'illa an adhana' may seem subtle, but it has significant legal consequences. This lesson will clarify how each phrase affects the obligation to seek permission for exit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two formulations
Recognize the Arabic phrases: 'illa bi-idhni' (except with my permission) and 'illa an adhana' (except that I permit).
2
Determine the legal effect of each
For 'illa bi-idhni', each exit requires separate permission. For 'illa an adhana', one permission ends the oath.
3
Understand the linguistic reasoning
The author explains the difference using grammatical principles: the continuous exception vs. the limit (ghaya).
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a husband's statement, classify it and rule whether a subsequent exit without permission breaks the oath.
5
Note the exception in the Quranic verse
The repetition requirement in verse 33:53 is due to an external cause, not the language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا تخرجي إلا بإذني شرط لكل خروج إذن بخلاف إلا أن وحتى) أي بخلاف لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك أو حتى أن أذن لك فأذن لها مرة انتهت اليمين حتى لو خرجت بإذنه ثم خرجت بعده بغير إذنه لا يحنث والفرق في الأول أن المستثنى خروج مقرون بالإذن لأنه مفرغ للمتعلق فصار المعنى إلا خروجاً ملصقاً به فما لم يكن ملصقاً بالإذن فهو داخل في اليمين لعموم النكرة فيحنث به وفي الثاني الإذن غاية، أما في «حتى» فظاهر، وأما في «إلا أن فتجوز بـ«إلا فيها لتعذر استثناء الإذن من الخروج وبالمرة يتحقق فينتهي المحلوف عليه . وأما لزوم تكرار الإذن في دخول بيوته عليه السلام مع تلك الصيغة إلا أن يؤذن لكم [الأحزاب: ٥٣] فبدليل خارجي وهو تعليله بالأذى إن ذلكـم كـان يؤذي النبي [الأحزاب: ٥٣] وتمامه في الأصول في بحث الباء ولا يرد أن إلا أن آذن» بمعنى «إلا بإذني لأن «إن» والفعل في تأويل المصدر ولا بد من تقدير الباء وإلا صار المعنى إلا خروجاً إذني فصار كالمسألة الأولى لأنه يلزم أحد الأمرين. أما ما ذكر من تقدير الباء محذوفة أو ما قلنا من جعلها بمعنى «حتى» مجازاً أي حتى آذن لك. وعلى الأول يكون كالأول، وعلى الثاني ينعقد على إذن واحد ، وإذا لزم في إلا أن أحد المجازين وجب الراجح منهما، ومجاز غير الحذف أولى . من مجاز الحذف عندهم لأنه تصرف في وصف نفس اللفظ ومجاز الحذف تصرف قوله : (يلزم أحد الأمرين) علة لقوله «ولا يرد».
He said: 'Do not go out except with my permission' is a condition for every exit; permission is needed for each, unlike 'illa an' and 'hatta'. That is, unlike 'Do not go out except that I permit you' or 'until I permit you'. If he permits her once, the oath ends, so that if she goes out with his permission then afterwards without permission, she does not break the oath. The difference: in the first, the excepted thing is an exit accompanied by permission because it is a continuous exception, so the meaning is 'except an exit attached to it'; what is not attached to permission is included in the oath due to the generality of the indefinite, so she breaks. In the second, permission is a limit; in 'hatta' it is obvious, and in 'illa an' it is interpreted as 'illa' due to the impossibility of excepting permission from exit, and with one occurrence the limit is reached, so the sworn matter ends. The necessity of repeating permission in the verse 'except that you be permitted' [33:53] is due to an external proof – the harm it caused. The full discussion is in usul. And it is not objected that 'illa an adhana' means 'illa bi-idhni' because 'in' and the verb are in the place of a masdar, and the preposition 'ba' must be assumed, otherwise the meaning becomes 'except an exit of my permission' making it like the first case, necessitating one of two things: either assuming the 'ba' is deleted or taking it metaphorically to mean 'hatta'. On the first, it becomes like the first case; on the second, it binds with one permission. When in 'illa an' one of the two metaphors is necessary, the preponderant one is required, and metaphor without deletion is preferable because it affects the description of the word itself. He said 'one of two things is necessary' is the cause for his saying 'and it is not objected'.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the difference between two common phrases used in oaths regarding a wife's exit. If the husband says 'لا تخرجي إلا بإذني' (Do not go out except with my permission), each exit requires separate permission; the oath is continuous. However, if he says 'لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك' (Do not go out except that I permit you) or 'حتى آذن لك' (until I permit you), then one single permission ends the oath, and subsequent exits without permission do not break it. The grammatical reasoning is that in the first phrase, the exception is of an exit qualified by permission, so the indefinite 'exit' is general and any exit without permission is included. In the second, permission is the limit (ghaya); once given, the oath is complete. The author also addresses the Quranic verse about entering the Prophet's houses (33:53) where repetition of permission is required, but he explains that is due to an external cause (harm), not the linguistic form. The discussion includes a preference for interpreting 'illa an' as a metaphor for 'hatta' rather than assuming a deleted preposition.

Key Terms
إلا بإذني (illa bi-idhni): A phrase meaning 'except with my permission', requiring permission for each occurrence.
إلا أن آذن (illa an adhana): A phrase meaning 'except that I permit', interpreted as setting a limit; one permission ends the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband tells his wife 'لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك' and then gives her permission once, what is the ruling if she goes out a second time without permission?
A) She breaks the oath because each exit needs permission.
B) She does not break the oath because the first permission ended the oath.
C) She breaks the oath only if she goes out more than three times.
D) The oath is invalid because the phrase is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The phrasing of an oath determines whether permission is needed for each exit or just once.

Distinguishing Conditional and Temporal Permission in Oaths
Explaining the difference between conditional and temporal permission in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the legal effects of the phrases 'except with my permission' and 'until I permit' in marital oaths and apply the Hanafi rule that the former requires repeated permission.

Core Idea
A conditional 'except with my permission' requires repeated consent; a temporal 'until I permit' ends the oath with one permission.

In Hanafi law, the wording of an oath determines its scope. The husband's declaration 'Do not go out except with my permission' creates a repetitive condition, while 'except that I permit' or 'until I permit' acts as a one-time limit. This lesson explores the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this distinction, including the role of intention and the exceptional case of the Quranic verse on entering the Prophet's house.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase
Determine whether the phrase uses 'illa bi-idhni' (conditional) or 'illa an ta'dhana' / 'hatta ta'dhana' (temporal). The former implies a repeated condition; the latter implies a limit.
The Arabic wording is key: 'إلا بإذني' vs 'إلا أن آذن' / 'حتى آذن'.
2
Analyze the grammatical structure
In the conditional phrase, the excepted item is an exit attached to permission (mustathna mufargh). In the temporal phrase, permission is a ghayah (limit/goal). This grammatical difference leads to different legal outcomes.
The author explains that in 'illa bi-idhni', the ba indicates attachment, so only exits with permission are excluded.
3
Apply the ruling
If conditional, each exit requires a new permission; if temporal, one permission ends the oath. Thus, in 'except with my permission', the wife breaks the oath if she goes out without permission even after first permission.
Exception: if the husband swears by divorce, the ruling may differ.
4
Consider the husband's intention
If the husband intended only one permission in the conditional phrase, his claim is accepted religiously (diyaanatan) but not in court (qadaan). The fatwa follows the apparent wording.
This is from al-Walwaljiyya: he is believed before Allah but not by the judge.
5
Understand the Quranic exception
The verse 'إلا أن يؤذن لكم' (Al-Ahzab 33:53) requiring repeated permission for entering the Prophet's house is due to an external reason (annoyance), not the general rule. In normal oaths, 'illa an ta'dhana' is interpreted as 'until I permit'.
Thus the verse does not override the standard interpretation of temporal permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا تخرجي إلا بإذني شرط لكل خروج إذن بخلاف إلا أن وحتى) أي بخلاف لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك أو حتى أن أذن لك فأذن لها مرة انتهت اليمين حتى لو خرجت بإذنه ثم خرجت بعده بغير إذنه لا يحنث والفرق في الأول أن المستثنى خروج مقرون بالإذن لأنه مفرغ للمتعلق فصار المعنى إلا خروجاً ملصقاً به فما لم يكن ملصقاً بالإذن فهو داخل في اليمين لعموم النكرة فيحنث به وفي الثاني الإذن غاية، أما في «حتى» فظاهر، وأما في «إلا أن فتجوز بـ«إلا فيها لتعذر استثناء الإذن من الخروج وبالمرة يتحقق فينتهي المحلوف عليه . وأما لزوم تكرار الإذن في دخول بيوته عليه السلام مع تلك الصيغة إلا أن يؤذن لكم [الأحزاب: ٥٣] فبدليل خارجي وهو تعليله بالأذى إن ذلكـم كـان يؤذي النبي [الأحزاب: ٥٣] وتمامه في الأصول في بحث الباء ولا يرد أن إلا أن آذن» بمعنى «إلا بإذني لأن «إن» والفعل في تأويل المصدر ولا بد من تقدير الباء وإلا صار المعنى إلا خروجاً إذني فصار كالمسألة الأولى لأنه يلزم أحد الأمرين. أما ما ذكر من تقدير الباء محذوفة أو ما قلنا من جعلها بمعنى «حتى» مجازاً أي حتى آذن لك. وعلى الأول يكون كالأول، وعلى الثاني ينعقد على إذن واحد ، وإذا لزم في إلا أن أحد المجازين وجب الراجح منهما، ومجاز غير الحذف أولى . من مجاز الحذف عندهم لأنه تصرف في وصف نفس اللفظ ومجاز الحذف تصرف قوله : (يلزم أحد الأمرين) علة لقوله «ولا يرد».
في ذاته بالإعدام مع الإرادة. وأشار المصنف بقوله «شرط أنه لو نوى الإذن مرة واحدة لم يصدق قضاء وعليه الفتوى كما في الولوالجية، لكنه يصدق ديانة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه فيستعار بمعنى «حتى» لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي بخلاف ما إذا نوى التعدد في المسألة الثانية حيث يصدق قضاء لأنه محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد على نفسه. ومثل قوله إلا بإذن بغير إذني فيشترط لكل خروج إذن لأن المعنى فيهما واحد مع وجود الباء، والرضا والأمر والعلم كالإذن فيما ذكرنا، كذلك إن خرجت إلا بقناع أو بملحفة ولو قال لها أذنت لك في الخروج كلما أردت فخرجت مرة بعد أخرى لا يحنث فإن نهاها عن الخروج بعد ذلك صح النهي وهذا قول محمد وبه أخذ الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل. ولو أذن لها في الخروج ثم قال لها كلما نهيتك فقد أذنت لك فنهاها لا يصح نهيه إياها، ولو أذن لها بالعربية ولا عهد لها بالعربية فخرجت حنث كما لو أذن لها وهي نائمة أو ومحمد لا يثبت الأمر بدون العلم والسماع والصحيح أن على قولهما لا يكون الإذن إلا بالسماع لأن الإذن إيقاع الخبر في الإذن وذلك لا يكون إلا بالسماع، وأجمعوا أن إذن العبد في التجارة لا يكون إلا بالسماع ولو أن المرأة سمعت سائلاً يسأل شيئاً بعد ما منعها زوجها عن الخروج إلا بإذنه فقال لها الزوج ادفعي هذه الكسرة إليه، فإن كان السائل بحيث لا تقدر المرأة على الدفع إليه إلا بالخروج فخرجت لا يحنث وإلا فيحنث ولو قالت لزوجها تريد أن أخرج حتى أصير مطلقة فقال الزوج نعم فخرجت طلقت لأن كلام الزوج هذا للتهديد لا للإذن، ولو قال لها أما والله لو خرجت ليخزينك الله تعالى ونحو ذلك قال محمد: لا يكون إذناً. وكذا او غضبت المرأة وتأهبت للخروج فقال الزوج دعوها تخرج لم يكن إذناً إلا أن ينوي الإذن . وكذا لو قال الزوج في غضبه أخرجي ينوي التهديد والتوعيد يعني أخرجي حتى تطلقي لم يكن ذلك إذناً. ولو قال لامرأته إن خرجت من هذه الدار فأنت طالق فخرجت قبل أن يقول الزوج طالق لم يحنث حتى تخرج مرة أخرى إلا أن يكون ابتداء اليمين مخاشنة كانت بينهما في الخروج فمتى كانت كذلك لا يحنث وإن خرجت بعد ذلك لأن اليمين كانت على الخروج الأول؛ الكل من الظهيرية .
اخرجي
.
وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة وفي قوله لها إن خرجت من الدار إلا بإذني فأنت طالق لا يحنث بخروجها الوقوع غرق أو حرق غالب فيها وكذا في القنية ا هـ. وفي القنية : لو
His statement: (Do not go out except with my permission is a condition for every exit requiring permission, unlike 'except that I permit' and 'until') meaning unlike 'do not go out except that I permit you' or 'until I permit you', so if he permits her once the oath ends, to the extent that if she goes out with his permission and then goes out afterwards without his permission she does not break the oath. The difference in the first is that the excepted thing is an exit accompanied by permission, because it is an empty [construction] attached to that which is connected, so the meaning becomes 'except an exit attached to it', so whatever is not attached to permission enters the oath due to the generality of the indefinite noun, so she breaks the oath by it. In the second, permission is a limit. As for 'until', it is apparent. As for 'except that', it is interpreted metaphorically with 'except' due to impossibility of excepting permission from exit, and with the occurrence the limit is realized so the sworn matter ends. As for the necessity of repeating permission in the entry into his houses - peace be upon him - with that formula 'except that you be permitted' [Al-Ahzab 33:53], it is due to an external evidence, which is its explanation by annoyance: 'that used to annoy the Prophet' [Al-Ahzab 33:53], and its completion is in the principles in the discussion of the preposition 'ba'. It cannot be objected that 'except that I permit' is in the meaning of 'except with my permission', because 'in' and the verb are in the interpretation of a verbal noun, and the preposition 'ba' must be estimated, otherwise the meaning becomes 'except a permission of mine' so it becomes like the first case because one of two things is necessitated. Either what is mentioned of the estimated 'ba' being omitted or what we said of making it metaphorical in the meaning of 'until', i.e., until I permit you. On the first, it is like the first; on the second, it is contracted on one permission. And when in 'except that' one of the two metaphors is necesitated, the more preponderant of them must be taken, and the metaphor without omission is better than the metaphor of omission according to them because it is a change in the description of the word itself, while the metaphor of omission is a change in its essence by removal with intention. The author indicated by his statement 'condition' that if he intended one permission, he is not believed judicially, and the fatwa is on that as in al-Walwaljiyya, but he is believed religiously because he intended a probable meaning of his speech, so it is used metaphorically in the meaning of 'until', but it is contrary to the apparent meaning so the judge does not believe him, unlike if he intended multiplicity in the second case where he is believed judicially because it is a probable meaning of his speech and it contains severity upon himself. And similar is his statement 'except with permission' to 'without my permission', so permission is required for each exit because the meaning in both is one with the presence of the 'ba'. And approval, command, and knowledge are like permission in what we mentioned. Likewise, 'if you go out except with a veil or with a cloak'. And if he said to her 'I have permitted you to go out whenever you want', and she went out once after another, he does not break the oath. If he forbids her from going out after that, the prohibition is valid. This is the statement of Muhammad, and Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl adopted it. And if he permitted her to go out, then said to her 'whenever I forbid you, I have permitted you', then he forbids her, his prohibition is not valid. And if he permitted her in Arabic while she has no knowledge of Arabic, and she went out, she breaks the oath, just as if he permitted her while she was sleeping or absent and did not hear, and she went out, she breaks the oath. Some said: This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar, it is permission. And some said: Permission is valid without knowledge and hearing in their statement, and the disagreement among them is only regarding command. According to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, command is not established without knowledge and hearing. And the correct is that according to their statement, permission is only by hearing because permission is the delivery of information about permission, and that is only by hearing. And they unanimously agreed that a slave's permission for trade is only by hearing. And if this woman swept the house and then went out to the door of the house to sweep the doorstep, she breaks the oath because she went out without his permission. And if he permitted her to go out to some of her family, and she did not go out, then she went out at another time to some of her [...]? Faqih Abu al-Layth said: I fear that she breaks the oath. And if a woman heard a beggar asking for something after her husband forbade her from going out except with his permission, and her husband said to her 'give this piece of bread to him', if the beggar is such that the woman cannot give it to him except by going out, and she goes out, she does not break the oath; otherwise she breaks the oath. And if the woman said to her husband 'I want to go out so that I become divorced', and the husband said 'yes', and she went out, she is divorced because the husband's speech is for threat, not permission. And if he said to her 'By Allah, if you go out, may Allah disgrace you' and the like, Muhammad said: It is not permission. And also if the woman became angry and prepared to go out, and the husband said 'Leave her, let her go out', it is not permission unless he intends permission. And also if the husband said in his anger 'Go out' intending threat and warning, meaning 'go out until you become divorced', that is not permission. And if he said to his wife 'If you go out from this house, you are divorced', and she went out before the husband said 'divorced', he does not break the oath until she goes out another time, unless the oath was initially due to hostility between them regarding going out, then whenever it is like that, he does not break the oath even if she goes out afterwards because the oath was on the first exit. All from al-Zahiriyya.
Go out.
And in al-Mubtagha [with dotted 'ghain'], and in his statement to her 'If you go out from the house except with my permission, you are divorced', she does not break the oath by her going out if the occurrence is drowning or burning prevalent in it. And similarly in al-Qunya. End quote. In al-Qunya: If
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling on oaths concerning a wife's exit from the house. The author, Imam Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, expounds on the matn's statement: 'Do not go out except with my permission' requires permission for each exit, unlike 'except that I permit' or 'until I permit' which only need one permission.

The key distinction: In the first expression (لا تخرجي إلا بإذني), the excepted item (al-mustathna) is an exit linked to permission, so any exit not accompanied by permission falls under the oath, causing a breach. In the second (لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك or حتى آذن لك), the permission acts as a ghayah (limit/goal); once permission is given once, the sworn matter ends, and subsequent exits without permission do not break the oath.

The author addresses a potential objection: the Quranic verse (33:53) 'لا تدخلوا بيوت النبي إلا أن يؤذن لكم' uses the phrase 'except that you be permitted', which might suggest repeated permission. He explains that this is due to an external reason: the annoyance caused to the Prophet, peace be upon him, which mandated repetition, not the literal wording. In normal oaths, 'illa an ta'dhana' is interpreted metaphorically as 'hatta ta'dhana' (until you permit), requiring only one permission.

The passage then discusses the husband's intention: if he intended only one permission, he is not believed in court but is believed before Allah (religiously). This is because the apparent wording implies repeated permission, and his intention contradicts it. However, if he intends multiplicity, he is believed judicially as it is a probable meaning and imposes severity on himself.

Further sub-rulings: Similar expressions like 'except with permission' and 'without my permission' are treated the same. Knowledge and hearing are necessary for permission according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, or else it is invalid. Various scenarios illustrate when going out breaks the oath: sweeping the doorstep, giving charity to a beggar outside, etc. The passage emphasizes that the context and intention matter—threats or anger do not constitute genuine permission.

Finally, the text notes that if the oath was prompted by hostility between spouses, only the first exit after the oath triggers a breach; subsequent exits without permission do not because the oath was on that specific first exit.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or using His attributes, binding the swearer to do or not do something.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by the husband to the wife to exit the house, which can be verbal or implicit.
الحنث (al-hinth): Breach of an oath by doing the sworn-against action without valid excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'Do not go out except that I permit you,' and he permits her once, does she break the oath if she goes out later without permission?
A) Yes, she breaks the oath because permission is required each time.
B) No, she does not break the oath because the permission acted as a limit ending the oath.
C) Yes, she breaks the oath because the wording is similar to 'except with my permission'.
D) No, she does not break the oath, but she is still sinful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the phrasing 'except with my permission' imposes a repetitive condition, whereas 'except that I permit' or 'until I permit' imposes a one-time limit.
