The Requirement of Hearing for Valid Permission in Hanafi Oaths
Explaining the requirement of hearing for valid permission in Hanafi oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the wife to hear the permission for it to be valid, and evaluate the contrary position of Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

Core Idea
Permission is a delivery of information; without hearing, it is incomplete.

In Hanafi oath law, a recurring dispute is whether the husband's verbal permission is valid if the wife does not hear it. This lesson examines the positions of the Hanafi imams and their proofs, focusing on the nature of permission as an informative act (ikhbar).

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
Permission is not valid without the wife's hearing (simaa') because permission is the delivery of information (ikhbar) about permission.
This is contrasted with the view of Abu Yusuf and Zufar.
2
Identify the dissenting position (Abu Yusuf and Zufar)
They consider permission valid even without hearing or knowledge, viewing it as a general authorization, not a communicative act.
This dispute is limited to permission (idhn), not command (amr).
3
Analyze the majority Hanafi proof
Permission is analogous to a slave's permission to trade, which unanimously requires hearing. Since permission entails informing (ikhbar), it must be heard by the addressee.
The text states: 'وأجمعوا أن إذن العبد في التجارة لا يكون إلا بالسماع'
4
Examine the opposing proof
Abu Yusuf and Zufar argue that permission is like a general authorization and does not require immediate awareness; the wife may discover it later.
They do not differentiate between permission and command in terms of hearing requirement.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the husband permits while the wife is asleep or absent and she later goes out, according to the majority she breaks the oath; according to the minority she does not.
The mufta bihi is the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Source Text

قوله : (لا تخرجي إلا بإذني شرط لكل خروج إذن بخلاف إلا أن وحتى) أي بخلاف لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك أو حتى أن أذن لك فأذن لها مرة انتهت اليمين حتى لو خرجت بإذنه ثم خرجت بعده بغير إذنه لا يحنث والفرق في الأول أن المستثنى خروج مقرون بالإذن لأنه مفرغ للمتعلق فصار المعنى إلا خروجاً ملصقاً به فما لم يكن ملصقاً بالإذن فهو داخل في اليمين لعموم النكرة فيحنث به وفي الثاني الإذن غاية، أما في «حتى» فظاهر، وأما في «إلا أن فتجوز بـ«إلا فيها لتعذر استثناء الإذن من الخروج وبالمرة يتحقق فينتهي المحلوف عليه . وأما لزوم تكرار الإذن في دخول بيوته عليه السلام مع تلك الصيغة إلا أن يؤذن لكم [الأحزاب: ٥٣] فبدليل خارجي وهو تعليله بالأذى إن ذلكـم كـان يؤذي النبي [الأحزاب: ٥٣] وتمامه في الأصول في بحث الباء ولا يرد أن إلا أن آذن» بمعنى «إلا بإذني لأن «إن» والفعل في تأويل المصدر ولا بد من تقدير الباء وإلا صار المعنى إلا خروجاً إذني فصار كالمسألة الأولى لأنه يلزم أحد الأمرين. أما ما ذكر من تقدير الباء محذوفة أو ما قلنا من جعلها بمعنى «حتى» مجازاً أي حتى آذن لك. وعلى الأول يكون كالأول، وعلى الثاني ينعقد على إذن واحد ، وإذا لزم في إلا أن أحد المجازين وجب الراجح منهما، ومجاز غير الحذف أولى . من مجاز الحذف عندهم لأنه تصرف في وصف نفس اللفظ ومجاز الحذف تصرف قوله : (يلزم أحد الأمرين) علة لقوله «ولا يرد».
في ذاته بالإعدام مع الإرادة. وأشار المصنف بقوله «شرط أنه لو نوى الإذن مرة واحدة لم يصدق قضاء وعليه الفتوى كما في الولوالجية، لكنه يصدق ديانة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه فيستعار بمعنى «حتى» لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي بخلاف ما إذا نوى التعدد في المسألة الثانية حيث يصدق قضاء لأنه محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد على نفسه. ومثل قوله إلا بإذن بغير إذني فيشترط لكل خروج إذن لأن المعنى فيهما واحد مع وجود الباء، والرضا والأمر والعلم كالإذن فيما ذكرنا، كذلك إن خرجت إلا بقناع أو بملحفة ولو قال لها أذنت لك في الخروج كلما أردت فخرجت مرة بعد أخرى لا يحنث فإن نهاها عن الخروج بعد ذلك صح النهي وهذا قول محمد وبه أخذ الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل. ولو أذن لها في الخروج ثم قال لها كلما نهيتك فقد أذنت لك فنهاها لا يصح نهيه إياها، ولو أذن لها بالعربية ولا عهد لها بالعربية فخرجت حنث كما لو أذن لها وهي نائمة أو غائبة لم تسمع فخرجت حنث . وقال بعضهم : هذا قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد. أما على قول أبي يوسف وزفر يكون إذناً . وقال بعضهم : الإذن يصح بدون العلم والسماع في قولهم وإنما الخلاف بينهم في الأمر على قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يثبت الأمر بدون العلم والسماع والصحيح أن على قولهما لا يكون الإذن إلا بالسماع لأن الإذن إيقاع الخبر في الإذن وذلك لا يكون إلا بالسماع، وأجمعوا أن إذن العبد في التجارة لا يكون إلا بالسماع ولو كنست البيت هذه المرأة فخرجت إلى باب الدار لكنس الباب حنث لأنها خرجت بغير إذنه، ولو أذن لها في الخروج إلى بعض أهلها فلم تخرج ثم خرجت في وقت آخر إلى بعض أمنها . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : أخاف أن يحنث، ولو أن المرأة سمعت سائلاً يسأل شيئاً بعد ما منعها زوجها عن الخروج إلا بإذنه فقال لها الزوج ادفعي هذه الكسرة إليه، فإن كان السائل بحيث لا تقدر المرأة على الدفع إليه إلا بالخروج فخرجت لا يحنث وإلا فيحنث ولو قالت لزوجها تريد أن أخرج حتى أصير مطلقة فقال الزوج نعم فخرجت طلقت لأن كلام الزوج هذا للتهديد لا للإذن، ولو قال لها أما والله لو خرجت ليخزينك الله تعالى ونحو ذلك قال محمد: لا يكون إذناً. وكذا او غضبت المرأة وتأهبت للخروج فقال الزوج دعوها تخرج لم يكن إذناً إلا أن ينوي الإذن . وكذا لو قال الزوج في غضبه أخرجي ينوي التهديد والتوعيد يعني أخرجي حتى تطلقي لم يكن ذلك إذناً. ولو قال لامرأته إن خرجت من هذه الدار فأنت طالق فخرجت قبل أن يقول الزوج طالق لم يحنث حتى تخرج مرة أخرى إلا أن يكون ابتداء اليمين مخاشنة كانت بينهما في الخروج فمتى كانت كذلك لا يحنث وإن خرجت بعد ذلك لأن اليمين كانت على الخروج الأول؛ His statement: (Do not go out except with my permission is a condition for every exit requiring permission, unlike 'except that I permit' and 'until') meaning unlike 'do not go out except that I permit you' or 'until I permit you', so if he permits her once the oath ends, to the extent that if she goes out with his permission and then goes out afterwards without his permission she does not break the oath. The difference in the first is that the excepted thing is an exit accompanied by permission, because it is an empty [construction] attached to that which is connected, so the meaning becomes 'except an exit attached to it', so whatever is not attached to permission enters the oath due to the generality of the indefinite noun, so she breaks the oath by it. In the second, permission is a limit. As for 'until', it is apparent. As for 'except that', it is interpreted metaphorically with 'except' due to impossibility of excepting permission from exit, and with the occurrence the limit is realized so the sworn matter ends. As for the necessity of repeating permission in the entry into his houses - peace be upon him - with that formula 'except that you be permitted' [Al-Ahzab 33:53], it is due to an external evidence, which is its explanation by annoyance: 'that used to annoy the Prophet' [Al-Ahzab 33:53], and its completion is in the principles in the discussion of the preposition 'ba'. It cannot be objected that 'except that I permit' is in the meaning of 'except with my permission', because 'in' and the verb are in the interpretation of a verbal noun, and the preposition 'ba' must be estimated, otherwise the meaning becomes 'except a permission of mine' so it becomes like the first case because one of two things is necessitated. Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling on oaths concerning a wife's exit from the house. The author, Imam Zayn al-Din ibn Nujaym, expounds on the matn's statement: 'Do not go out except with my permission' requires permission for each exit, unlike 'except that I permit' or 'until I permit' which only need one permission.

The key distinction: In the first expression (لا تخرجي إلا بإذني), the excepted item (al-mustathna) is an exit linked to permission, so any exit not accompanied by permission falls under the oath, causing a breach. In the second (لا تخرجي إلا أن آذن لك or حتى آذن لك), the permission acts as a ghayah (limit/goal); once permission is given once, the sworn matter ends, and subsequent exits without permission do not break the oath.

The author addresses a potential objection: the Quranic verse (33:53) 'لا تدخلوا بيوت النبي إلا أن يؤذن لكم' uses the phrase 'except that you be permitted', which might suggest repeated permission. He explains that this is due to an external reason: the annoyance caused to the Prophet, peace be upon him, which mandated repetition, not the literal wording. In normal oaths, 'illa an ta'dhana' is interpreted metaphorically as 'hatta ta'dhana' (until you permit), requiring only one permission.

The passage then discusses the husband's intention: if he intended only one permission, he is not believed in court but is believed before Allah (religiously). This is because the apparent wording implies repeated permission, and his intention contradicts it. However, if he intends multiplicity, he is believed judicially as it is a probable meaning and imposes severity on himself.

Further sub-rulings: Similar expressions like 'except with permission' and 'without my permission' are treated the same. Knowledge and hearing are necessary for permission according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, or else it is invalid. Various scenarios illustrate when going out breaks the oath: sweeping the doorstep, giving charity to a beggar outside, etc. The passage emphasizes that the context and intention matter—threats or anger do not constitute genuine permission.

Finally, the text notes that if the oath was prompted by hostility between spouses, only the first exit after the oath triggers a breach; subsequent exits without permission do not because the oath was on that specific first exit.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or using His attributes, binding the swearer to do or not do something.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by the husband to the wife to exit the house, which can be verbal or implicit.
الحنث (al-hinth): Breach of an oath by doing the sworn-against action without valid excuse.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is permission valid if the wife does not hear it?
A) Yes, even without hearing.
B) No, it is invalid.
C) Yes, but only if she later learns of it.
D) No, but she is excused from expiation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, permission (idhn) is an informative act that requires the recipient's hearing to be legally effective; the mufta bihi is that permission without hearing is invalid.

Oath Requiring Permission for Each Exit: Principle and Application
Expands ruling on repetition of permission in oaths of exit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repetition of permission in oaths of exit, explain the internal disagreement regarding hearing, and apply the rule to various scenarios.

Core Idea
Each exit requires its own permission unless the oath explicitly allows repeated exits.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the far' (branch) of oaths concerning a wife's exit. The central question: if a husband swears that his wife may only exit with his permission, does one permission suffice for all future exits, or must each exit be separately permitted? The Hanafi school, with internal differences, provides a nuanced answer based on literal interpretation and the husband's intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The husband swears that his wife may not exit without his permission. This creates a conditional oath, often involving a suspended divorce.
The act is the wife's exit; the oath is the husband's statement.
2
State the default ruling
The default is that each exit requires a new permission. The apparent meaning of the oath indicates a condition attached to each instance of exit.
This is the relied-upon position according to the fatwa.
3
Analyze the effect of intention
If the husband intended one permission only, he is not believed in court (qada'an) but is believed religiously (diyanatan) because his wording is ambiguous. The court follows the apparent meaning.
The fatwa is that he is not believed in court.
4
Note the internal disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that permission requires hearing and knowledge to be valid. Abu Yusuf and Zufar consider it valid without hearing. The correct view is that hearing is required, as for a slave's trade permission.
The text sides with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Apply to examples
Work through scenarios: permission in Arabic to a non-Arabic speaker, permission while asleep, sweeping the door, giving to a beggar, threats, etc.
Each example tests the boundaries of what constitutes permission.

Source Text

شرط أنه لو نوى الإذن مرة واحدة لم يصدق قضاء وعليه الفتوى كما في الولوالجية، لكنه يصدق ديانة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه فيستعار بمعنى «حتى» لكنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يصدقه القاضي بخلاف ما إذا نوى التعدد في المسألة الثانية حيث يصدق قضاء لأنه محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد على نفسه. ومثل قوله إلا بإذن بغير إذني فيشترط لكل خروج إذن لأن المعنى فيهما واحد مع وجود الباء، والرضا والأمر والعلم كالإذن فيما ذكرنا، كذلك إن خرجت إلا بقناع أو بملحفة ولو قال لها أذنت لك في الخروج كلما أردت فخرجت مرة بعد أخرى لا يحنث فإن نهاها عن الخروج بعد ذلك صح النهي وهذا قول محمد وبه أخذ الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل. ولو أذن لها في الخروج ثم قال لها كلما نهيتك فقد أذنت لك فنهاها لا يصح نهيه إياها، ولو أذن لها بالعربية ولا عهد لها بالعربية فخرجت حنث كما لو أذن لها وهي نائمة أو غائبة لم تسمع فخرجت حنث . وقال بعضهم : هذا قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد. أما على قول أبي يوسف وزفر يكون إذناً . وقال بعضهم : الإذن يصح بدون العلم والسماع في قولهم وإنما الخلاف بينهم في الأمر على قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يثبت الأمر بدون العلم والسماع والصحيح أن على قولهما لا يكون الإذن إلا بالسماع لأن الإذن إيقاع الخبر في الإذن وذلك لا يكون إلا بالسماع، وأجمعوا أن إذن العبد في التجارة لا يكون إلا بالسماع ولو كنست البيت هذه المرأة فخرجت إلى باب الدار لكنس الباب حنث لأنها خرجت بغير إذنه، ولو أذن لها في الخروج إلى بعض أهلها فلم تخرج ثم خرجت في وقت آخر إلى بعض أمنها . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : أخاف أن يحنث، ولو أن المرأة سمعت سائلاً يسأل شيئاً بعد ما منعها زوجها عن الخروج إلا بإذنه فقال لها الزوج ادفعي هذه الكسرة إليه، فإن كان السائل بحيث لا تقدر المرأة على الدفع إليه إلا بالخروج فخرجت لا يحنث وإلا فيحنث ولو قالت لزوجها تريد أن أخرج حتى أصير مطلقة فقال الزوج نعم فخرجت طلقت لأن كلام الزوج هذا للتهديد لا للإذن، ولو قال لها أما والله لو خرجت ليخزينك الله تعالى ونحو ذلك قال محمد: لا يكون إذناً. وكذا او غضبت المرأة وتأهبت للخروج فقال الزوج دعوها تخرج لم يكن إذناً إلا أن ينوي الإذن . وكذا لو قال الزوج في غضبه أخرجي ينوي التهديد والتوعيد يعني أخرجي حتى تطلقي لم يكن ذلك إذناً. ولو قال لامرأته إن خرجت من هذه الدار فأنت طالق فخرجت قبل أن يقول الزوج طالق لم يحنث حتى تخرج مرة أخرى إلا أن يكون ابتداء اليمين مخاشنة كانت بينهما في الخروج فمتى كانت كذلك لا يحنث وإن خرجت بعد ذلك لأن اليمين كانت على الخروج الأول ؛ الكل من الظهيرية .
Condition that if he intended permission once, he is not believed in court and the fatwa is upon that, as in al-Walwaljiyya, but he is believed religiously because he intended a possible meaning of his speech, so it is used metaphorically in the sense of 'until', but it is contrary to the apparent meaning, so the judge does not believe him, unlike if he intended repetition in the second issue where he is believed in court because it is a possible meaning of his speech and it involves hardship on himself. And similar to his saying 'except with permission' and 'without my permission', so each exit requires permission because the meaning is the same with the presence of the ba, and consent, command, and knowledge are like permission in what we mentioned. Similarly, if she goes out except with a veil or a cloak. And if he said to her 'I have permitted you to go out whenever you wish' and she went out repeatedly, he does not break the oath, and if he forbids her after that, the prohibition is valid. This is the saying of Muhammad, and by it the shaykh al-imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl took. And if he permitted her to go out then said to her 'whenever I forbid you I have permitted you' and he forbade her, his forbidding is not valid. And if he permitted her in Arabic and she has no knowledge of Arabic, she goes out and he breaks the oath, just as if he permitted her while she was asleep or absent and did not hear, she goes out and he breaks the oath. And some said: this is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for the saying of Abu Yusuf and Zufar, it is considered permission. And some said: permission is valid without knowledge and hearing in their saying, and the disagreement is only concerning command; according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, command is not established without knowledge and hearing, and the correct view is that according to them permission is only with hearing because permission is the occurrence of the report of permission, and that does not occur except with hearing. And they agreed that the permission of a slave for trade is only with hearing. And if this woman swept the house and went out to the door of the house to sweep the door, she breaks the oath because she went out without his permission. And if he permitted her to go out to some of her relatives and she did not go out, then she went out at another time to some of her trusted ones, al-Imam Abu al-Layth said: I fear that she breaks the oath. And if a woman heard a beggar asking for something after her husband forbade her from going out except with his permission, and her husband said to her 'give this piece to him', then if the beggar is such that the woman cannot give it except by going out, she goes out and does not break the oath, otherwise she breaks it. And if she said to her husband 'I want to go out so that I become divorced' and the husband said 'yes', she goes out and becomes divorced because the husband's speech is for threat, not permission. And if he said to her 'by Allah, if you go out, Allah will disgrace you' and similar, Muhammad said: it is not permission. Similarly, if the woman became angry and prepared to go out and the husband said 'let her go out', it is not permission unless he intends permission. Similarly, if the husband said in his anger 'go out' intending threat and warning, meaning 'go out so that you become divorced', that is not permission. And if he said to his wife 'if you go out of this house, you are divorced' and she went out before the husband said 'divorced', he does not break the oath until she goes out another time, unless the beginning of the oath was a quarrel between them about going out; if it was so, he does not break the oath even if she goes out after that because the oath was about the first exit. All from al-Zahira.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths about entering and exiting. The passage begins by stating the legal principle: if someone swears that his wife may only exit with his permission, the default ruling is that each exit requires a new permission. If the husband intended only one permission, he is not believed in court (qada'an) but is believed religiously (diyanatan) because his wording is ambiguous. The relied-upon fatwa is that he is not believed in court. This is contrasted with a second case (mas'ala thaniya) where he intends repetition; there he is believed in court because his wording allows that interpretation, and it imposes hardship on himself. The text then discusses equivalent phrases like 'except with permission' and 'without my permission' where the ba is present; both require a new permission for each exit. Consent (rida), command (amr), and knowledge ('ilm) are treated like permission. If he says 'I have permitted you to go out whenever you wish' and she goes out repeatedly, he does not break the oath, but he can subsequently forbid her, and that prohibition is valid — this is the view of Imam Muhammad. If he permits her once then says 'whenever I forbid you I have permitted you', his later forbidding is ineffective. Permission given in Arabic to a non-Arabic speaker is ineffective, so she breaks the oath if she goes out. Similarly, permission given while she is asleep or absent is ineffective. The text notes a difference: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that permission does not take effect without hearing, while Abu Yusuf and Zufar consider it effective without hearing. The correct view is that hearing is required, and they all agree about slaves: permission for trade requires hearing. The passage then gives numerous practical examples: if she sweeps the house and goes out to sweep the door, she breaks the oath; if she is told to give something to a beggar and can only do so by going out, she does not break the oath; a husband's words of threat or anger are not considered permission; if the oath is about a specific first exit and she goes out before the husband pronounces divorce, she does not break the oath until a subsequent exit, unless the oath was prompted by a quarrel. All these are taken from al-Zahira.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission by the husband for the wife to exit; can be explicit or implied by consent, command, or knowledge.
حنث (hanath): Breaking of the oath, incurring the consequence (e.g., divorce or kaffarah).
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath sworn by the husband regarding the wife's actions, often with a conditional divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa, if a husband swears 'You may not exit without my permission' and then says 'I intended permission only once', what is the ruling?
A) He is believed in court and each exit does not require a new permission.
B) He is not believed in court and each exit requires a new permission, but he is believed religiously.
C) He is not believed in court and each exit requires a new permission.
D) The oath is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default in oaths requiring permission is that each exit needs its own permission, and the husband's claim of intending one permission is not accepted in court.

Oaths of Exit and Permission: Key Rulings on Repetition, Delegation, and Valid Permission
Expansion on oaths of exit and permission with multiple scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on when a single permission suffices for multiple exits versus when each exit requires new permission, and apply these rules to scenarios involving wives, slaves, and other individuals.

Core Idea
An oath of exit is fulfilled by one violation; permission for a wife is not repeated without explicit wording, but for a man or speech, custom dictates non-repetition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq expands on various scenarios related to oaths of exit and conditional permission. It addresses situations where an oath is made by divorce or manumission, and how different formulations affect the need for repeated permission. The commentary distinguishes between wives and slaves versus other contexts, relying on custom and the wording of the oath. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on conditional oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main legal issue
The passage explores whether a single permission covers multiple exits or each exit requires a new permission. The key distinction is between oaths concerning wives/slaves and those concerning other matters like speaking to someone or a man's exit.
2
Examine the wife/slave cases
For a wife or slave, each exit after an initial permission requires a new permission unless the husband/master explicitly says 'I permit you to go out whenever you wish.' This is because the oath is dissolved by the first violation; subsequent exits are not covered by the original oath.
Refer to al-Zahira for the ruling that a single violation dissolves the oath.
3
Analyze delegated and conditional permissions
The passage discusses scenarios where the master delegates permission to another person or uses conditional language. It rules that delegation does not constitute valid permission unless the master himself permits. Conditional statements like 'if you sell your servant, I have permitted you' are considered a gamble (mukhatara) and invalid.
The reasoning is that such conditions do not clearly express permission for the specific exit.
4
Distinguish by custom and wording
For oaths about speaking to someone or a man's exit, custom dictates that permission is not repeated; one permission suffices for all instances. For a wife's exit, the opposite is true due to the different nature of the acts involved.
Fath al-Qadir is cited for this distinction based on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اخرجي . وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة وفي قوله لها إن خرجت من الدار إلا بإذني فأنت طالق لا يحنث بخروجها الوقوع غرق أو حرق غالب فيها وكذا في القنية ا هـ. وفي القنية : لو حلف لا يشرب خمراً بغير إذنها فأذنت له أن يشربها في دار كذا فشربها في غيرها حنث ا هـ . وفي باب آخر منها : إن دفعت شيئاً بغير إذني فأنت طالق فدفعت من مال نفسها بغير إذنه لم يقع ا هـ . وينبغي أن ينظر إلى السبب الداعي إلى اليمين كما لا يخفى. ثم اعلم أن في المسألة الأولى إذا كانت اليمين بالطلاق ثم خرجت بغير إذن ووقع الطلاق ثم خرجت مرة ثانية بغير إذن لا يقع شيء لانحلال اليمين بوجود الشرط، وليس فيها ما يدل على التكرار كما في الظهيرية. ولو أذن لها أن تخرج في المسألة الأولى عشرة أيام فدخلت وخرجت مراراً في العشرة لا يحنث ولا فرق في المسألة الأولى بين أن يكون المخاطب الزوجة أو العبد حتى لو قال المولى لعبده إن خرجت من هذه الدار إلا بإذني فأنت حر فإنه يشترط لكل خروج إذن، فلو قال له أطع فلاناً في جميع ما يأمرك به فأمره فلان بالخروج فخرج فالمولى حانث لوجود شرط الحنث وهو الخروج من غير إذن المولى لأن المولى لم يأذن له بالخروج وإنما أمره بطاعة فلان. وكذلك لو قال المولى لرجل ائذن له في الخروج فأذن له الرجل فخرج لأنه لم يأذن له بالخروج وإنما أمر فلاناً بالإذن، وكذلك لو قال له قل يا فلان مولاك قد أذن لك في الخروج فقال له فخرج فإن المولى حانث لأنه لم يأذن له وإنما أمر فلاناً بكذب، ولو قال المولى لعبده بعد يمينه ما أمرك به فلان فقد أمرتك به فأمره الرجل بالخروج فخرج فالمولى حانث لأن مقصود المولى من هذا أن لا يخرج إلى برضاه، فإذا قال ما أمرك به فلان فقد أمرتك به فهو لا يعلم أن فلاناً يأمره بالخروج والرضا بالشيء بدون العلم به لا يتصور فلم يعلم كون هذا الخروج مرضياً . به فلم يعلم كونه مستثنى فبقي تحت المستثنى منه ولو قال المولى للرجل قد أذنت له في الخروج فأخبر الرجل به العبد لم يحنث المولى ولو قال لامرأته إن خرجت إلا بإذني ثم قال لها إن بعت خادمك فقد أذنت لك لم يكن منه هذا إذناً لأنه مخاطرة؛ كذا في البدائع : وقيد بالزوجة والعبد لأنه لو قال لا أكلم فلاناً إلا يأذن فلان أو حتى يأذن أو إلا أن يأذن أو إلا أن يقدم فلان أو حتى يقدم أو قال الرجل في داره والله لا تخرج إلا بإذني فإنه لا يتكرر الإذن في هذا كله لأن قدوم فلان لا يتكرر عادة والإذن في الكلام يتناول كلما يوجد من الكلام بعد الإذن وكذا خروج الرجل مما لا يتكرر عادة بخلاف الإذن للزوجة فإنه لا يتناول إلا ذلك الخروج المأذون فيه لا كل خروج إلا بنص صريح فيه مثل أذنت لك أن تخرجي كلما أردت الخروج ونحوه ، فكان الاقتصار في هذا الوجود الصارف عن التكرار لا لأن العرف في الكل على التفصيل المذكور، كذا في فتح القدير .
Go out. And in al-Mubtagha with the letter ghain and in his saying to her 'if you go out of the house except with my permission, you are divorced', she does not break the oath by her going out if drowning or burning is prevalent in it, and likewise in al-Qunyah. End quote. In al-Qunyah: If he swore not to drink wine without her permission, and she permitted him to drink it in a certain house, and he drank it elsewhere, he breaks the oath. End quote. In another chapter of it: 'If you give anything without my permission, you are divorced', and she gave from her own wealth without his permission, divorce does not occur. End quote. And it is appropriate to consider the reason prompting the oath, as is not hidden. Then know that in the first issue, if the oath was by divorce and she went out without permission and divorce occurred, then she went out a second time without permission, nothing occurs because the oath is dissolved by the occurrence of the condition, and there is nothing in it indicating repetition, as in al-Zahira. And if he permitted her to go out in the first issue for ten days, and she entered and exited repeatedly within the ten, he does not break the oath. And there is no difference in the first issue between the addressee being the wife or the slave, so that if the master said to his slave 'if you go out of this house except with my permission, you are free', then each exit requires permission. If he said to him 'obey so-and-so in everything he commands you', and so-and-so commanded him to go out, and he went out, the master breaks the oath because the condition for breaking is present: going out without the master's permission, because the master did not permit him to go out but rather commanded him to obey so-and-so. Similarly, if the master said to a man 'give him permission to go out' and the man permitted him, and he went out, [the master breaks the oath] because he did not permit him to go out but rather commanded the man to give permission. Similarly, if he said to him 'say: O slave, your master has permitted you to go out' and he said it and he went out, the master breaks the oath because he did not permit him but rather commanded the man to lie. And if the master said to his slave after his oath 'whatever so-and-so commands you, I have commanded you thereby', then so-and-so commanded him to go out, and he went out, the master breaks the oath because the master's intent was that he should not go out with his consent. When he said 'whatever so-and-so commands you, I have commanded you thereby', he does not know that so-and-so will command him to go out, and consent to something without knowledge of it is inconceivable, so he did not know that this exit is consented to, thus it is not known to be excepted, so it remains under the exception. And if the master said to the man 'I have permitted him to go out' and the man informed the slave, the master does not break the oath. And if he said to his wife 'if you go out except with my permission', then said to her 'if you sell your servant, I have permitted you', this is not from him permission because it is a gamble; thus in al-Bada'i'. And it is restricted to the wife and slave because if he said 'I will not speak to so-and-so except with so-and-so's permission' or 'until so-and-so permits' or 'except that so-and-so permits' or 'except that so-and-so comes' or 'until he comes', or a man said in his house 'by Allah, you will not go out except with my permission', then permission is not repeated in all this because the coming of so-and-so is not usually repeated, and permission in speech encompasses each instance of speech after permission, and likewise the exit of a man is not usually repeated, unlike the permission for the wife, which encompasses only that permitted exit, not every exit, except with an explicit text such as 'I have permitted you to go out whenever you wish to go out' and similar. So the restriction [to wife and slave] is due to the presence of a factor diverting from repetition, not because the custom in all is according to the mentioned detail. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on oaths of exit, beginning with a marginal note from al-Mubtagha about an oath where the wife is told 'if you go out except with my permission, you are divorced' and the exit is in a context where drowning or burning is prevalent — in such a case, she does not break the oath because the exit is coerced. Then from al-Qunyah, examples about drinking wine and giving gifts are cited: if a man swears not to drink wine without his wife's permission and she permits him in a specific house, but he drinks elsewhere, he breaks the oath; if a woman swears not to give anything without her husband's permission and she gives from her own wealth, divorce does not occur because her wealth is not subject to his permission. The passage emphasizes that the reason behind the oath should be considered. It then revisits the first issue: if the oath was by divorce and she goes out once without permission, divorce occurs, and a second exit does not cause another divorce because the oath is already fulfilled. Permission for ten days allows repeated exits within that period without breaking the oath. The ruling applies equally to wives and slaves. Several scenarios involving a master and his slave are analyzed: if the master tells the slave to obey someone, that does not constitute permission for exit unless the master explicitly permits. Similarly, delegating permission to another person does not count as the master's own permission, and if the master instructs someone to inform the slave of permission, it is effective if the master actually permitted. The passage concludes by distinguishing between oaths about a wife's exit (which require repetition) and oaths about other things like speaking to someone or a man's exit, where repetition is not required due to custom. This distinction is from Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
مخاطرة (mukhatara): A gamble or risky transaction; here, a conditional statement that does not constitute valid permission because it is speculative.
الظهيرية (al-Zahira): A Hanafi legal text; referenced as the source for the ruling that a single violation dissolves the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to his wife 'if you go out except with my permission, you are divorced' and she goes out in a situation where drowning is common, what is the ruling?
A) She breaks the oath and divorce occurs.
B) She does not break the oath because the exit is due to necessity.
C) She breaks the oath only if she intended to do so.
D) The oath is invalid due to the danger.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths of exit require careful attention to the addressee: a wife or slave needs separate permission for each exit, while for other matters like speech or a man's exit, custom dictates that permission does not repeat.

Two Phrasings of Exception in Oaths: Temporal vs. Attributive
Distinguishes between two formulations of exception in oaths of entry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two formulations of exception in oaths of entry and apply the Hanafi ruling to determine whether the oath is dissolved or remains active.

Core Idea
The phrasing of the exception determines whether the oath ends upon forgetful entry or continues to bind for intentional entries.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths. The author distinguishes between two similar phrasings of exception for forgetfulness, showing that a small grammatical difference leads to opposite legal consequences. Understanding this distinction deepens your grasp of how intent and linguistic construction interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Phrasings
Identify the two phrasings from the text: 'except that I forget' (illa an yansa) and 'except forgetfully' (illa nasyan).
Both use the particle 'illa' but differ in grammatical construction.
2
Analyze the First Phrasing
Interpret 'except that I forget' as a temporal exception equivalent to 'until I forget' (bi-ma'na hatta). A forgetful entry ends the oath.
The oath is dissolved after the first forgetful entry; subsequent intentional entries do not violate it.
3
Analyze the Second Phrasing
Interpret 'except forgetfully' as an attributive exception, excluding forgetful entries from the oath's scope. The oath remains active for all non-excluded entries.
A forgetful entry is not a violation, but the oath still binds for intentional entries.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Given a scenario, determine which phrasing was used and then decide whether a subsequent intentional entry triggers the oath's consequence.
Scenario: 'You are free if I enter this house except that I forget' vs. 'except forgetfully'.
5
Compare Outcomes
Contrast the two rulings: first phrasing ends the oath, so no penalty for later intentional entry; second phrasing keeps the oath active, so later intentional entry breaks it.
The Hukm (ruling) hinges on the linguistic form of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف بالمسألة الثانية إلى أنه لو قال عبده حر إن دخل هذه الدار إلا أن ينسى فدخلها ناسياً ثم دخل بعد ذلك ذاكراً لم يحنث بخلاف ما إذا قال إن دخل هذه الدار إلا ناسياً فدخلها ناسياً ثم دخلها ذاكراً فإنه يحنث لأنه استثنى من كل دخول دخولاً بصفة فبقي ما سواه داخلاً تحت اليمين بخلاف الأول فإنه بمعنى «حتى» فلما دخلها ناسياً انتهت اليمين،
And the author indicated by the second issue that if he said to his slave 'you are free if I enter this house except that I forget', and he entered it forgetfully, then entered it afterwards intentionally, he does not break the oath, contrary to if he said 'if I enter this house except forgetfully', and he entered it forgetfully, then entered it intentionally, then he breaks the oath, because he excepted from every entry an entry with a description, so what remains is included under the oath, unlike the first, which is in the meaning of 'until', so when he entered it forgetfully the oath ended.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage addresses a second distinct issue (mas'ala thaniya) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, focusing on oaths involving an exception for forgetfulness. The author (al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, which al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on) explains the difference between two phrasings. First: if a person says to his slave 'you are free if I enter this house except that I forget', the phrasing 'except that I forget' is understood as 'until I forget' (bi-ma'na hatta). Thus, the first forgetful entry fulfills the oath and dissolves it; any subsequent intentional entry does not cause a violation. Second: if the phrasing is 'if I enter this house except forgetfully' (ista'thna nasyan), the exception is a description attached to each entry, meaning 'every entry except a forgetful one'. So a forgetful entry is excluded from the oath, but the oath remains active for all other entries. Therefore, if he later enters intentionally, he breaks the oath. The key difference lies in the grammatical construction: 'illa an yansa' (except that he forgets) is a temporal exception equivalent to 'until', while 'illa nasyan' (except forgetfully) is an attributive exception that excludes a specific type of entry. This distinction is crucial for determining whether the oath is dissolved after one forgetful act or continues to apply.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception in an oath; the word 'illa' (except) used to exclude a specific circumstance.
حتى (hatta): A particle meaning 'until' or 'for the purpose of'; here, the exception is interpreted as a temporal limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his slave: 'You are free if I enter this house except forgetfully.' He enters forgetfully and later intentionally. What is the ruling?
A) The slave is freed upon the first forgetful entry.
B) The slave is not freed; the oath is broken upon intentional entry.
C) The oath is invalid because the exception is unclear.
D) The slave is freed only if the master intended to free him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between temporal and attributive exceptions in oaths, leading to different rulings on when the oath ends upon a forgetful act.

Oath Interpreted by Context: Fawran vs Abad
Explaining how oaths are limited by immediate context and intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between oaths that are immediate (fawran) and perpetual (abad), and apply contextual and intentional factors to determine whether an oath is violated.

Core Idea
An oath's wording is not always decisive; context and intention can restrict a general oath to a specific situation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the Hanafi approach to oaths, emphasizing that the literal wording must be understood in light of the circumstances and the speaker's intent. You'll learn how a simple response like "If I eat lunch" can be limited to the immediate invitation, and why additions or different conjunctions change the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oaths
Distinguish between oaths that are perpetual in wording but temporary in meaning, and those based on a current matter. The passage gives examples of each.
The first type is restricted by the situation; the second is built on a current matter.
2
Apply the principle of response matching the question
Analyze the example of the invitation to eat: the oath is a response to a specific request, so it must be limited to that request to achieve congruence (mutabaqah).
If additional wording is added, the oath becomes general and no longer a pure response.
3
Analyze how conjunctions affect immediacy vs. perpetuity
Study how the conjunctions fa (then), wa (and), and thumma (then) indicate whether the second action is expected immediately or can be delayed. For example, fa indicates immediacy if the second action is customarily immediate.
Thumma indicates delay, making the oath perpetual.
4
Understand the role of intention (niyyah)
When there is no clear context, the swearer's intention is considered. If he intends immediacy or perpetuity, that may override the apparent meaning, provided it does not contradict explicit wording.
Intention is more readily accepted when it aligns with contextual clues.
5
Recognize the dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i
Contrast the Hanafi position with the opposing view that takes the literal wording at face value. Understand the Hanafi response that contextual indication (dalalah haliyyah) is valid evidence.
The Hanafi tarjih relies on the principle of response correspondence and is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فخرجت هي أيضاً وأتت دار والده بعدما أتاها الزوج لا يحنث لأن رجوع المرأة وجلوسها ما دامت في تهيؤ الخروج لا يكون تركاً للفور، ألا ترى أنه لو أخذها البول فبالت قبل لبس الثياب ثم لبست الثياب لم يحنث ألا لأن الصلاة عمل آخر فينقطع به فور الأول. وعلى قياس الحسن بن زياد لا يحنث وعليه الفتوى ولو اشتغلت بالوضوء للصلاة المكتوبة أو اشتغلت بالصلاة المكتوبة لا يحنث لأنه عذر شرعاً فصار مستثنى من يمينه شرعاً وعرفاً، ولو اشتغلت بالتطوع أو بالوضوء أو أكلت أو شربت حنث لأن هذا ليس بعذر شرعاً .اهـ. وفي القنية : قال لها في الخصومة الحلال علي حرام إن لم تخرجي وقال ما أردت به الخروج للحال ثم خرجت بعد ساعات يحنث إن كانت الخصومة في الخروج وإلا فلا وفي الجامع : لو قال لها إن لم أضربك فأنت طالق فهي على أربعة أقسام : فإن كان فيه دلالة الفور بأن قصد ضربها فمنع انصرف إلى الفور، وإن نوى الفور بدون الدلالة يصدق أيضاً لأن فيه تغليظاً، وإن نوى الأبد أو لم تكن له نية انصرف الأبد، وإن نوى اليوم أو الغد لم تقبل نيته . ولو قال لها إن أخذت من مالي شيئاً ولم تخبريني فكذا فأخذت ولم تخبره في الحال ولا قبله وإنما أخبرته بعد أيام لا يحنث إن رأيت سارقاً فلم أخبرك فهو على الفور، وإن قال ولم أخبرك وإن لم أخبرك فعلى التراخي ولا بد من الشرطين. اهـ. ما في القنية .
And no one disagreed with him after that, for all people are dependent on Abu Hanifa in this. Rather, all people are dependent on Abu Hanifa in all of fiqh. And it is an oath perpetual in wording but temporary in meaning, restricted by the situation or based on a current matter. From the second [type]: a woman who prepared to go out, and he swore that she would not go out, then she sat for an hour and then went out – he does not violate the oath because his intent was to prevent her from the departure for which she had prepared, as if he said: if you go out now. And from it: one who wanted to strike his slave and swore that he would not strike him, then left him for an hour so that the immediacy passed, then struck him – he does not violate the oath for the same reason. From the first [type]: Sit and eat lunch with me, and he says: If I eat lunch, my slave is free – it is restricted by the situation, so if he eats lunch at home on that day, he does not violate the oath because the oath occurred as a response embodying what was in the question, and the requested lunch is the current one, so the oath refers to the current lunch to achieve congruence. And all this is when the swearer has no intention. It is conditioned that he said 'If I eat lunch' and did not add to it, because if he added by saying 'If I eat lunch today' or 'with you' then his slave is free, and he eats at home or at another time, then he violates the oath because he added to the response particle, thus becoming an initiator. It cannot be said that Moses (peace be upon him) added in the response when asked about the staff and was not an initiator, for we say: when he was asked 'What is that?' which refers to a thing without reason and attributes, the situation was ambiguous, so he responded with both to answer whichever it was. And the author indicated that if he said to his wife when she was leaving the house: 'If you return to my house, you are thrice divorced,' then she sat and did not go out for a time, then went out and returned, and the man says 'I intended immediacy,' the apparent ruling is that he is believed, because if he said 'If you go out' with no intention, it refers to that specific departure; similarly, if he said 'If you return' and intended return after that departure, it is more appropriate that it refers to return from that departure. This is from al-Muhit. Then know that restriction is sometimes established explicitly and sometimes by indication, and indication is of two types: verbal indication and contextual indication. Verbal indication is like when he swears not to enter upon so-and-so, it is restricted by the life of the one sworn upon. Contextual indication is as in the book and in al-Muhit: its basis is that the swearer connected an action to another action with a conjunction letter, which is fa or wa. If the second action is normally done immediately after the first and he does not do it, he violates; if it is not normally done immediately after the first, he does not violate unless he dies. If he mentioned the second action with the letter of condition or delay, which is thumma, then it is perpetual, because the conditioned does not occur except after the condition exists, and thumma indicates delay. So if he says 'If you hit me, then I did not hit you,' or 'If I meet you, then I did not greet you,' or 'If you speak to me, then I did not answer you,' it is immediate according to custom. Similarly if he says 'If I borrow your animal and you do not lend it to me,' or 'If you enter the house and I did not sit.' And if he mentions with the letter wa by saying 'If I speak to you and you do not speak to me,' this could be before or after, so his intention is considered. And if he says 'If I ride my animal and I do not give you my animal,' it is immediate. And if he says 'If you come to me and I do not come to you,' or 'If you visit me and I do not visit you,' it is perpetual, to the end of what he mentioned. Then he said: If he said to his wife 'If you do not get up now and come to my father's house, you are thrice divorced,' and she got up immediately, put on clothes, and went out, then returned and sat until the husband went out, then she also went out and came to his father's house after the husband had come – she does not violate, because the woman's return and sitting as long as she is in the preparation of going out is not a abandonment of immediacy. Do you not see that if she was seized by the urge to urinate and urinated before putting on clothes then put on clothes, she does not violate? Do you not see that if a man says to his wife 'If you do not come to bed this hour, you are divorced' while they are quarrelling, and it takes long between them, it is immediate, so that if she goes to bed, he does not violate? If she fears missing the prayer and prays, Nasr ibn Yahya said: the man violates because prayer is another action that cuts off the immediacy of the first. According to the analogy of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, he does not violate, and the fatwa is upon it. And if she occupies herself with ablution for the prescribed prayer or with the prescribed prayer itself, she does not violate because it is a legal excuse, so it becomes exempted from her oath legally and customarily. And if she occupies herself with supererogatory prayer, or with ablution (other than for prescribed prayer), or eats or drinks, she violates because this is not a legal excuse. End quote. In al-Qunyah: He said to her in a dispute 'What is lawful for me is forbidden if you do not go out,' and he said 'I intended the immediate going out,' then she went out after hours: he violates if the dispute was about going out, otherwise not. In al-Jami': If he said to her 'If I do not strike you, you are divorced,' it is of four types: if there is indication of immediacy, such as he intended to strike her and was prevented, it refers to immediacy; if he intended immediacy without such indication, he is believed because it is a severity; if he intended perpetuity or had no intention, it refers to perpetuity; if he intended a specific day or tomorrow, his intention is not accepted. And if he said to her 'If you take something from my wealth and do not inform me, then [it is such],' and she took and did not inform him immediately nor before, but only informed him after days, he does not violate. If he said 'If you see a thief and I do not inform you,' it is immediate; if he said 'and I did not inform you' or 'if I do not inform you,' it is delayed, and both conditions are necessary. End of what is in al-Qunyah.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi principle that oaths are often interpreted based on context and intention rather than literal wording alone. The author begins by stating that all scholars rely on Abu Hanifa in this matter. He then distinguishes between oaths that are perpetual in wording but temporary in meaning, meaning they are tied to a specific situation. Several examples illustrate this: a woman preparing to go out, a man intending to strike his slave, and an invitation to eat. In each case, the oath is limited to the immediate action. The passage also discusses how restrictions are established, either explicitly or by indication (verbal or contextual). It details how conjunctions like fa, wa, and thumma affect whether an oath is immediate or perpetual. There is a discussion of a dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who take the literal wording at face value. The Hanafi school argues that the oath as a response must match the question, so it is limited. Later examples cover oaths related to a wife's actions, including excuses like prayer and ablution. The passage concludes with rulings from al-Qunyah and al-Jami' on intention and immediate vs. delayed oaths.

Key Terms
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy; the oath is considered effective only for the immediate action.
الأبد (al-abad): Perpetuity; the oath applies indefinitely.
الدلالة الحالية (al-dilalah al-haliyyah): Contextual indication; understanding the meaning from the situation rather than explicit words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says 'If I eat lunch, my slave is free' in response to an invitation, and then eats lunch at home the next day, what is the ruling?
A) He violates the oath because the wording is general.
B) He does not violate the oath because it is limited to the offered meal.
C) He must free the slave anyway.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by context and intention; the literal wording is not absolute.

Oath on Riding a Slave's Animal: Conditions for Violation
Ruling on when an oath not to ride someone's animal applies to their slave's animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which riding a slave's animal violates an oath not to ride the master's animal.

Core Idea
A slave's animal is treated as the master's in oaths only if the swearer intended it and the master has no overwhelming debt.

When a person swears not to ride someone's animal, does riding that person's slave's animal break the oath? The Hanafi answer depends on two key factors: the intention of the swearer and the debt status of the master. This lesson unpacks the conditions laid out in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The oath is 'I will not ride so-and-so's animal'. The act in question is riding the animal of so-and-so's slave.
The animal is outwardly associated with the slave, but legally owned by the master.
2
Recognize the default attribution
Custom and law attribute the animal to the slave, not the master. Therefore, riding the slave's animal does not automatically violate the oath.
This default is the starting point.
3
Apply the two conditions
The oath is violated only if (1) the swearer intended to include the slave's animal, and (2) the master has no overwhelming debt that nullifies his effective ownership.
Both conditions must be met; if either is missing, no violation occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومركب عبده مركبه إن ينو ولا دين) يعني لو حلف لا يركب دابة فلان فركب دابة عبد فلان فإنه يحنث بشرطين : الأول أن ينويها الثاني أن لا يكون عليه دين أي مستغرق فإن لم ينو لا حنث مطلقاً لأن الملك وإن كان للمولى إلا أنه يضاف إلى العبد عرفاً وكذا شرعاً
His statement: (And the mount of his slave is his mount if he intends and there is no debt) means: if he swore not to ride so-and-so's animal, and then he rode the animal of so-and-so's slave, then he violates the oath under two conditions: first that he intends it, second that he has no overwhelming debt. If he does not intend, he does not violate at all, because ownership although belonging to the master is nevertheless attributed to the slave customarily and legally.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn: 'And the mount of his slave is his mount if he intends and there is no debt.' The meaning is that if a man swears not to ride a specific person's animal, and then rides that person's slave's animal, he violates the oath only if two conditions are met: (1) he intended to include the slave's animal in his oath, and (2) that person does not have an overwhelming debt that would render his ownership null in effect. If he did not intend inclusion, then no violation occurs because customarily and legally the animal is attributed to the slave, even though ownership remains with the master.

Key Terms
مركب (markab): A riding animal; mount.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): Overwhelming debt; debt that covers all of one's assets.
نية (niyyah): Intention; here it refers to the swearer's intention to include the slave's animal in the scope of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to ride Zayd's animal, and then rides the animal of Zayd's slave, when does he violate the oath?
A) Always, because the slave's animal belongs to the master.
B) Never, because the animal is attributed to the slave.
C) Only if he intended the slave's animal and Zayd has no overwhelming debt.
D) Only if Zayd has overwhelming debt.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, an oath not to ride someone's animal extends to their slave's animal only if the swearer intended it and the master has no overwhelming debt.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oaths Regarding Slave Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership and intention in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether an oath regarding a slave's property is broken when the slave's debt is overwhelming, and apply the same reasoning to the master's statement freeing his slave's slaves.

Core Idea
When a slave's debt overwhelms his property, the master's ownership is null, so an oath regarding that property is not broken even with intention.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute rooted in a hadith about selling a slave with property. The hadith indicates that the attribution of property to the master is defective, requiring intention for an oath to take effect. The three leading Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—differ on whether the master's ownership persists when the slave's debt is overwhelming. This difference then applies to analogous cases, such as the master's declaration of freeing all his slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is: If a master swears regarding property in the hands of his slave, and the slave's debt is so large that it consumes all the property (mustaghriq), does the master's oath break when he intentionally does the act?
The hadith cited makes the attribution defective, so intention normally repairs it.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that if the debt is overwhelming, the master has no ownership in the slave's earnings, so the oath is not broken even if he intended.
The defective attribution cannot be cured by intention because there is no ownership at all.
3
State the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf says the oath is broken if he intended ownership, because intention corrects the defective attribution. Muhammad says the oath is always broken regardless of intention, because the slave and his property are truly the master's property in reality.
Abu Yusuf focuses on intention; Muhammad focuses on the reality of ownership.
4
Apply the analogy to freeing slaves
The same disagreement applies if the master says 'All my slaves are free.' Regarding the slaves of his trading slave: Abu Yusuf requires intention, Muhammad frees them regardless, and Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the trading slave has debt.
This shows the principle extends to other verbal acts.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
The practical difference: In a case where the master swears on his slave's property and the slave has overwhelming debt, Abu Hanifa says the oath is not broken, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad would consider it broken under certain conditions.
This affects whether expiation (kaffarah) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الدخول والخروج لأن قال عليه السلام من باع عبداً وله مال الحديث فتختل الإضافة إلى المولى فلا بد من النية، فإن نواها ولا دين على العبد أو كان دينه غير مستغرق حنث لأنه شدد على نفسه بنيته، وإن كان الدين مستغرقاً فلا حنث وإن نوى لأنه لا ملك للمولى في كسب عبده المديون المستغرق عند أبي حنيفة وقال أبو يوسف : يحنث في الوجوه كلها إذا نوى الملك للمولى لكن الإضافة إليه قد اختلت لما ذكرنا فلا يدخل إلا بالنية . وقال محمد يحنث في الوجوه كلها نوى أو لم ينو اعتباراً للحقيقة لأن العبد وما في يده ملك المولى حقيقة ونظير هذا الاختلاف ما لو قال كل مملوك لي حر فعند أبي يوسف لا يدخل عبيد عبده التاجر إلا بالنية سواء كان على العبد دين أو لا، وعند محمد عتقوا نواهم أو لا، كان عليه دين أو لا . وعند أبي حنيفة إن لم يكن عليه دين عتقوا إذا نواهم وإلا فلا، وإن كان على العبد دين لم يعتقوا وإن نواهم وفي المحيط ولو ركب دابة مكاتبة لا يحنث لأن ملكه ليس بمضاف إلى المولى لا ذاتاً ولا يداً اهـ. ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله من مسائل الركوب غير هذه المسألة ولا بأس بذكر بعض مسائله قال في الواقعات : حلف لا يركب فاليمين على ما يركب الناس من الفرس والبغل وغير ذلك، فلو ركب ظهر إنسان ليعبر النهر لا يحنث لأن أوهام الناس لا تسبق إلى هذا اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : حلف أن لا يركب دابة ولم ينو شيئاً فركب حماراً أو فرساً أو برذوناً أو بغلاً حنث، فإن ركب غيرها نحو البعير والفيل لا يحنث استحساناً إلا أن ينوي ولو حلف لا يركب فرساً فركب برذوناً لا يحنث، وكذلك لو حلف لا يركب برذوناً فركب فرساً لأن الفرس اسم للعربي والبرذون للعجمي والخيل ينتظم الكل وهذا إذا كانت اليمين بالعربية وإن كانت بالفارسية يحنث بكل حال. ولو حلف لا يركب دابة فحمل على الدابة مكرهاً لا يحنث، وإن حلف لا يركب أو لا يركب مركباً فركب سفينة أو محملاً أو دابة حنث، ولو ركب آدمياً ينبغي أن لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يركب على هذا السرج فزيد فيه أو نقص عنه فركب عليه حنث اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : قال كلما ركبت دابة فلله علي أن أتصدق بها فركب دابة يلزمه التصدق بها، فإن تصدق بها ثم اشتراها فركب مرة أخرى لزمه التصدق بها مرة أخرى ثم وثم بخلاف مسألة التنجيز حيث يبطل التعليق، أما لو قال لأجنبية كلما تزوجتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً فتزوجها تطلق ثلاثاً، فلو تزوجت بآخر وعادت إليه فتزوجها تطلق ثلاثاً ثم وثم اهـ . والله أعلم. قوله : (فركب سفينة أو محملاً أو دابة حنث هذا بالنسبة إلى قوله وإن حلف لا يركب مخالف الما مر آنفاً عن الواقعات تأمل. وفي بعض الكتب الاقتصار على قوله لا يركب مركباً» وفي الخانية كما هنا .
Chapter of Oath regarding Entry and Exit. Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave who has property...' the hadith. Thus the attribution to the master becomes defective, so intention is necessary. If he intends it and the slave has no debt or his debt is not overwhelming, he breaks the oath because he imposed a hardship on himself by his intention. If the debt is overwhelming, he does not break the oath even if he intended, because according to Abu Hanifa the master has no ownership in the earnings of his indebted slave whose debt is overwhelming. Abu Yusuf said: He breaks the oath in all cases if he intended ownership for the master, but the attribution to him has become defective as we mentioned, so it only enters by intention. Muhammad said: He breaks the oath in all cases whether he intended or not, considering reality, because the slave and what he has in his hand are truly the property of the master. An analogy to this difference is if someone said 'All my slaves are free,' then according to Abu Yusuf the slaves of his trading slave do not enter except by intention, whether the slave has debt or not. According to Muhammad, they are freed whether he intended them or not, whether he had debt or not. According to Abu Hanifa, if there is no debt on the slave, they are freed if he intended them, otherwise not; if the slave has debt, they are not freed even if he intended them. In al-Muhit: If one rides the mount of a mukatab, he does not break his oath because his ownership is not attributed to the master, neither in essence nor in possession. The author did not mention aside from this issue any riding matters, but it is good to mention some of them. He said in al-Waqi'at: If someone swears not to ride, the oath applies to what people ride like horses, mules, etc. If he rides a person's back to cross a river, he does not break the oath because people's minds do not jump to this. In al-Zahiriyya: If he swears not to ride a beast and did not intend anything, and rode a donkey, horse, bardoon, or mule, he breaks his oath. If he rode something else like a camel or elephant, he does not break the oath by istihsan unless he intended. If he swore not to ride a horse and rode a bardoon, he does not break his oath. Similarly, if he swore not to ride a bardoon and rode a horse, because 'horse' is the name for the Arabian and 'bardoon' for the non-Arabian, and 'khayl' encompasses all. This applies if the oath is in Arabic; if it is in Persian, he breaks the oath in all cases. If he swore not to ride a beast and was forced to ride it, he does not break the oath. If he swore not to ride or not to ride a conveyance, and rode a ship, a camel-litter, or a beast, he breaks the oath. If he rode a human, it should be that he does not break his oath. If he swore not to ride on this saddle and then something was added or subtracted from it and he rode on it, he breaks his oath. In al-Khulasa: He said 'Whenever I ride a beast, I must give it in charity' and he rode a beast, he is obligated to give it in charity. If he gave it in charity and then bought it and rode again, he is obligated to give it in charity again, repeatedly, unlike the case of immediate effect where the conditional clause becomes void. However, if he said to a foreign woman 'Whenever I marry you, you are thrice divorced,' and he marries her, she is thrice divorced. If she marries another and returns to him, and he marries her, she is thrice divorced again, repeatedly. And Allah knows best. His statement: 'If he rode a ship, camel-litter, or beast, he breaks his oath'—this regarding his statement 'if he swore not to ride' contradicts what was mentioned earlier from al-Waqi'at? Ponder. In some books they restrict to his statement 'he did not ride a conveyance', and in al-Khaniya it is as here.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of a slave's overwhelming debt on the master's ownership, and extends the principle to rulings on oaths regarding riding. The author begins by citing a hadith about selling a slave with property, noting that the attribution of property to the master becomes defective, necessitating intention in oaths. Abu Hanifa holds that if the slave's debt is overwhelming (mustaghriq), the master has no ownership in the slave's earnings, so an oath about the slave's property is not broken. Abu Yusuf holds that it is broken if the master intended ownership, while Muhammad holds it is broken regardless of intention, as the slave and his property are truly the master's. This same difference applies to a master saying 'all my slaves are free' regarding the slaves of his trading slave. The text then mentions a related ruling: riding a mukatab's mount does not break an oath because the mukatab's ownership is not attributed to the master. After this, the author lists many detailed rulings on oaths related to riding, covering types of mounts, language of oath, coercion, and repeated charity vows. The commentary ends with a marginal note questioning a possible contradiction about riding a ship, etc. Overall, this is a classic fiqh discussion showing how the Hanafi school applies principles of ownership and intention to practical oath situations.

Key Terms
حنث (hanath): The breaking of an oath by committing the act sworn against, incurring sin and required expiation (kaffarah).
مستغرق (mustaghriq): A debt that completely covers the value of the slave's property, leaving no surplus for the master.
إضافة (idafah): Attribution of property to the master; defective (ikhtilal) means the property is not fully attributed to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when does a master break an oath regarding his slave's property if the slave has an overwhelming debt?
A) Always, regardless of intention.
B) Only if the master intended ownership.
C) Never, because ownership is absent.
D) Only if the slave has no debt.

Key Takeaway
The master's ownership is not absolute when the slave's debt overwhelms, so an oath regarding that property may not be broken even with intention, according to Abu Hanifa.

Detailed Rulings on Oaths Related to Riding
Detailed rulings on oaths related to riding: custom, language, coercion, and special cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on oaths not to ride, distinguishing between types of mounts, language of the oath, coercion, and repeated charity vows.

Core Idea
The scope of an oath not to ride depends on customary usage, language of the oath, and specific intentions.

This lesson covers a comprehensive set of rulings on oaths related to riding. The author expands from a single ruling about riding a mukatab's mount to numerous cases: what constitutes 'riding' for an oath, how language (Arabic vs. Persian) affects interpretation, the effect of coercion, and the special case of a conditional charity vow. Understanding these details is essential for a mufti dealing with oath-related inquiries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle for 'not riding' oaths
The oath applies to what people customarily ride: horses, mules, donkeys, etc. Uncommon things like riding a person's back do not break the oath.
Custom ('urf) is the basis.
2
Distinguish between types of mounts and language
If the oath uses specific Arabic terms like 'faras' (Arabian horse) or 'bardhūn' (non-Arabian horse), the ruling follows linguistic definitions. In Persian, all horses are included.
Language specificity matters.
3
Note the effect of coercion
If forced to ride, the oath is not broken, because intention is absent.
Coercion nullifies the voluntary act.
4
Apply the ruling to other conveyances and unusual acts
Riding a ship, camel-litter, or beast breaks the oath if the oath was general ('not ride' or 'not ride a conveyance'), but riding a human does not.
Dābbah (beast) includes these, but not humans.
5
Analyze the conditional charity vow
If someone swears to give a beast in charity every time he rides it, the obligation repeats each ride, unlike immediate charity.
This shows the distinction between conditional and unconditional vows.

Commentary

برذون (bardhūn): A non-Arabian horse or a common horse.
فرس (faras): An Arabian horse, specifically in Arabic.
دابة (dābbah): A riding beast, generally including horses, mules, donkeys, etc.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom.

According to the Hanafi school, if a man swears in Arabic 'I will not ride a faras' and then rides a bardhūn, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because both are horses.
B) No, because faras specifically means Arabian horse.

D) No, because the oath is defective.

Key Takeaway
Oaths about riding are interpreted according to customary usage and the specific language used; coercion prevents breaking the oath.

Defining Eating and Drinking in Oaths
Defining 'eating' and 'drinking' for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi legal definitions of eating (akl) and drinking (shurb) and apply them to determine whether an oath is broken in given scenarios.

Core Idea
Eating requires delivering a chewable substance to the stomach; drinking requires delivering a liquid. Swallowing whole breaks an oath, but tasting does not.

When someone swears not to eat or drink, what exactly counts? The Hanafi school provides precise definitions hinging on the nature of the substance and its delivery to the stomach. This lesson unpacks those definitions through examples from the classical text of al-Bahr al-Rā’iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act in Question
Determine exactly what the person did with the substance (e.g., swallowed whole, sucked juice, chewed and spat, mixed with water and drank).
The act must be classified as eating, drinking, or merely tasting.
2
Classify the Substance
Is the substance normally solid and chewable (like bread, meat, fruit) or is it a liquid that cannot be chewed (like water, milk, honey)? This determines whether the act falls under eating or drinking.
Customary usage ('urf) also plays a role – e.g., eating yogurt with bread is considered eating.
3
Check Delivery to the Stomach
Did the substance actually reach the stomach? If it was only placed in the mouth and then discarded, it is not eating or drinking – it is mere tasting (dhawq), which does not break the oath.
4
Apply the Definition
If the substance is chewable and reaches the stomach, the oath not to eat is broken – even if swallowed without chewing. If the substance is a liquid and reaches the stomach, the oath not to drink is broken.
The same act may be eating in one context (e.g., eating yogurt with bread) and drinking in another (e.g., yogurt alone).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام الأكل إيصال ما يحتمله المضغ بفيه إلى الجوف مضغ أو لم يمضغ كالخبز واللحم والفاكهة ونحوها والشرب إيصال ما لا يحتمل المضغ من المائعات إلى الجوف مثل الماء والنبيذ واللبن والعسل فإن وجد ذلك يحنث وإلا فلا يحنث إلا إذا كان ذلك أكلاً أو يسمى شرباً في العرف والعادة فيحنث، فإذا حلف لا يأكل كذا أو لا فأدخله في فيه ومضغه ثم ألقاه لم يحنث حتى يدخله في جوفه لأنه بدون ذلك لا يكون أكلاً وشرباً بل يكون ذوقاً . ولو حلف لا يأكل هذه البيضة أو لا يأكل هذه الجوزة فابتلعها قال قد حنث لوجود حد الأكل وهو ما ذكرنا، ولو حلف لا يأكل عنباً أو رماناً فجعل يمصه ويرمي تفله ويبتلع ماءه لم يحنث في الأكل ولا في الشرب لأن ذلك ليس بأكل ولا شرب بل هو مص. وإن عصر ماء العنب فلم يشربه وأكل قشره وحصرمه فإنه يحنث لأن الذاهب ليس إلا الماء وذهاب الماء لا يخرجه أن يكون أكلاً له، ألا من تری أنه إذا مضغه وابتلع الماء أنه لا يكون أكلاً له بابتلاع الماء بل بابتلاع الحصرم فدل أن أكل العنب هو أكل القشر والحصرم منه وقد وجد فيحنث . وقال هشام عن محمد في رجل حلف لا يأكل سكراً فأخذ سكرة فجلها في فيه فجعل يبتلع ماءها حتى ذابت قال : لم يأكل لأنه حين أوصلها إلى فيه وصلت وهي لا تحتمل المضغ . وكذلك روي عن أبي يوسف فيمن حلف لا يأكل رمانة فمص رمانة أنه لا يحنث. ولو حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن فأكله بخبز أو تمر أو حلف لا يأكل من هذا السعل فأكله بخبز يحنث لأن اللبن هكذا يكون، وكذلك الخل لأنه من جملة الإدام فيكون أكله بالخبز كاللبن، فإن أكل ذلك بانفراده لا يحنث لأن ذلك شرب وليس بأكل، فإن صب على ذلك الماء ثم شربه لا يحنث في قوله لا آكل لعدم الأكل ويحنث في قوله لا أشرب لوجود الشرب، وكذلك إن حلف لا يأكل هذا الخبز فجففه ثم دقه وصب عليه الماء فشربه لا يحنث لأن هذا شرب لا أكل، فإن أكله مبلولاً أو غير مبلول يحنث لأن الخبز هكذا يؤكل عادة وكذلك السويق إذا شربه بالماء فهو شارب وليس بأكل ؛ كذا في البدائع. ولم يذكر المصنف الذوق وهو معرفة الشيء بفيه من غير إدخال عينه ألا ترى أن الأكل والشرب مفطر لا الذوق، كذا في قوله : (لأنه حين أوصلها إلى فيه صوابه إلى جوفه». وعبارة الذخيرة فهذا ليس بأكل فقد وصل إلى جوفه ما لا يتأتى فيه المضغ .
Chapter of Oath regarding Eating, Drinking, Clothing, and Speech. Eating is delivering what can be chewed to the stomach, chewed or not chewed, like bread, meat, fruit, and the like. Drinking is delivering what cannot be chewed, from liquids, to the stomach, like water, nabidh, milk, and honey. If that occurs, he breaks his oath; otherwise not, unless that is considered eating or is called drinking by custom and habit, in which case he breaks his oath. If he swore not to eat something and put it in his mouth and chewed it, then spat it out, he does not break his oath until he delivers it to his stomach because without that it is not eating or drinking, but tasting. If he swore not to eat this egg or this walnut and swallowed it whole, he has broken his oath due to the existence of the definition of eating which we mentioned. If he swore not to eat grapes or pomegranates and sucked them, discarding the pulp and swallowing the juice, he does not break his oath in eating or drinking because that is not eating or drinking but sucking. If he squeezed grape juice and did not drink it, but ate the skin and sour grapes, he breaks his oath because what escapes is only water, and the escape of water does not prevent it from being eating of it. Do you not see that when he chews it and swallows the water, it is not considered eating by swallowing the water but by swallowing the pulp? So it indicates that eating grapes is eating the skin and pulp, and that has occurred, so he breaks his oath. Hisham narrated from Muhammad regarding a man who swore not to eat sugar – he took a piece of sugar and dissolved it in his mouth, swallowing its water until it melted – he said: He did not eat because when he delivered it to his mouth, it arrived while not being able to be chewed. Similarly, it is narrated from Abu Yusuf regarding one who swore not to eat a pomegranate and sucked a pomegranate, that he does not break his oath. If he swore not to eat this milk and ate it with bread or dates, or swore not to eat this vinegar and ate it with bread, he breaks his oath because milk is like that, and similarly vinegar because it is part of the condiment, so eating it with bread is like milk. If he eats that alone, he does not break his oath because that is drinking, not eating. If he poured water on it and then drank it, he does not break his oath in saying 'I will not eat' due to lack of eating, and breaks his oath in saying 'I will not drink' due to the occurrence of drinking. Similarly, if he swore not to eat this bread and then dried it, ground it, poured water on it, and drank it, he does not break his oath because this is drinking, not eating. If he ate it moistened or not moistened, he breaks his oath because bread is normally eaten that way. Similarly, sawiq: if he drinks it with water, he is a drinker, not an eater. This is from al-Bada'i. The author did not mention tasting, which is knowing something with the mouth without ingesting its substance. Do you not see that eating and drinking break the fast, not tasting? This is as in his statement: 'Because when he delivered it to his mouth' – the correct wording is 'to his stomach.' And the expression in al-Dhakhira: 'This is not eating, because what reaches his stomach does not allow chewing.'
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage defines the legal meanings of eating and drinking in the context of oaths. Eating requires delivering a chewable substance to the stomach, even if not chewed; drinking requires delivering a liquid to the stomach. If a person does an act that falls under these definitions, he breaks his oath. The author gives examples: swallowing an egg whole counts as eating; sucking a pomegranate and discarding the pulp does not. Eating something alone, like yogurt (labann), is considered drinking, so an oath not to eat it is not broken if eaten alone, but if eaten with bread, it is considered eating. The passage also clarifies that tasting (dhawq) does not break an oath or fast, as it does not involve ingestion. The commentary ends with a textual correction regarding a phrase in the earlier text. The rulings are based on customary usage ('urf) and the physical nature of the substance.

Key Terms
أكل (akl): Delivering a substance that can be chewed (like bread, meat, fruit) to the stomach, whether chewed or not.
شرب (shurb): Delivering a liquid that cannot be chewed (like water, milk, honey) to the stomach.
ذوق (dhawq): Tasting with the mouth without ingesting the substance, does not break an oath or fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'I will not eat this bread.' He dries the bread, grinds it into powder, mixes it with water, and drinks it. Does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because he consumed the bread.
B) No, because drinking ground bread mixed with water is considered drinking, not eating.
C) Yes, if he intended to break his oath.
D) No, because the bread was not chewed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, eating and drinking in oaths are defined by the nature of the substance and ingestion; tasting does not break an oath.
