Tasting Does Not Break an Oath of Eating or Drinking
Definition and analogy clarifying dhawq in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that dhawq (tasting) does not invalidate an oath of eating or drinking and distinguish it from actual consumption (akl/shurb).

Core Idea
Dhawq is not akl: tasting without swallowing does not break an oath of eating or drinking.

Oaths often involve specific acts like eating or drinking. But what about tasting a food without swallowing? The Hanafi school carefully defines dhawq as knowing something by mouth without conveying its substance to the stomach. This brief passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that such tasting does not constitute akl or shurb, drawing a parallel with the rules of fasting. You will learn the precise legal distinction and its practical implications for oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term defined
Locate the definition of dhawq in the Arabic text. Note that it is defined as knowing something with the mouth without conveying its substance (عَيْنَه) to the stomach.
This definition is crucial because it establishes that dhawq does not involve ingestion.
2
Recognize the analogy with fasting
The passage states: 'Eating and drinking break the fast, not tasting.' This analogy shows that the same logic applies in oaths: what invalidates fasting (actual consumption) is what breaks an oath of eating/drinking.
The analogy is a qiyas from fasting rules to oath rules.
3
Correct a textual error
The passage corrects a previous phrase: 'when he conveyed it to his mouth' should be 'to his stomach.' This emphasizes that the key is delivering substance to the stomach, not just the mouth.
This correction reinforces the definition of akl and shurb as requiring arrival in the stomach.
4
Apply the ruling to an oath scenario
If someone swears 'I will not eat in this house' and then tastes food without swallowing, the Hanafi ruling is that the oath is not broken because dhawq does not constitute akl.
The same applies if the oath is about drinking: tasting a liquid without swallowing does not break it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف الذوق وهو معرفة الشيء بفيه من غير إدخال عينه ألا ترى أن الأكل والشرب مفطر لا الذوق، كذا في قوله : (لأنه حين أوصلها إلى فيه صوابه إلى جوفه». وعبارة الذخيرة فهذا ليس بأكل فقد وصل إلى جوفه ما لا يتأتى فيه المضغ .
And the author did not mention tasting (dhawq), which is knowing something with the mouth without conveying its substance. Do you not see that eating and drinking break the fast, not tasting. Thus in his statement: (Because when he conveyed it to his mouth, the correct thing is to his stomach). And the expression of al-Dhakhira: This is not eating, for what cannot be chewed has reached his stomach.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This brief passage discusses tasting (dhawq) in the context of oaths. The author notes that the matn author did not explicitly mention dhawq. He defines dhawq as knowing something by mouth without swallowing its substance. He then points out an important analogy: eating and drinking invalidate the fast, but tasting does not. This analogy helps determine that dhawq does not break an oath of eating or drinking either. The passage then corrects a previous statement: the phrase 'when he conveyed it to his mouth' should be 'to his stomach'. Finally, a quotation from al-Dhakhira reiterates that if something that cannot be chewed reaches the stomach, it is not considered eating. This underscores the principle that for akl, the substance must normally be chewable.

Key Terms
الذوق (al-dhawq): Knowing something with the mouth without conveying its substance to the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the prophet said so in a hadith.
B) Because dhawq is defined as not conveying the substance to the stomach.
C) Because tasting is always permissible.
D) Because the oath was not made specifically about tasting.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, dhawq (tasting without swallowing) does not constitute akl or shurb and therefore does not break an oath of eating or drinking.

Interpretive Principle for Oaths on Inedible Objects: The Palm Tree Example
Hanafi ruling on interpreting an oath on a palm tree figuratively to its produce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that an oath on an inedible object is interpreted figuratively to its produce, and identify which natural products break the oath.

Core Idea
An oath on what cannot be eaten goes figuratively to its natural produce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common scenario in oaths: swearing about a palm tree. Since the tree itself is not eaten, the Hanafi school interprets the oath as referring to its fruit (thamar). Understanding what counts as fruit and what does not is essential for determining if the oath is broken. We will walk through the key principles and apply them to various produce from the palm tree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of the oath
Recognize that the oath is attached to a palm tree, which is not eaten in its substance.
The passage uses 'palm tree' as a representative of anything whose substance is not eaten.
2
Apply the figurative interpretation rule
Since the tree itself is inedible, the oath is understood figuratively to refer to what comes out of it – its produce.
The legal basis is that the word is used metaphorically because the literal meaning is impossible.
3
List natural products that break the oath
The oath is broken by anything naturally produced by the tree: palm heart (jammar), unripe dates (busr), fresh dates (rutab), dry dates (tamr), pollen (tala'), and fresh syrup (dibs that hasn't been cooked).
These are directly attributed to the tree, so eating any of them constitutes breaking the oath.
4
Exclude processed items
If the produce has been altered by human action (e.g., cooked dibs, vinegar, wine), the oath is not broken because the item is no longer attributed to the tree but to a new process.
Processed items are considered separate, so they do not fall under the figurative extension.
5
Handle the case of no fruit
If the tree bears no fruit at the time of the oath, the oath turns to the price of the tree. Breaking the oath occurs if the money is used to buy and eat food.
This ensures the oath still has a meaningful object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برطبه وتمره وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لا يأكل بسراً فأكل الكافي. ولذا قال في الظهيرية : لو حلف لا يذوق في منزل فلان طعاماً ولا شراباً فذاق فيه شيئاً أدخله في فيه ولم يصل إلى جوفه حنث ويمينه على الذوق حقيقة إلا أن يكون تقدمه كلام. وبيان ذلك أن يقول له غيره تعالى تغد عندي اليوم فحلف لا يذوق في منزله طعاماً ولا شراباً فهذا على الأكل والشرب وعن محمد فيمن حلف لا يذوق الماء فتمضمض للصلاة لا يحنث لأن هذا لا يراد بذكر الذوق اهـ. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يأكل ولا يشرب فذاق لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يذوق فأكل أو شرب حنث لأن في الأكل والشرب ذوقاً وزيادة اهـ. وسيأتي بيان اللبس والكلام إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله : (لا يأكل من هذه النخلة حنث بثمرها لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى ما لا يؤكل فينصرف إلى ما يخرج منه لأنه سبب له فيصلح مجازاً عنه . والثمر بالمثلثة ما يخرج منها فيحنث بالجمار والبسر والرطب والتمر والطلع والدبس الخارج من ثمرها. والجمار رأس النخلة وهي شيء أبيض لين والطلع ما يطلع من النخل وهو الكم قبل أن ينشق ويقال لما يبدو من الكم طلع أيضاً وهو شيء أبيض يشبه بلونه الأسنان وبرائحته المني؛ كذا في المغرب. وقيد بالثمر لأنه لا يحنث بما تغير بصفة حادثة فلا يحنث بالنبيذ والناطف والدبس المطبوخ والخل لأنه مضاف إلى فعل حادث فلم يبق مضافاً إلى الشجر. ويحنث بالعصير لأنه لم يتغير بصنعة جديدة ولو لم يكن للشجرة ثمرة ينصرف اليمين إلى ثمنها فيحنث إذا اشترى به مأكولاً وأكله. وأشار بقوله بثمرها إلى أنه لو قطع غصناً منها فوصله بشجرة أخرى فأكل من ثمر تلك الشجرة من هذا الغصن أنه لا يحنث وقال بعضهم يحنث وإلى أنه لو تكلف وأكل من عين النخلة لا يحنث قالوا : وهو الصحيح ؛ كذا في المحيط. وأشار بالنخلة إلى كل ما لا يؤكل عينه. فلو حلف لا يأكل من هذا الكرم فهو على عنبه وحصرمه وزبيبه وعصيره ـ وفي بعض المواضع . ودبسه والمراد عصيره فإنه ماء العنب وهو ما يخرج بلا صنع عند انتهاء نضج العنب. وقيد بما لا يؤكل عينه لأنه ولو حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة فإنه يحنث باللحم خاصة ولا يحنث باللبن والزبد لأنها مأكولة فينعقد اليمين عليها وكذا لو حلف لا يأكل من هذا العنب فإنه لا يحنث بزبيبه وعصيره لأن حقيقته ليست مهجورة فيتعلق الحلف العنب. وأطلق المصنف ولم يقيد بالنية للإشارة إلى أنه عند عدمها، فلو نوى أكل عينها لم يحنث بأكل ما يخرج منها لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه ؛ كذا في المحيط. وينبغي أن لا يصدق قضاء لأن المجاز صار متعيناً ظاهراً فإذا نوى بخلاف الظاهر لا يقبل وإن كان حقيقة وله شواهد كثيرة . قوله : (ولو عين البسر والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين، وكذا كونه
By its fresh dates, its dry dates, and its syrup, unlike this boy and this young man and this lamb. He does not eat unripe dates? So he ate [enough?]. Hence said in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to taste in so-and-so's house food or drink, and he tasted something, putting it in his mouth without it reaching his stomach, he breaks his oath, and his oath is on tasting literally unless preceded by speech. The explanation is that another said to him: Come to lunch at my place today, so he swore not to taste in his house food or drink; then this is about eating and drinking. From Muhammad: If someone swore not to taste water and rinsed his mouth for prayer, he does not break his oath because this is not intended by mentioning taste. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to eat or drink and he tasted, he does not break. But if he swore not to taste and he ate or drank, he breaks because eating and drinking include taste and more. The explanation of clothing and speech will come later, God willing. His saying: (He does not eat from this palm tree, he breaks by its fruit) because he attached the oath to something that is not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it, since it is a cause for it, so it is suitable as a metaphor for it. 'Fruit' with the three letters (th-m-r) is what comes out of it, so he breaks by the palm heart, unripe dates, fresh dates, dry dates, pollen, and the syrup that comes from its fruit. The palm heart is the head of the palm tree, a white soft thing; 'tala' is what emerges from the palm, the spathe before it splits; also what appears from the spathe is called 'tala', which is a white thing similar in color to teeth and in smell to semen; thus in al-Maghrib. He restricted to fruit because he does not break by what has changed by an accidental quality, so he does not break by wine, thick syrup, cooked dibs, and vinegar, because they are attributed to an accidental action and are no longer attributed to the tree. He breaks by fresh juice because it has not changed by a new process. If the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to its price, so he breaks if he buys food with it and eats it. He indicated by his saying 'by its fruit' that if he cut a branch from it and attached it to another tree and ate from the fruit of that tree from this branch, he does not break; some say he breaks; and that if he went to the trouble and ate from the trunk of the tree, he does not break; they said: that is correct; thus in al-Muhit. He indicated by 'palm tree' anything whose substance is not eaten. So if he swore not to eat from this vine, it is upon its fresh grapes, unripe grapes, raisins, and fresh juice — and in some places, its dibs, meaning its juice which is grape water that comes out without preparation at the end of ripening. He restricted to what is not eaten in substance because if he swore not to eat from this sheep, he breaks by the meat only and does not break by milk and butter because they are eaten, so the oath is attached to them. Similarly, if he swore not to eat from this grape, he does not break by its raisins and juice because its reality is not abandoned, so the oath attaches to the grape. The author made it absolute and did not restrict by intention to indicate that when there is no intention, if he intended to eat its substance, he does not break by eating what comes from it because he intended the reality of his speech; thus in al-Muhit. It is appropriate that he not be believed judicially because the metaphor has become clearly specified, so if he intends contrary to the apparent, he is not accepted even if it is real, and there are many evidences for this. His saying: (And if he specifies unripe dates, fresh dates, and milk, he does not break by its fresh dates, dry dates, and syrup, unlike this boy and this young man and this lamb) because the quality of freshness and unripeness prompts the oath, and also its [being]...
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with oaths that are attached to something whose substance is not eaten, concretely a palm tree. Since the palm tree itself is not eaten, the oath is understood figuratively to refer to its produce (fruit). The commentator explains that the oath is broken by anything that comes from the tree naturally: the palm heart (jammar), unripe dates (busr), fresh dates (rutab), dry dates (tamr), pollen (tala'), and fresh syrup (dibs) that is not cooked. However, if the produce has been processed (like cooked dibs, vinegar, wine), the oath is not broken because the product is no longer attributed to the tree but to a human action. Similarly, if the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to the value of the tree. The passage also distinguishes between an oath about a tree (which covers its produce) and an oath about a sheep or grape (which attaches directly to the thing itself). The section then addresses the issue of specifying a particular type (like busr or rutab) in the oath: if he specifies a certain stage, he only breaks by that stage, not by other stages. This is because the specification shows his intention is limited to that type. The comparison to a boy, young man, and lamb illustrates that for living beings, the oath attaches directly to the being, not to what it produces. Finally, the commentator notes that if the person had a hidden intention different from the apparent meaning, he is not believed in court because the figurative meaning (the produce) has become the clear intended meaning. This follows the Hanafi principle that in oaths, the literal meaning is given weight, and hidden intentions contrary to the explicit wording are not accepted. The source cited is al-Muhit.

Key Terms
النخلة (al-nakhlah): The palm tree; in oaths, something whose substance is not eaten, so the oath goes to its produce.
الثمر (al-thamar): Fruit or produce of a plant; here specifically what comes out of the palm tree (dates, etc.).
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative meaning; the oath about the palm tree is interpreted metaphorically to refer to its fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following breaks an oath sworn not to eat from a specific palm tree?
A) Eating cooked date syrup (dibs matbukh).
B) Eating fresh dates (rutab) from the tree.
C) Eating the trunk of the tree.
D) Drinking vinegar made from the dates.

Key Takeaway
When an oath is attached to an inedible object, it is interpreted figuratively to its natural produce; processed items and the substance itself are excluded.

Edge Cases and Specifications in Oaths on a Palm Tree
Hanafi rulings on special cases and specifications in oaths on palm trees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an oath on a palm tree is broken in special cases: grafted branches, eating the trunk, specifying a type of fruit, and the effect of hidden intentions.

Core Idea
Specifying a fruit type limits the oath; hidden intentions contrary to the apparent are not accepted in court.

This passage continues the discussion on oaths about a palm tree, addressing scenarios that go beyond the basic rule. We consider cases like grafted branches, eating the trunk, and what happens if the person explicitly mentions a specific type of fruit (e.g., unripe dates only). The passage also clarifies that hidden intentions are not given weight in court because the figurative meaning has become clear. Understanding these nuances is crucial for accurate fatwa on oath-related issues.

Guided Path
1
Examine the grafted branch scenario
If a branch from the sworn tree is grafted onto another tree, and fruit grows from that branch, eating that fruit does NOT break the oath (according to the relied-upon position).
The fruit is now attributed to the host tree, not the original.
2
Consider eating the trunk or wood
If the person goes out of his way to eat parts of the tree itself (e.g., the trunk), he does not break his oath because the oath is only about the produce.
This is the correct position; it underscores that the figurative interpretation is limited to fruit.
3
Analyze specification of a type
If the person says 'I will not eat from this palm tree' but then specifies 'unripe dates (busr)', the oath is limited to unripe dates only. He does not break by eating fresh dates (rutab) or dry dates (tamr).
The specification indicates his intention is restricted to that type.
4
Understand the comparison to living beings
The passage compares to a boy, young man, and lamb: if he specifies 'this boy', the oath attaches to that individual, not to what he produces (e.g., his offspring). Similarly for young man and lamb.
This contrasts with the palm tree where specification limits to a stage of fruit.
5
Assess hidden intentions
If the person had a hidden intention that the oath meant something else (e.g., eating the tree itself), he is not believed in court because the figurative meaning (fruit) has become unequivocal.
This follows the Hanafi principle that clear apparent meanings take precedence over hidden intentions in judicial settings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برطبه وتمره وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لا يأكل بسراً فأكل الكافي. ولذا قال في الظهيرية : لو حلف لا يذوق في منزل فلان طعاماً ولا شراباً فذاق فيه شيئاً أدخله في فيه ولم يصل إلى جوفه حنث ويمينه على الذوق حقيقة إلا أن يكون تقدمه كلام. وبيان ذلك أن يقول له غيره تعالى تغد عندي اليوم فحلف لا يذوق في منزله طعاماً ولا شراباً فهذا على الأكل والشرب وعن محمد فيمن حلف لا يذوق الماء فتمضمض للصلاة لا يحنث لأن هذا لا يراد بذكر الذوق اهـ. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يأكل ولا يشرب فذاق لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يذوق فأكل أو شرب حنث لأن في الأكل والشرب ذوقاً وزيادة اهـ. وسيأتي بيان اللبس والكلام إن شاء الله تعالى. قوله : (لا يأكل من هذه النخلة حنث بثمرها لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى ما لا يؤكل فينصرف إلى ما يخرج منه لأنه سبب له فيصلح مجازاً عنه . والثمر بالمثلثة ما يخرج منها فيحنث بالجمار والبسر والرطب والتمر والطلع والدبس الخارج من ثمرها. والجمار رأس النخلة وهي شيء أبيض لين والطلع ما يطلع من النخل وهو الكم قبل أن ينشق ويقال لما يبدو من الكم طلع أيضاً وهو شيء أبيض يشبه بلونه الأسنان وبرائحته المني؛ كذا في المغرب. وقيد بالثمر لأنه لا يحنث بما تغير بصفة حادثة فلا يحنث بالنبيذ والناطف والدبس المطبوخ والخل لأنه مضاف إلى فعل حادث فلم يبق مضافاً إلى الشجر. ويحنث بالعصير لأنه لم يتغير بصنعة جديدة ولو لم يكن للشجرة ثمرة ينصرف اليمين إلى ثمنها فيحنث إذا اشترى به مأكولاً وأكله. وأشار بقوله بثمرها إلى أنه لو قطع غصناً منها فوصله بشجرة أخرى فأكل من ثمر تلك الشجرة من هذا الغصن أنه لا يحنث وقال بعضهم يحنث وإلى أنه لو تكلف وأكل من عين النخلة لا يحنث قالوا : وهو الصحيح ؛ كذا في المحيط. وأشار بالنخلة إلى كل ما لا يؤكل عينه. فلو حلف لا يأكل من هذا الكرم فهو على عنبه وحصرمه وزبيبه وعصيره ـ وفي بعض المواضع . ودبسه والمراد عصيره فإنه ماء العنب وهو ما يخرج بلا صنع عند انتهاء نضج العنب. وقيد بما لا يؤكل عينه لأنه ولو حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة فإنه يحنث باللحم خاصة ولا يحنث باللبن والزبد لأنها مأكولة فينعقد اليمين عليها وكذا لو حلف لا يأكل من هذا العنب فإنه لا يحنث بزبيبه وعصيره لأن حقيقته ليست مهجورة فيتعلق الحلف العنب. وأطلق المصنف ولم يقيد بالنية للإشارة إلى أنه عند عدمها، فلو نوى أكل عينها لم يحنث بأكل ما يخرج منها لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه ؛ كذا في المحيط. وينبغي أن لا يصدق قضاء لأن المجاز صار متعيناً ظاهراً فإذا نوى بخلاف الظاهر لا يقبل وإن كان حقيقة وله شواهد كثيرة . قوله : (ولو عين البسر والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين، وكذا كونه
By its fresh dates, its dry dates, and its syrup, unlike this boy and this young man and this lamb. He does not eat unripe dates? So he ate [enough?]. Hence said in al-Zahiriyya: If he swore not to taste in so-and-so's house food or drink, and he tasted something, putting it in his mouth without it reaching his stomach, he breaks his oath, and his oath is on tasting literally unless preceded by speech. The explanation is that another said to him: Come to lunch at my place today, so he swore not to taste in his house food or drink; then this is about eating and drinking. From Muhammad: If someone swore not to taste water and rinsed his mouth for prayer, he does not break his oath because this is not intended by mentioning taste. In al-Muhit: If he swore not to eat or drink and he tasted, he does not break. But if he swore not to taste and he ate or drank, he breaks because eating and drinking include taste and more. The explanation of clothing and speech will come later, God willing. His saying: (He does not eat from this palm tree, he breaks by its fruit) because he attached the oath to something that is not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it, since it is a cause for it, so it is suitable as a metaphor for it. 'Fruit' with the three letters (th-m-r) is what comes out of it, so he breaks by the palm heart, unripe dates, fresh dates, dry dates, pollen, and the syrup that comes from its fruit. The palm heart is the head of the palm tree, a white soft thing; 'tala' is what emerges from the palm, the spathe before it splits; also what appears from the spathe is called 'tala', which is a white thing similar in color to teeth and in smell to semen; thus in al-Maghrib. He restricted to fruit because he does not break by what has changed by an accidental quality, so he does not break by wine, thick syrup, cooked dibs, and vinegar, because they are attributed to an accidental action and are no longer attributed to the tree. He breaks by fresh juice because it has not changed by a new process. If the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to its price, so he breaks if he buys food with it and eats it. He indicated by his saying 'by its fruit' that if he cut a branch from it and attached it to another tree and ate from the fruit of that tree from this branch, he does not break; some say he breaks; and that if he went to the trouble and ate from the trunk of the tree, he does not break; they said: that is correct; thus in al-Muhit. He indicated by 'palm tree' anything whose substance is not eaten. So if he swore not to eat from this vine, it is upon its fresh grapes, unripe grapes, raisins, and fresh juice — and in some places, its dibs, meaning its juice which is grape water that comes out without preparation at the end of ripening. He restricted to what is not eaten in substance because if he swore not to eat from this sheep, he breaks by the meat only and does not break by milk and butter because they are eaten, so the oath is attached to them. Similarly, if he swore not to eat from this grape, he does not break by its raisins and juice because its reality is not abandoned, so the oath attaches to the grape. The author made it absolute and did not restrict by intention to indicate that when there is no intention, if he intended to eat its substance, he does not break by eating what comes from it because he intended the reality of his speech; thus in al-Muhit. It is appropriate that he not be believed judicially because the metaphor has become clearly specified, so if he intends contrary to the apparent, he is not accepted even if it is real, and there are many evidences for this. His saying: (And if he specifies unripe dates, fresh dates, and milk, he does not break by its fresh dates, dry dates, and syrup, unlike this boy and this young man and this lamb) because the quality of freshness and unripeness prompts the oath, and also its [being]...
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with oaths that are attached to something whose substance is not eaten, concretely a palm tree. Since the palm tree itself is not eaten, the oath is understood figuratively to refer to its produce (fruit). The commentator explains that the oath is broken by anything that comes from the tree naturally: the palm heart (jammar), unripe dates (busr), fresh dates (rutab), dry dates (tamr), pollen (tala'), and fresh syrup (dibs) that is not cooked. However, if the produce has been processed (like cooked dibs, vinegar, wine), the oath is not broken because the product is no longer attributed to the tree but to a human action. Similarly, if the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to the value of the tree. The passage also distinguishes between an oath about a tree (which covers its produce) and an oath about a sheep or grape (which attaches directly to the thing itself). The section then addresses the issue of specifying a particular type (like busr or rutab) in the oath: if he specifies a certain stage, he only breaks by that stage, not by other stages. This is because the specification shows his intention is limited to that type. The comparison to a boy, young man, and lamb illustrates that for living beings, the oath attaches directly to the being, not to what it produces. Finally, the commentator notes that if the person had a hidden intention different from the apparent meaning, he is not believed in court because the figurative meaning (the produce) has become the clear intended meaning. This follows the Hanafi principle that in oaths, the literal meaning is given weight, and hidden intentions contrary to the explicit wording are not accepted. The source cited is al-Muhit.

Key Terms
النخلة (al-nakhlah): The palm tree; in oaths, something whose substance is not eaten, so the oath goes to its produce.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Specification; in oaths, when a person specifies a type of fruit, the oath is limited to that type.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention; in oaths, hidden intentions contrary to the apparent meaning are not accepted in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears, 'I will not eat from this palm tree,' but later eats a date from a branch that was grafted onto another palm tree, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because the branch originally came from the sworn tree.
B) He does not break his oath because the fruit is now attributed to the host tree.
C) He breaks his oath only if he knew the branch was grafted.
D) He does not break his oath because eaten from another tree, not the one sworn.

Key Takeaway
Specification limits an oath to the specified type, while hidden intentions are not accepted in court when the apparent meaning is clear.

The Legal Meaning of 'Tasting' in Hanafi Oaths
Clarifying the legal meaning of dhawaq in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal and contextual interpretations of 'tasting' (dhawaq) in Hanafi oath rulings and apply the correct ruling to scenarios involving eating, drinking, and rinsing.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'tasting' defaults to literal mouth entry, but context can shift its meaning to eating/drinking.

When someone swears not to 'taste' food or drink, does merely inserting food into the mouth break the oath? Or does it require swallowing? Hanafi fiqh answers this by examining both the literal meaning of dhawaq and the context of the oath. In this lesson, we will explore how the Hanafi masters distinguish between mere tasting and actual eating or drinking, and how prior conversation can change the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on literal dhawaq
The passage states that if someone swears not to taste food/drink in a house, and then puts something in his mouth without it reaching the throat, he breaks his oath. This is the default ruling: dhawaq literally means inserting into the mouth.
This is from al-Dhahira.
2
Recognise the role of context
If the oath was preceded by a conversation inviting the person to eat, then the oath is interpreted as referring to eating and drinking, not mere tasting. Context changes the meaning from literal to customary.
Example: someone says 'come eat with me' and he swears not to taste in that house.
3
Apply the exception for rinsing
Imam Muhammad rules that if one swears not to taste water and rinses his mouth for prayer, he does not break his oath because rinsing is not intended as tasting. The act must be done with the intent of tasting or consuming.
This shows that intention and customary usage matter.
4
Distinguish between related oaths
From al-Muhit: if one swears not to eat or drink and merely tastes, he does not break his oath. But if he swears not to taste and then eats or drinks, he breaks his oath because eating includes tasting.
This highlights the logical relationship between the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذا قال في الظهيرية : لو حلف لا يذوق في منزل فلان طعاماً ولا شراباً فذاق فيه شيئاً أدخله في فيه ولم يصل إلى جوفه حنث ويمينه على الذوق حقيقة إلا أن يكون تقدمه كلام. وبيان ذلك أن يقول له غيره تعالى تغد عندي اليوم فحلف لا يذوق في منزله طعاماً ولا شراباً فهذا على الأكل والشرب وعن محمد فيمن حلف لا يذوق الماء فتمضمض للصلاة لا يحنث لأن هذا لا يراد بذكر الذوق اهـ. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يأكل ولا يشرب فذاق لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يذوق فأكل أو شرب حنث لأن في الأكل والشرب ذوقاً وزيادة اهـ. وسيأتي بيان اللبس والكلام إن شاء الله تعالى.
Hence it is stated in al-Dhahira: If someone swears not to taste in the house of so-and-so food or drink, then he tastes something, inserting it into his mouth without reaching his throat, he breaks his oath. His oath is upon tasting in reality unless preceded by speech. Explanation: if another says to him 'come eat with me today' and he swears not to taste in his house food or drink, then this refers to eating and drinking. And from Muhammad: if someone swears not to taste water and rinses his mouth for prayer, he does not break his oath because this is not intended by mentioning tasting. End. In al-Muhit: If he swears not to eat nor drink and tastes, he does not break his oath. But if he swears not to taste and eats or drinks, he breaks his oath because eating and drinking include tasting and more. End. And the explanation of clothing and speech will come later, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal meaning of the term 'tasting' (ذوق) in the context of oaths. The author clarifies that when someone swears not to taste food or drink in a specific place, the default understanding is the literal meaning of tasting, i.e., bringing something into the mouth. However, if an oath is preceded by a conversation where one is invited to eat, the oath is interpreted as referring to eating and drinking, not mere tasting. Imam Muhammad reports that if one swears not to taste water and merely rinses his mouth for prayer, he does not break his oath because the act of rinsing is not intended as tasting. In al-Muhit, it is stated that if one swears not to eat or drink and merely tastes, he does not break his oath, but if he swears not to taste and then eats or drinks, he breaks his oath because eating/drinking subsumes tasting. This highlights the Hanafi principle that in oaths, words are interpreted according to their customary usage with attention to context.

Key Terms
ذوق (dhawaq): In oaths, the literal meaning is inserting something into the mouth; but it can be interpreted as eating/drinking if context indicates.
حنث (hanth): Breaking or violating an oath, incurring legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if someone swears not to taste water and then rinses his mouth for prayer without swallowing, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because water entered his mouth.
B) He does not break his oath because rinsing is not intended as tasting.
C) He breaks his oath only if he swallows the water.
D) It depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'tasting' (dhawaq) is literally mouth entry, but context can elevate its meaning to eating/drinking, and customary usage excludes incidental acts like rinsing.

The Scope of an Oath on a Date Palm or Grapevine
Explains what constitutes breaking an oath on a tree or vine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for oaths on inedible items and apply it to determine if the oath is broken by eating produce or processed derivatives.

Core Idea
An oath not to eat from an inedible tree extends to its produce; an oath on an edible item stays on the item itself.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position on oaths related to trees and vines. The key is whether the item sworn by is itself edible. If it is not edible per se (like a date palm), the oath transfers to its fruit and all natural derivatives. However, if the item is edible (like a sheep or grape), the oath remains confined to that specific item. Understanding this distinction prevents common errors in oath-breaking determinations.

Guided Path
1
Determine edibility of the object
Identify whether the item sworn by is eaten in its essence. If yes, the oath stays on that item; if no, proceed to step 2.
A date palm is inedible per se; a sheep is edible.
2
Identify the scope for inedible objects
For inedible objects, the oath applies to all produce (thamar) that comes out of it, including unprocessed derivatives like juice, syrup, and heart.
The text lists jammār, busr, rutab, tamr, tal', and syrup as included.
3
Exclude processed items
Items that have undergone human processing (e.g., vinegar, cooked syrup) are attributed to a new action and do not break the oath.
Natural changes (e.g., raisins) are still included.
4
Handle missing produce and branch transfer
If the tree has no fruit, the oath transfers to its price. Cutting a branch and attaching it to another tree does not result in breaking the oath.
This is the correct view per al-Muhit.
5
Consider intention
If the swearer intended the tree itself, that intention is not accepted judicially because the apparent meaning (fruit) is dominant.
The metaphor has become specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا يأكل من هذه النخلة حنث بثمرها لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى ما لا يؤكل فينصرف إلى ما يخرج منه لأنه سبب له فيصلح مجازاً عنه . والثمر بالمثلثة ما يخرج منها فيحنث بالجمار والبسر والرطب والتمر والطلع والدبس الخارج من ثمرها. والجمار رأس النخلة وهي شيء أبيض لين والطلع ما يطلع من النخل وهو الكم قبل أن ينشق ويقال لما يبدو من الكم طلع أيضاً وهو شيء أبيض يشبه بلونه الأسنان وبرائحته المني؛ كذا في المغرب. وقيد بالثمر لأنه لا يحنث بما تغير بصفة حادثة فلا يحنث بالنبيذ والناطف والدبس المطبوخ والخل لأنه مضاف إلى فعل حادث فلم يبق مضافاً إلى الشجر. ويحنث بالعصير لأنه لم يتغير بصنعة جديدة ولو لم يكن للشجرة ثمرة ينصرف اليمين إلى ثمنها فيحنث إذا اشترى به مأكولاً وأكله. وأشار بقوله بثمرها إلى أنه لو قطع غصناً منها فوصله بشجرة أخرى فأكل من ثمر تلك الشجرة من هذا الغصن أنه لا يحنث وقال بعضهم يحنث وإلى أنه لو تكلف وأكل من عين النخلة لا يحنث قالوا : وهو الصحيح ؛ كذا في المحيط. وأشار بالنخلة إلى كل ما لا يؤكل عينه. فلو حلف لا يأكل من هذا الكرم فهو على عنبه وحصرمه وزبيبه وعصيره ـ وفي بعض المواضع . ودبسه والمراد عصيره فإنه ماء العنب وهو ما يخرج بلا صنع عند انتهاء نضج العنب. وقيد بما لا يؤكل عينه لأنه ولو حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة فإنه يحنث باللحم خاصة ولا يحنث باللبن والزبد لأنها مأكولة فينعقد اليمين عليها وكذا لو حلف لا يأكل من هذا العنب فإنه لا يحنث بزبيبه وعصيره لأن حقيقته ليست مهجورة فيتعلق الحلف العنب. وأطلق المصنف ولم يقيد بالنية للإشارة إلى أنه عند عدمها، فلو نوى أكل عينها لم يحنث بأكل ما يخرج منها لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه ؛ كذا في المحيط. وينبغي أن لا يصدق قضاء لأن المجاز صار متعيناً ظاهراً فإذا نوى بخلاف الظاهر لا يقبل وإن كان حقيقة وله شواهد كثيرة .
His statement: '(He swears not to eat from this date palm) He breaks his oath by its fruit because he added the oath to what is not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it because it is a cause for it, so it is valid as a metaphor for it. And 'thamar' (fruit) with the triple-pointed letter (thā') is what comes out of it, so he breaks his oath by the heart of the palm, unripe dates, moist dates, dry dates, pollen spathe, and the syrup that comes from its fruit. The 'jammār' is the head of the palm, a white soft thing; the 'tal' is what comes out of the palm, which is the spathe before it splits, and what appears from the spathe is also called 'tal', a white thing resembling in color teeth and in smell semen; thus in al-Mughrib. He restricted to fruit because he does not break his oath by what has changed by a new attribute, so he does not break his oath by nabidh, nātif, cooked syrup, and vinegar because it is attributed to a new action, so it is no longer attributed to the tree. And he breaks his oath by juice because it has not changed by a new processing. And if the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to its price, so he breaks his oath if he buys with it edible food and eats it. And his indication by 'its fruit' is that if he cuts a branch from it and attaches it to another tree and eats from the fruit of that tree from this branch, he does not break his oath; and some say he breaks his oath. And that if he takes trouble and eats from the tree itself he does not break his oath; they said: this is correct; thus in al-Muhit. And he indicated by 'date palm' everything whose essence is not eaten. So if he swears not to eat from this grapevine, it applies to its grapes, unripe grapes, raisins, and juice; and in some places, its syrup, meaning its juice, for it is grape water that comes out without processing at the end of grape ripeness. And he restricted to what is not eaten in essence because if he swears not to eat from this sheep, he breaks his oath only by meat, and does not break his oath by milk and butter because they are edible, so the oath is contracted upon them. Similarly, if he swears not to eat from this grape, he does not break his oath by its raisins and juice because its reality is not abandoned, so the oath is attached to the grape. And the author gave an absolute statement without restricting to intention to indicate that when there is no intention [it is as stated]. So if he intended to eat the tree itself, he does not break his oath by eating what comes out of it because he intended the reality of his speech; thus in al-Muhit. And it should not be believed judicially because the metaphor has become specifically apparent, so if he intends contrary to the apparent, it is not accepted even if it is reality, and there are many evidences for this.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof explaining the scope of an oath when someone swears not to eat from a date palm or grapevine. The key principle is that if the thing sworn by is inedible (like a tree), the oath is transferred to its produce, as the tree is the cause of the produce. If the item is edible itself (like a sheep or grape), the oath remains on that specific item and does not extend to its derivatives (e.g., milk, raisins) because the reality is not abandoned. The author discusses what constitutes fruit (thamar) including the heart of the palm, various stages of dates, and syrup. He notes that items that have undergone significant processing (like vinegar) do not break the oath because they are attributed to a new action. If the tree has no fruit, the oath turns to its monetary value. The author also addresses the case of cutting a branch and attaching it to another tree—the correct view is that the oath is not broken by eating produce from that branch. Finally, he discusses intention: if the oath-taker intended the tree itself, his oath is not broken by eating its fruit, but this intention is not accepted judicially because the apparent meaning (the fruit) is the default.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): All produce that comes out of a tree, including fruit, pollen, and syrup.
النخلة (al-nakhla): Date palm; used as an example of an inedible object whose oath extends to its produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from a date palm and then eats vinegar made from its dates, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because vinegar comes from dates.
B) No, because vinegar is a processed product that has changed by a new action.
C) Yes, if the vinegar is homemade.
D) No, because the oath applies only to fresh fruit.

Key Takeaway
The oath on an inedible item transfers to its natural produce; processed items are excluded because they are attributed to a new action.

Oaths on Items That Change State: When Does the Oath Persist?
Ruling on oaths when the object changes form, with examples and principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of dā'ī (impetus) to determine whether an oath on a definite item is broken when the item's description changes.

Core Idea
An oath is not broken if the changed description was the reason for the oath; it is broken otherwise.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains a nuanced rule about oaths on specific objects that change form, such as fresh dates becoming dry or milk turning into yogurt. The key is whether the attribute that changed was the very reason (dā’ī) for the oath. If it was, the oath does not apply to the new form; if not, the oath persists. Understanding this distinction prevents unnecessary expiation and reflects the Law's consideration of intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage rules on whether an oath on a definite item is broken when the item changes its description. The condition is whether the description was the impetus (dā’ī) for the oath.
The text uses 'ain (definite) items: 'this unripe date', 'this milk'.
2
Locate the proof (dalīl)
The proof is the principle that the dā'ī restricts the oath. This is shown through examples: if the description was the impetus, the oath ceases when the description changes; if not, the oath remains.
The child and lamb examples illustrate when the description is not the impetus.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The attribute (e.g., moistness) is the reason for the oath; thus the oath is tied to that attribute. When it vanishes, the object of the oath is no longer present, so no breach occurs.
In the child case, childhood is not a reason for shunning, so the oath attaches to the person, not the description.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
There is a dispute about whether transformation always affects the oath. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on dā'ī; others might not.
The text highlights the difference between fruit/dairy (description is dā'ī) and human/animal (description is not dā'ī).
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The Hanafi position is muftā bihi, as stated by the author. The evidence from the child case shows that ignoring the dā'ī when it is not the reason is unreasonable.
The additional examples (grape to raisin, etc.) confirm the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو عين البسر والرطب واللبن لا يحنث برطبه وتمره وشيرازه بخلاف هذا الصبي وهذا الشاب وهذا الحمل لأن صفة الرطوبة والبسورة داعية إلى اليمين، وكذا كونه لبناً فيتقيد به فإذا حلف لا يأكل هذا البسر فأكله بعدما صار رطباً أو حلف لا يأكل هذا الرطب فأكله بعد ما صار تمراً يعني يابساً وهو بالتاء المثناة، أو حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن فأكله بعدما صار شيرازاً أي رائباً وهو الخائر إذا استخرج ماؤه فإنه لا يحنث في هذه المسائل الثلاث بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يكلم هذا الصبي أو الشاب فكلمه بعد ما شاخ فإنه يحنث لأن هجران المسلم بمنع الكلام منهي عنه فلم يعتبر الداعي في الشرع، ولأن صفة الصبا داعية إلى المرحمة لا إلى الهجران فلا تعتبر وتتعلق اليمين بالإشارة. وكذا لو حلف لا يأكل هذا الحمل - بفتحتين ولد الشاة - فأكله بعد ما صار كبشاً فإنه يحنث لأن صفة الصغر في هذا ليست داعية إلى اليمين فإن الممتنع عنه أكثر امتناعاً عن لحم الكبش والأصل أن المحلوف عليه إذا كان بصفة داعية إلى اليمين تقيد به في المعرف والمنكر فإن زالت زال اليمين عنه وما لا يصلح داعية اعتبر في المنكر دون المعرف قيد بقوله «عين» لأنه لو نكر فسيأتي. وقيد بهذا الصبي لأنه لو حلف لا يكلم صبياً فكلم بالغاً لا يحنث لأنه صار مقصوداً بالحلف لكونه هو المعرف للمحلوف عليه فيجب تقييد اليمين به وإن كان حراما ؛ كذا في الكشف الكبير. فالصبي من لم يبلغ وكذا الغلام فإذا بلغ فهو شاب وفتى إلى ثلاثين سنة أو ثلاث وثلاثين على الاختلاف فهو كهل إلى خمسين سنة فهو شيخ كما في الذخيرة. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو حلف لا يأكل هذا العنب فصار زبيباً أو لا يأكل هذا اللبن فصار جبناً أو حلف لا يأكل من هذه البيضة فأكل من فراريجها أو لا يذوق من هذا الخمر فصار خلاً أو حلف لا يأكل من زهرة هذه الشجرة فأكل بعد ما صار لوزاً أو مشمشاً فإنه لا يحنث بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل تمراً فأكل حيساً فإنه يحنث لأنه تمر مفتت فإن التمر بجميع أجزائه قائم إذ تفرقت أجزاؤه لا غير؛ كذا في المحيط. وفسر الحيس في البدائع بأنه اسم لتمر ينقع في اللبن ويتشرب فيه اللبن، وقيل هو طعام يتخذ من تمر ويضم إليه شيء من السمن أو غيره، والغالب هو التمر فكان أجزاء التمر بحالها فيبقى الاسم اهـ. والكلام ليس بقيد في مسألتي الصبي لأنه لو حلف لا يجامع هذه الصبية فجامعها بعد ما صارت كبيرة يحنث كما في البدائع. ولو حلف لا يأكل هذه الجدحة فأكلها بعد ما صارت بطيخاً لا رواية فيه، واختلف المشايخ فيه؛ كذا في البدائع أيضاً. وفيها أيضاً إذا نوى في الفصول المتقدمة ما يوجب الحنث حنث لأنه شدد على نفسه .
His statement: 'And if he specifies unripe dates, moist dates, and milk, he does not break his oath by their becoming ripe, dry, or yogurt, unlike this child, this youth, and this lamb because the attribute of moistness and unripeness is an impetus to the oath, and similarly being milk, so it is restricted by it. So if he swears not to eat this unripe date and eats it after it becomes moist, or swears not to eat this moist date and eats it after it becomes dry (meaning dry dates, with a tā' with two dots), or swears not to eat this milk and eats it after it becomes yogurt (meaning sour milk that is whey when its water is extracted), then he does not break his oath in these three cases, contrary to if he swears not to speak to this child or youth and speaks to him after he becomes old, for he breaks his oath because abandoning a Muslim by preventing speech is forbidden, so the impetus was not considered in the Law. And because the attribute of childhood is an impetus to mercy, not to abandonment, so it is not considered, and the oath attaches to the referent. Similarly, if he swears not to eat this lamb (with two fathas, the young of a sheep) and eats it after it becomes a ram, he breaks his oath because the attribute of smallness in this is not an impetus to the oath, for the abstention from it is more abstention from the meat of a ram. And the principle is: if the thing sworn by has a description that is an impetus to the oath, it is restricted by it in both definite and indefinite; if it ceases, the oath ceases from it. And what is not suitable as an impetus is considered in indefinite but not in definite. He restricted with 'ain (specific) because if it were indefinite, it will come later. And he restricted with 'this child' because if he swears not to speak to a child and speaks to an adult, he does not break his oath because the adult became intended by the oath by virtue of being the definite reference of what is sworn by, so the oath must be restricted to him even if it is forbidden; thus in al-Kashf al-Kabir. So a child is one who has not reached puberty, and similarly a boy; when he attains puberty, he is a youth and a young man up to thirty or thirty-three years, differing; then he is middle-aged to fifty, then old; as in al-Dhakhira. And the author indicated that if he swears not to eat this grape and it becomes raisins, or not to eat this milk and it becomes cheese, or swears not to eat from this egg and eats from its chicks, or not to taste this wine and it becomes vinegar, or swears not to eat from the flower of this tree and eats after it becomes almond or apricot, then he does not break his oath, contrary to if he swears not to eat dates and eats hayis (a dish of mashed dates), for he breaks his oath because it is crushed dates, since the dates in all their parts are present as their parts have dispersed only; thus in al-Muhit. And hayis is explained in al-Bada'i' as a name for dates soaked in milk and absorbing milk, and it is said to be a food made from dates with some clarified butter or other, and the dominant part is dates, so the parts of dates are in their state, so the name remains. End. And the word 'speech' is not a restriction in the two cases of the child, because if he swears not to have intercourse with this girl and has intercourse with her after she becomes an adult, he breaks his oath; as in al-Bada'i'. And if he swears not to eat this watermelon-seedling and eats it after it becomes a watermelon, there is no transmission; the scholars differed on it; thus in al-Bada'i' also. And in it (al-Bada'i'): if he intended in the aforementioned chapters what necessitates breaking the oath, he breaks his oath because he has tightened upon himself.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling-with-proof regarding oaths on specific items that undergo a change of state. The key distinction is whether the description (e.g., unripe, moist, milk) was the impetus (dā'ī) for the oath. If it was, then when the description changes, the oath no longer applies and is not broken. If the description was not the impetus (e.g., a child or lamb), then the oath attaches to the referent itself, so change does not affect it and the oath is broken. The text provides examples: swearing not to eat unripe dates and eating them when ripe does not break the oath because unripeness was the reason for abstention. Similarly for moist dates and milk. But swearing not to speak to a child and speaking to him as an adult breaks the oath because childhood was not the impetus (rather, shunning a Muslim is forbidden). The same applies to a lamb becoming a ram. The author then gives additional transformation examples (grape to raisin, milk to cheese, egg to chick, wine to vinegar, flower to fruit) where the oath is not broken, except for dates becoming hayis (mashed dates) where the date essence remains. Finally, he touches on intention: if the oath-taker intends to include the changed form, his intention is accepted because he tightened upon himself.

Key Terms
داعية (dā'iyah): The impetus or reason that motivates the oath; a description that is the cause for the oath.
معرف (mu'arraf): Definite noun; the object of the oath is definite (e.g., 'this child').
حنث (ḥanīth): Breaking the oath; the legal consequence of violating an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat this yogurt and eats cheese made from it, does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because cheese is made from yogurt.
B) No, because the yogurt's description (being milk) was the impetus, and it changed to cheese.
C) It depends on whether he intended to avoid cheese.
D) Yes, because the oath is on the original item regardless of change.

Key Takeaway
The impetus (dā'ī) is key: if the oath was motivated by a specific description, a change in that description ends the oath's applicability.

Eating Part of the Thing Sworn: The One-Sitting Rule
Ruling on whether eating part of the object breaks the oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when eating part of an object breaks an oath based on customary consumption in one sitting.

Core Idea
Eating part of something breaks the oath only if the whole cannot be consumed in one sitting.

Swearing not to eat a specific item raises the question: does eating a portion break the oath? The Hanafi school answers by looking at customary consumption. If the item is normally eaten in one sitting (like a loaf of bread), the oath covers the whole, and part does not break it. But if the item is not normally consumed in one sitting (like a large quantity of fruit), the oath is to abstain generally, so any part breaks it. This lesson explains the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Object of the Oath
Determine the specific item the person swore not to eat (e.g., 'this loaf', 'these dates'). The ruling depends on how the item is customarily consumed as a whole.
The object may be a single unit or a collection (e.g., a bunch of dates).
2
Determine Customary Consumption
Is the item normally eaten in one sitting (majlis) for solids, or drunk in one gulp (shurbah) for liquids? This is based on the common practice of people.
Custom (urf) is the benchmark; not the individual's eating speed.
3
Apply the Rule
If the item is customarily consumed in one sitting, the oath applies to the whole; eating any part does not break the oath. If not, the oath applies generally; eating any part breaks the oath.
The key distinction is between 'the whole' and 'generally'.
4
Apply to Examples
Work through the examples in the text: fruit of a garden, dates from two trees, two loaves, milk of two sheep, one sheep. Determine which are single-sitting items.
A single loaf is normally eaten in one sitting; fruit from a garden is not.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi Position
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school. There is no disagreement within the school on this principle.
Other schools may have different criteria, but the Hanafi position is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الأصل فيما إذا حلف لا يأكل معيناً فأكل بعضه إن كان يأكله الرجل في مجلس أو يشربه في شربة فالحلف على جميعه، ولا يحنث بأكل بعضه لأن المقصود الامتناع عن أكله وكل شيء لا يطاق أكله في المجلس ولا شربه في شربة يحنث بأكل بعضه لأن المقصود من اليمين الامتناع أصله لا عن عن جميعه، فلو حلف لا يأكل من ثمر هذا البستان أو من تمرهاتين النخلتين أو من هذين الرغيفين أو من لبن هاتين الشاتين أو هذا الغنم أو لا من
Then know that the principle regarding when he swears not to eat something specific and eats part of it: if it is something that a man eats in one sitting or drinks in one gulp, then the oath applies to all of it, and he does not break his oath by eating part because the intent is to abstain from eating it entirely. And anything that cannot be eaten in one sitting nor drunk in one gulp, he breaks his oath by eating part of it because the intent of the oath is to abstain from it generally, not from all of it entirely. So if he swears not to eat from the fruit of this garden, or from the dates of these two palm trees, or from these two loaves of bread, or from the milk of these two sheep, or from this sheep, or not from...
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage states a clear principle regarding oaths on specific edible items: if the item is normally consumed in one sitting (like a single loaf of bread or a glass of milk), then the oath is considered to apply to the whole item, and eating only a part does not break the oath because the intent was to abstain from the entire item. Conversely, if the item is not normally consumed in one sitting (like a large quantity of fruit from a garden or two whole loaves), then eating any part breaks the oath because the oath's purpose is to abstain from that item generally, not necessarily from the entire quantity. The author gives examples: swearing not to eat from the fruit of a garden or from two palm trees or two loaves—these are large amounts normally not eaten in one sitting, so eating part breaks the oath. The text ends abruptly but the rule is complete.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): A single sitting where food is customarily consumed.
شربة (shurbah): A single draught or gulp of a drink.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from the dates of two palm trees and eats one date, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He does not break his oath because dates from two trees are a large quantity not consumed in one sitting.
B) He breaks his oath because eating part of a multi-sitting item breaks the oath.
C) He does not break his oath because a date is a single serving.
D) He breaks his oath only if he eats the entire crop.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, eating part of a sworn item breaks the oath only if the item is not customarily consumed in one sitting; otherwise, it does not.

Eating or Drinking Part of the Subject of an Oath Breaches It
Examples of oaths where consuming part of the object constitutes a breach

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that consuming part of the subject of an oath constitutes a breach, and apply it to scenarios involving divisible and indivisible items.

Core Idea
If you swear not to eat something, eating any part breaks the oath unless the whole can be eaten in one sitting.

Oaths in Islamic law are serious commitments. When you swear not to consume a specific item, what constitutes a breach? This lesson explores the Hanafi view that the particle 'min' indicates partiality (tab'id), so consuming any part of the subject violates the oath. Through several examples, you'll learn the general rule and its exceptions based on the nature of the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic principle
Recognize that the Arabic particle 'min' in the oath indicates partiality, meaning the oath applies to any part of the subject.
This is the core proof: the word 'min' implies tab'id.
2
Apply to divisible consumables
For items like ghee, river water, or honey, consuming any part breaks the oath. The text gives examples: eating some ghee, drinking some water, taking one coin from two.
These are all cases where part can be consumed separately.
3
Handle composite subjects
When the subject is a collection (e.g., milk of two goats), the oath is not broken until part from each component is consumed. Drinking from one goat only does not suffice because the oath covers each.
This follows from the same principle: the oath covers each part individually.
4
Distinguish based on nature of act
Compare eating vs. selling: eating part of a jar breaks the oath because eating cannot be done in one go, but selling part does not break a sale oath because selling is divisible.
The nature of the verb matters: 'eating' is normally done gradually.
5
Apply the whole-consumption exception
If the item is normally consumed whole in one sitting (e.g., an egg, or a small portion of food), the oath is not broken until all is consumed. The ability to eat it all at once determines the threshold.
This is the preferred view of the Hanafi scholars: 'al-asahh al-mukhtar'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يأكل سمن أشرب . من ماء هذه الأنهار فأكل أو شرب بعضه يحنث لأن كلمة «من» للتبعيض فكانت اليمين متناولة بعض المذكور وقد وجد وكذلك لو قبض ديناراً فوجد درهمين زائفين فحلف لا يأخذ منهما شيئاً وأخذ أحدهما حنث . ولو قال لا أشرب لبن هاتين الشاتين ونحو ذلك لم يحنث حتى يشرب من لبن كل شاة ولا يعتبر شرب الكل لأنه غير مقصود. ولو حلف لا هذه الخابية فأكل بعضه حنث ولو كان مكان الأكل بيعاً فباع بعضها لا يحنث لأن الأكل لا يتأتى على جميعه في مجلس واحد ويتأتى البيع. ولو حلف لا يأكل هذه البيضة لا يحنث حتى يأكلها كلها ولو حلف لا يأكل هذا الطعام فإن كان يقدر على أكل كله دفعة واحدة لا يحنث حتى يأكل كله، وإن لم يقدر حنث بأكل بعضه وهو الأصح المختار لمشايخنا.
If one swears, 'I will not eat ghee' or 'I will not drink from the water of these rivers' and then eats or drinks some of it, he breaks the oath because the word 'min' (from) indicates partiality, so the oath covered part of the mentioned (thing) and it has occurred. Similarly, if he took hold of a dinar and found two counterfeit dirhams and swore, 'I will not take anything from them', and then took one of them, he breaks the oath. If he said, 'I will not drink the milk of these two goats' and similar, he does not break the oath until he drinks from the milk of each goat, and drinking all is not considered because it is not intended. If he swore, 'I will not (eat) this jar', and then ate some of it, he breaks the oath. If instead of eating it was a sale, and he sold some of it, he does not break the oath because eating cannot be done all at once in one sitting, while selling can be. If he swore, 'I will not eat this egg', he does not break the oath until he eats it all. If he swore, 'I will not eat this food', then if he is able to eat all of it in one go, he does not break the oath until he eats it all; if he is not able, he breaks the oath by eating some of it, and this is the most correct and chosen (view) of our scholars.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on oaths concerning eating and drinking. The text begins by stating the general principle: if one swears not to eat or drink something and then consumes any part of it, the oath is broken. The reason is that the word 'min' (from) in the oath indicates partiality (tab'id). Therefore, the oath applies to any part of the specified item. Several examples follow: swearing on river water, on counterfeit coins (taking one breaks the oath), on the milk of two goats (must drink from each goat's milk to break the oath, as drinking from one only is not enough), on a jar (eating part breaks, but selling part does not because the nature of the act differs), on an egg (must eat the whole egg due to its indivisible nature), and on food (if one can eat it all at once, he must eat it all; otherwise, eating part breaks the oath). The last point is the preferred view of the Hanafi scholars. The common thread is that the oath is broken by consuming any part unless the object is customarily consumed whole in one sitting.

Key Terms
من (min): The Arabic preposition indicating partiality (tab'id) when used in oaths, so that the oath applies to any part of the subject.
حنث (hanith): Breach of an oath; the legal consequence of violating the condition of the oath.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; a solemn declaration binding oneself to do or not do something, with legal consequences for breach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat this jar of honey' and then eats half of it, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) He does not break the oath because he did not eat the whole jar.
B) He breaks the oath because eating part of the subject breaches the oath.
C) He breaks the oath only if he eats the jar itself, not the contents.
D) The oath is nullified because the jar is not normally eaten.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, swearing not to consume an item generally means that consuming any part breaches the oath, unless the item is customarily consumed whole in one sitting.

Negligible Amounts in Oaths: The Hanafi Use of Istihsan
Rulings on oaths with multiple subjects or conditional clauses affecting others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of yasir (negligible amount) and istihsan to determine whether an oath is broken when a small part remains unconsumed.

Core Idea
Custom disregards what is negligible — one or two seeds still count as eating the whole.

The Hanafi school employs istihsan to avoid impractical strictness in oaths. This lesson examines how custom determines what is negligible, using the example of a pomegranate and a loaf. You will learn to distinguish between amounts that custom treats as non-existent and those that are substantial enough to affect the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the object
Read the scenario: a man swears not to eat a pomegranate, but eats it except for one or two seeds. Determine the object of the oath and the amount left unconsumed.
The object is a pomegranate; the unconsumed amount is one or two seeds.
2
Apply the principle of istihsan
Recognize that strict analogy (qiyas) would treat any leftover as non-consumption, but istihsan based on custom overrides this. Custom regarding pomegranates treats one or two seeds as negligible.
The text explicitly invokes istihsan for this reason.
3
Generalize the principle
The text states that a small amount (yasir) of bread or similar is like nothing, analogous to a morsel. Understand that this applies to any food where custom considers the remaining amount insignificant.
The phrase 'كاللقمة' (like a morsel) indicates a qiyas-like extension.
4
Distinguish from substantial leftovers
If the leftover is half, a third, or more that custom does not treat as negligible, the oath is not broken. Note the threshold: custom determines what counts as 'eating the whole.'
The text gives examples of half, third, etc., as amounts that are not negligible.
5
Apply to multiple persons
Consider the earlier scenario with two wives and two loaves. If each eats one loaf, the slave is freed. If one eats almost all and the other the rest, the oath is also broken. This illustrates that collective consumption of the whole quantity triggers breach.
This establishes that the condition can be satisfied by multiple persons acting together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لامرأتيه إن أكلتما هذين الرغيفين فعبدي حر فأكلت كل واحدة منهما رغيفاً عتق العبد، وكذلك لو أكلت إحداهما الرغيفين إلا شيئاً وأكلت الباقي الأخرى يحنث ؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي البدائع معزياً إلى الأصل بعد ما ذكر هذه المسائل قال : ولو قال لا آكل هذه الرمانة فأكلها إلا حبة أو حبتين حنث في الاستحسان لأن ذلك القدر لا يعتد به فإنه يقال في العرف لمن أكل رمانة وترك منها حبة أو حبتين أنه أكل رمانة وإن ترك نصفها أو ثلثها أو ترك أكثر مما لا يجري في العرف أنه يسقط من الرمانة لم يحنث لأنه لا أكلاً لجميعها يسمى اهـ. وبه يعلم أن اليسير من الرغيف وغيره كالعدم كاللقمة وفي الواقعات : اغترف من القدر ثم قال والله لا آكل من هذا القدر فأكل ما في القصعة لا يحنث لأن اليمين على ما بقي في القدر. ثم قال في الفصل التاسع: قال إن أكلت هذا الرغيف اليوم فامرأته طالق ثلاثاً وإن لم آكله اليوم فأمته حرة فأكل النصف لم يحنث لانعدام شرط الحنث في اليمينين وهو أكل الكل أو ترك الكل، ولو أخذ لقمة فوضعها في فيه فقال له رجل امرأتي طالق إن أكلتها وقال آخر امرأتي طالق إن أخرجتها من فيك فأكل البعض وأخرج البعض لم يحنث أحدهما لأن شرط الحنث أكل الكل أو إخراج الكل ولم يوجد قال هذا الرغيف علي حرام فأكل بعضه حنث وهذا بخلاف قوله لا آكل هذا الرغيف إذا كان مما يؤكل كله في مجلس واحد والفتوى على ذلك اهـ.
If he said to his two wives, 'If you both eat these two loaves, then my slave is free,' and each one ate a loaf, the slave becomes free. Similarly, if one of them ate the two loaves except for a small amount and the other ate the remainder, he (the husband) breaks the oath. This is stated in al-Muhit. In al-Bada'i, attributed to al-Asl, after mentioning these issues, it said: If he said, 'I will not eat this pomegranate,' and then ate it except for one or two seeds, he breaks the oath by istihsan because that amount is not considered. For it is said customarily of one who ate a pomegranate and left one or two seeds that he ate the pomegranate. But if he left half, a third, or more that is not customarily considered to fall from the pomegranate, he does not break the oath because it is not called eating all of it. From this it is known that a small amount of a loaf and similar is like nothing, like a morsel. In al-Waqi'at: He scooped from the pot and then said, 'By Allah, I will not eat from this pot,' and then ate what was in the bowl; he does not break the oath because the oath is on what remained in the pot. Then he said in the ninth chapter: If he said, 'If I eat this loaf today, my wife is triply divorced, and if I do not eat it today, my slave is free,' and he ate half, he does not break either oath because the condition for breach is absent in both oaths, which is eating all or leaving all. If he took a morsel and put it in his mouth, and one man said, 'My wife is divorced if you eat it,' and another said, 'My wife is divorced if you take it out of your mouth,' and he ate part and spit out part, neither man's oath is broken because the condition for breach is eating all or spitting out all, which did not occur. He said: 'This loaf is forbidden to me,' and he ate some of it; he breaks the oath. This is contrary to his saying, 'I will not eat this loaf,' if it is something that can be eaten all in one sitting, and the fatwa is on that.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage deals with complex oaths involving multiple persons or conditional formulations. The first scenario involves two wives and two loaves: if each eats one loaf, the slave is freed; if one eats almost all and the other the remainder, the oath is also broken. The second scenario concerns a pomegranate: leaving one or two seeds still counts as eating the pomegranate by istihsan (juristic preference) because custom disregards negligible amounts. The third scenario involves scooping from a pot and then swearing not to eat from that pot; eating from a bowl does not break the oath because the oath was on the pot's remaining content. The fourth scenario presents conditional oaths with two conditions (eating all or refraining all): eating half does not trigger either. Similarly, a morsel scenario with two conditional oaths on the same morsel (one on eating, one on spitting out) requires complete consumption or complete expulsion for breach. Finally, if one declares food haram on himself (tahrim), eating part breaches the prohibition, unlike a normal oath where the ability to eat all at once matters. The key principle is that the wording and intention determine the threshold for breach.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference based on custom (urf) that deems a negligible amount as non-existent for the purpose of the oath.
يسير (yasir): A negligible or insignificant amount that custom considers as nothing, such as one or two seeds of a pomegranate.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; in this context, a conditional oath suspending divorce or manumission on an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not eat this pomegranate,' but eats it except for one seed. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) He does not break the oath because he left a seed.
B) He breaks the oath by istihsan because the amount left is negligible.
C) He breaks the oath only if he intended to eat the whole pomegranate.
D) The oath is invalid because pomegranates are usually eaten with seeds.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, a negligible leftover is treated as non-existent by istihsan, based on custom, so the oath is broken.

Conditional Oaths: The Requirement of Complete Action
Rulings on conditional oaths and the requirement of complete action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze complex conditional oaths involving multiple conditions or two parties and determine when breach occurs based on the requirement of complete consumption or complete abstention.

Core Idea
A conditional oath is broken only when the condition is fully met — eating all or leaving all, not partial action.

When an oath is linked to a condition, the condition must be completely fulfilled for breach. This lesson explores scenarios where partial action does not satisfy the condition, such as eating half a loaf in a two-condition oath, or partly eating and partly spitting out a morsel. It also contrasts the tahrim (declaring something forbidden) with a normal oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in the oath
In the first scenario, the man says: 'If I eat this loaf today, my wife is triply divorced; if I do not eat it today, my slave is free.' The conditions are 'eating all' or 'leaving all'. Partial action satisfies neither.
The condition is explicit: eating the whole or not eating at all.
2
Determine the action taken
He eats half the loaf. Does this fulfill 'eating the loaf'? No, because he did not eat all. Does it fulfill 'not eating'? No, because he ate part. Thus, neither condition is met.
The action must correspond exactly to the wording.
3
Apply the same logic to the morsel scenario
Two men make conditional oaths on the same morsel: one says 'my wife is divorced if you eat it,' the other says 'my wife is divorced if you take it out of your mouth.' The person eats part and spits out part. Neither condition is fully met.
The condition requires complete eating or complete removal.
4
Analyze the pot and bowl scenario
A person scoops from the pot, then swears 'I will not eat from this pot,' but eats from the bowl. The oath is on what remains in the pot, not the food already removed. So eating from the bowl does not break the oath.
The object of the oath is carefully defined by the remaining contents.
5
Distinguish tahrim from normal oath
If he says 'this loaf is forbidden to me' (tahrim), and eats part, he breaks the prohibition. This is different from a normal oath (like 'I will not eat this loaf') where the fatwa is based on whether the loaf can be eaten in one sitting.
Tahrim is a stronger statement; partial consumption is sufficient for breach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
A man says, 'If I eat this loaf today, my wife is triply divorced; if I do not eat it today, my slave is free.' He eats half the loaf. What is the ruling?
A) Both the divorce and the manumission take effect.
B) The divorce takes effect because he ate some.
C) Neither takes effect because he neither ate all nor refrained completely.

Key Takeaway
Conditional oaths are broken only when the condition is fully satisfied; partial action does not trigger breach.
