Oaths on Dates: The Ruling on Mixed Mudhnab Dates
Detailed analysis of oaths on mixed dates with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four scenarios of oaths on mixed dates and state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
When you swear not to eat a specific date type, a mixed date with even a little of that type breaks your oath — because in eating, every bit counts.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths regarding dates require precise knowledge of date types: rutab (ripe), busr (unripe), and mudhnab (mixed). The central dispute is whether eating a mudhnab date that predominantly belongs to the opposite category still constitutes a breach. This lesson unpacks the definitions, the four possible scenarios, and the reasoning of the Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Define the three date categories
Identify the definitions of rutab (fully ripe), busr (unripe), and mudhnab (partly ripe, with a visible tail of ripening). Understand that mudhnab can be mostly one type but contains some of the other.
rutab mudhnab = mostly ripe with a little unripe; busr mudhnab = mostly unripe with a little ripe.
2
Enumerate the four oath scenarios
List the four cases: (1) oath on rutab → eating rutab mudhnab (agreed breach), (2) oath on busr → eating busr mudhnab (agreed breach), (3) oath on rutab → eating busr mudhnab (disputed), (4) oath on busr → eating rutab mudhnab (disputed).
Cases 1-2 are agreed upon; cases 3-4 are the source of ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the rationale for the disputed cases
Examine Abu Hanifa's argument: in eating, each component is individually consumed and intended, so even a small amount of the forbidden type triggers the oath. Contrast with Abu Yusuf's view that the majority governs, analogous to purchase.
The distinction between eating (piecemeal) and purchase (whole) is key.
4
Determine the relied-upon position
Learn that in most authoritative books, Imam Muhammad agreed with Abu Hanifa, making the position that both disputed cases break the oath the mufta bihi view. Abu Yusuf alone dissents.
Thus the operative ruling: eating busr mudhnab breaks an oath on rutab, and eating rutab mudhnab breaks an oath on busr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رطباً لا يحنث وفي لا يأكل رطباً أو بسراً أو لا يأكل رطباً ولا بسراً حنث بالمذنب وهو بكسر النون كما في المغرب. يقال بسر مذنب وقد ذنب إذا بدا الأرطاب من قبل ذنبه وهو ما سفل من جانب القمع والعلاقة وأما الرطب فهو ما أدرك من تمر النخل الواحدة رطبة، فالرطب المذنب هو الذي أكثره رطب وشيء قليل منه بسر، والبسر المذنب عكسه. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا يحنث في الرطب بالبسر المذهب ولا في البسر بالرطب المذنب لأن الرطب رطباً والبسر المذنب يسمى بسراً وصار كما إذا كانت اليمين على الشراء. وله أن الرطب المذنب ما يكون في ذنبه قليل بسر والبسر المذنب على عكسه فصار أكله أكل البسر والرطب، وكل واحد مقصود في الأكل بخلاف الشراء فإنه يصادف الجملة فيتبع القليل فيه الكثير، وفي أكثر الكتب المعتبرة أن محمداً مع أبي حنيفة وحاصل المسائل أربع : وفاقيتان وخلافيتان؛ فالوفاقيتان ما إذا حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل رطباً مذنباً، وما إذا حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل بسراً مذنباً فيحنث فيهما اتفاقاً. والخلافيتان ما إذا حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، وما إذا حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً مذنباً فإنه يحنث عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف.
He does not break the oath by (eating) fresh ripe dates (rutab). And in 'I will not eat fresh dates (rutab) or unripe dates (busr)' or 'I will not eat fresh dates nor unripe dates', he breaks the oath by the mudhnab (date that is partly ripe). Mudhnab is with a kasra on the noon, as in al-Mughrib. It is said 'busr mudhnab' and 'dhanaba' when the ripeness appears from its tail (dhanab), which is the lower part of the stem and attachment. As for rutab, it is what has ripened of the palm dates, one is ratbah. So the rutab mudhnab is that which is mostly ripe with a little unripe part, and the busr mudhnab is the opposite. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not break the oath by eating busr mudhnab in a (oath on) rutab, nor by eating rutab mudhnab in a (oath on) busr, because the rutab (in question) is rutab and the busr mudhnab is called busr, and it becomes like if the oath was on purchase. He (Abu Hanifa) argues that the rutab mudhnab has in its tail a little busr, and the busr mudhnab is the opposite, so its eating is eating of both busr and rutab, and each one is intended in eating, unlike purchase which confronts the whole and the little follows the much. In most authoritative books, Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa. The issues are four: two agreed upon and two disagreed upon. The agreed upon are: if he swears not to eat rutab and eats rutab mudhnab, and if he swears not to eat busr and eats busr mudhnab; he breaks the oath in both by agreement. The disagreed upon are: if he swears not to eat rutab and eats busr mudhnab, and if he swears not to eat busr and eats rutab mudhnab; he breaks the oath according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths concerning dates, specifically the categories of fresh ripe dates (rutab) and unripe dates (busr), and the intermediate type called 'mudhnab' (dates that are partly ripe). The text defines these terms: mudhnab refers to dates where ripeness is visible at the tail (dhanab) near the stem. A rutab mudhnab is mostly ripe with a little unripe part, and busr mudhnab is mostly unripe with a little ripe part. The legal dispute is between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa holds that if one swears not to eat rutab and eats busr mudhnab (mostly busr with a little rutab), or swears not to eat busr and eats rutab mudhnab (mostly rutab with a little busr), the oath is broken because the act of eating involves both types and each is intended in consumption. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (except in one variant) differ: they say such eating does not breach the oath because the dominant type is what counts, analogous to purchase where the minority follows the majority. The summary given is that the relied-upon position is that Muhammad (al-Shaybani) agrees with Abu Hanifa on the breach, resulting in two agreed cases (eating mudhnab of the same kind) and two disputed cases (eating the opposite mudhnab). In the disputed cases, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule breach; Abu Yusuf disagrees.

Key Terms
رطب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates that have fully matured on the palm.
بسر (busr): Unripe dates that are still hard and have not yet begun to ripen.
مذنب (mudhnab): A date that is partly ripe, showing ripeness at its tail (dhanab). It can be mostly ripe (rutab mudhnab) or mostly unripe (busr mudhnab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears, 'I will not eat rutab (ripe dates),' and then eats a busr mudhnab (mostly unripe with some ripe parts), what is the ruling according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) He does not break the oath because the date is predominantly busr.
B) He breaks the oath because the date contains some rutab and is intended in eating.
C) The oath is nullified because mudhnab dates are not considered rutab or busr.
D) He breaks the oath only if he eats the entire bunch.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that eating a mixed date (mudhnab) breaks an oath against eating the specific type, even if the mixed date is predominantly the other type, due to the nature of consumption.

Oaths on Buying vs Eating: The Principle of the Dominated Following the Majority
Oath rulings: buying vs eating mixed commodities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between oaths on buying and oaths on eating concerning mixed commodities, and apply the Hanafi principle of al-maghlub tabi' (the dominated follows the majority) to determine breach.

Core Idea
In oaths, purchase follows the majority; eating targets each part individually.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle but critical distinction in Hanafi oath jurisprudence. When a person swears not to buy a specific item but buys a mixed commodity containing that item, the oath is not broken because the purchase is a single transaction on the whole. However, if the oath concerns eating, even a small amount of the forbidden item in a mixture triggers a breach. The key is the nature of the act: purchase is holistic, eating is incremental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts compared
The passage compares buying versus eating in the context of oaths. Note both acts and their respective rulings.
Buying is a single composite act; eating is incremental.
2
Understand the key principle: al-maghlub tabi'
In purchase, the dominated component (minority) follows the dominant (majority). The purchase is on the whole, not on individual parts.
This principle applies to contracts and oaths.
3
Apply the principle to the date bunch example
A bunch of unripe dates (busr) containing some ripe dates (rutab): buying does not break an oath against buying rutab because the bunch is mostly busr and the rutab is dominated.
The oath is on the specific type, but the transaction is on the whole commodity.
4
Contrast with eating
If the oath is on eating, consuming any part of the rutab breaks the oath because eating is piece by piece, and each piece is intended.
The same applies to mixtures like wheat with barley grains.
5
Note the analogy and exceptions
The text also touches on oaths about touching (e.g., cotton/linen) where the name change matters. Recognize that the ruling on touching differs because the raw material's name is lost in the finished product.
This shows that the principle is limited to buying/eating, not touching.

قوله : (ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة بسر فيها رطب في لا يشتري رطباً) أي لو حلف لا يشتري رطباً فاشترى كباسة بسر فيها رطب لم يحنث لأن الشراء يصادف جملته والمغلوب تابع، ولو كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث لأن الأكل يصادفه شيئاً فشيئاً فكان كل واحد منهما مقصوداً، وصار كما إذا حلف لا يشتري شعيراً أو لا يأكل فاشترى حنطة فيها حبات شعيراً أو أكلها يحنث في الأكل دون الشراء لما قدمنا قال في الخانية : لو حلف لا يشتري إليه فاشترى شاة مذبوحة كان حانثاً، وكذا إذا حلف لا يشتري رأساً والكباسة - بكسر الكاف ـ عنقود النخل والجمع كبائس. قال في التبيين : بخلاف ما إذا عقد يمينه على المس حيث يحنث في الوجوه كلها لأن المس فيها متصور حقيقة واسم المحلوف عليه باق بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا أو كتاناً فمس ثوباً اتخذ منه حيث لا يحنث لزوال اسم القطن والكتان عنه فصار كمن حلف لا يأكل سمناً أو زبداً أو لا يمسه فأكل لبناً أو مسه. قوله : (وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً) أي
His saying: 'And he does not break the oath by buying a bunch (kibasah) of unripe dates (busr) that contains ripe dates (rutab) in 'I will not buy ripe dates' — meaning, if he swore, 'I will not buy ripe dates,' and then bought a bunch of unripe dates that contains ripe dates, he does not break the oath because the purchase confronts the whole and the dominated is a follower. But if the oath was on eating, he breaks the oath because eating confronts it bit by bit, so each one is intended. And it becomes like if he swore not to buy barley or not to eat, and then bought wheat that contains grains of barley, or ate it: he breaks the oath in eating but not in buying, due to what we mentioned. He said in al-Khaniyya: If he swore, 'I will not buy meat,' and bought a slaughtered sheep, he is a breaker of the oath. Likewise if he swore, 'I will not buy a head.' And 'kibasah' with a kasra on the kaf is a bunch of dates; the plural is kaba'is. He said in al-Tabyin: Contrary to if he made his oath on touching, where he breaks the oath in all aspects because touching is conceptually real and the name of the subject remains. Unlike if he swore, 'I will not touch cotton or linen,' and then touched a garment made from them, where he does not break the oath because the name of cotton and linen is removed from it. So it becomes like one who swore, 'I will not eat ghee or butter' or 'I will not touch it,' and then ate milk or touched it. His saying: 'And with fish in 'I will not eat meat' — meaning, ...
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage explains the crucial difference between oaths concerning buying and oaths concerning eating, using the example of a bunch of dates that contains both ripe and unripe dates. The principle is that when one buys a composite item, the purchase is on the whole, and the minority (the ripe dates) follows the majority (the unripe dates). Thus, buying a bunch of busr that has some rutab does not break an oath against buying rutab. However, if the oath is on eating, eating any part of the rutab within the bunch would break the oath because eating is done piece by piece, and each component is individually consumed and intended. The same analogy applies to buying wheat mixed with barley: buying does not break an oath against buying barley, but eating the mixture does break an oath against eating barley. The text also cites al-Khaniyya for a related ruling: buying a slaughtered sheep breaks an oath against buying meat because the sheep is meat. Then al-Tabyin distinguishes oaths on touching: if one swears not to touch a specific raw material (like cotton), touching a finished garment does not break the oath because the name has changed. Finally, the passage begins to address fish in an oath about meat, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
كباسة (kibasah): A bunch of dates from the palm tree; in the text, it refers to a bunch containing both ripe and unripe dates.
مغلوب (maghlub): The dominated or minority component that follows the majority in a composite item, especially in purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears, 'I will not buy ripe dates (rutab),' but buys a bunch of unripe dates (busr) that contains some ripe dates, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath because the bunch contains ripe dates.
B) He does not break the oath because the purchase is on the whole and the minority follows the majority.
C) He breaks the oath only if he intended to buy ripe dates.
D) The oath is invalid because dates are not a recognized commodity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between buying and eating in oaths turns on whether the act is holistic (purchase) or incremental (eating), with the minority following the majority in purchase.

Oath on Eating Dates: The Muthannab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on muthannab dates in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on muthannab dates in oaths, explain the reasoning of the Sahibayn, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Eating a muthannab date breaks the oath because each part is separately consumed.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles a subtle but important issue in the fiqh of oaths: what happens when the food item sworn upon is neither fully ripe nor fully unripe, but a mixture? The muthannab date, with both rutab and busr portions, creates a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle that oaths are broken by consumption of any part of the sworn item, and that different rules apply to eating versus buying.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify that Abu Hanifa holds that eating a muthannab date (whether mostly rutab or mostly busr) breaks an oath sworn on either rutab or busr, because the act of eating encounters each part separately.
This is the position attributed to the master.
2
Identify the opposing position (Sahibayn)
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold the opposite: eating a muthannab does not break the oath, because the whole item is considered and the minor part follows the majority.
They analogize to buying, where the whole is the subject of the contract.
3
Examine the proofs for each side
For Abu Hanifa, the key is that the oath is about eating, which is piecemeal. For the Sahibayn, the key is that the item as a whole is named after its majority; a busr muthannab is still called busr, so it does not violate a 'no rutab' oath.
Understand the difference in the underlying analogy (eating vs. buying).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes eating from buying: in eating, each portion is independently consumed, so the name of each part matters. In buying, the contract attaches to the whole object, so the minor component is subsumed.
This is the critical distinction that resolves the apparent contradiction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: if one swears not to eat rutab and eats a busr muthannab, Abu Hanifa says hanth occurs, Sahibayn say no. The commentator notes that in most reliable books, Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa, making the hanth view the mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on the weight of the riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب واللبس والكلام
رطباً لا يحنث وفي لا يأكل رطباً أو بسراً أو لا يأكل رطباً ولا بسراً حنث بالمذنب ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة بسر فيها رطب في لا يشتري رطباً
اليمين تناول بعض المحلوف عليه فلا يحنث بخلاف ما إذا أوصى بعنب ثم صار زبيباً ثم مات الموصي بطلت الوصية . والفرق أن الرطب والتمر صنف واحد لقلة التفاوت بينهما بخلاف العنب والزبيب فإنه تبديل وهلاك؛ كذا في غاية البيان قوله : (لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً لا يحنث) لأنه ليس ببسر كما لو حلف لا يأكل عنباً فأكل زبيباً قيد به لأنه لو حلف لا يأكل جوزاً فأكل منه رطباً أو يابساً، وكذلك اللوز والفستق والبندق والتين وأشباه ذلك لأن الاسم يتناول الرطب واليابس جميعاً؛ كذا في البدائع.
المذنب
يسمى
قوله : (وفي لا يأكل رطباً أو بسراً أو لا يأكل رطباً ولا بسراً حنث بالمذنب) وهو بكسر النون كما في المغرب. يقال بسر مذنب وقد ذنب إذا بدا الأرطاب من قبل ذنبه وهو ما سفل من جانب القمع والعلاقة وأما الرطب فهو ما أدرك من تمر النخل الواحدة رطبة، فالرطب المذنب هو الذي أكثره رطب وشيء قليل منه بسر، والبسر المذنب عكسه. وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا يحنث في الرطب بالبسر المذهب ولا في البسر بالرطب المذنب لأن الرطب رطباً والبسر المذنب يسمى بسراً وصار كما إذا كانت اليمين على الشراء. وله أن الرطب المذنب ما يكون في ذنبه قليل بسر والبسر المذنب على عكسه فصار أكله أكل البسر والرطب، وكل واحد مقصود في الأكل بخلاف الشراء فإنه يصادف الجملة فيتبع القليل فيه الكثير، وفي أكثر الكتب المعتبرة أن محمداً مع أبي حنيفة وحاصل المسائل أربع : وفاقيتان وخلافيتان؛ فالوفاقيتان ما إذا حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل رطباً مذنباً، وما إذا حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل بسراً مذنباً فيحنث فيهما اتفاقاً. والخلافيتان ما إذا حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، وما إذا حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً مذنباً فإنه يحنث عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف. قوله: (ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة بسر فيها رطب في لا يشتري رطباً) أي لو حلف لا يشتري رطباً فاشترى كباسة بسر فيها رطب لم يحنث لأن الشراء يصادف جملته والمغلوب تابع، ولو كان اليمين على الأكل يحنث لأن الأكل يصادفه شيئاً فشيئاً فكان كل واحد منهما مقصوداً، وصار كما إذا حلف لا يشتري شعيراً أو لا يأكل فاشترى حنطة فيها حبات شعيراً أو أكلها يحنث في الأكل دون الشراء لما قدمنا قال في الخانية : لو حلف لا يشتري إليه فاشترى شاة مذبوحة كان حانثاً، وكذا إذا حلف لا يشتري رأساً والكباسة - بكسر الكاف ـ عنقود النخل والجمع كبائس. قال في التبيين : بخلاف ما إذا عقد يمينه على المس حيث يحنث في الوجوه كلها لأن المس فيها متصور حقيقة واسم المحلوف عليه باق بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يمس قطناً أو كتاناً فمس ثوباً اتخذ منه حيث لا يحنث لزوال اسم القطن والكتان عنه فصار كمن حلف لا يأكل سمناً أو زبداً أو لا يمسه فأكل لبناً أو مسه.
Book of Oaths, Chapter on oath concerning eating, drinking, clothing, and speech. (If one swears not to eat) moist dates (rutab), he is not guilty (of breaking the oath); and in (the case where he swears) not to eat moist dates or unripe dates (busr), or not to eat moist dates nor unripe dates, he breaks the oath by (eating) a 'muthannab' (date that is partly ripe, partly unripe). And he does not break the oath by buying a bunch (kibasa) of unripe dates that has moist dates in it, when he swore not to buy moist dates. (The commentary explains:) The oath touches some of the thing sworn by, so he is not guilty, contrary to the case where one bequeaths grapes and they become raisins, then the testator dies, the bequest is void. The difference is that moist dates and dried dates are one species due to little difference between them, unlike grapes and raisins, which is a transformation and destruction. So in Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying: (If he swears not to eat unripe dates and eats moist dates, he is not guilty) because it is not unripe dates, just as if he swore not to eat grapes and ate raisins. He qualified it because if he swore not to eat nuts and ate from them moist or dry, similarly almonds, pistachios, hazelnuts, figs and the like, because the name includes both moist and dry. So in al-Bada'i. (The word) 'al-muthannab' is (used). His saying: (And in (the case where he swears) not to eat moist dates or unripe dates or not to eat moist dates and not unripe dates, he breaks the oath by (eating) a muthannab) - it is with a kasra on the nun as in al-Maghrib. It is said 'busr muthannab' and 'muthannab' if the moistness appears from its stalk (dhanab), which is the lower part of the crown and attachment. As for moist dates, it is what has ripened of the dates of the palm, singular 'rutba'. So the 'rutab muthannab' is that which is mostly moist and a little of it is unripe, and the 'busr muthannab' is the opposite. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not break the oath with moist dates by the unripe muthannab, nor with unripe dates by the moist muthannab, because the moist dates are moist, and the unripe muthannab is called unripe, so it is as if the oath was on buying. And he (Abu Hanifa) has (the argument) that the moist muthannab has a little unripe in its stalk, and the unripe muthannab is the opposite, so eating it is eating the unripe and the moist, and each one is intended in eating, contrary to buying, for it encounters the whole and the little follows the much. And in most reliable books, Muhammad is with Abu Hanifa. The summary of the issues is four: two agreeable and two disagreeable. The agreeable two: when he swears not to eat moist dates and eats a moist muthannab, and when he swears not to eat unripe dates and eats an unripe muthannab, he breaks the oath in both by agreement. The disagreeable two: when he swears not to eat moist dates and eats an unripe muthannab, and when he swears not to eat unripe dates and eats a moist muthannab, then he breaks the oath according to them (the two) contrary to Abu Yusuf. His saying: (He does not break the oath by buying a bunch of unripe dates that has moist dates in it, when he swore not to buy moist dates) meaning if he swore not to buy moist dates and bought a bunch of unripe dates that has moist dates in it, he does not break the oath because buying encounters the whole and the overcome follows. And if the oath was on eating, he breaks the oath because eating encounters it piece by piece, so each one is intended. And it becomes like if he swore not to buy barley or not to eat, and bought wheat that has barley grains in it or ate them, he breaks the oath in eating but not in buying, as we presented. He said in al-Khaniyya: If he swore not to buy sheep (؟) and bought a slaughtered sheep, he is guilty; similarly if he swore not to buy a head (of cattle). And 'al-kibasa' - with a kasra on the kaf - is a bunch of dates, plural 'kaba'is'. He said in al-Tabyin: Contrary to if he made his oath on touching, for he breaks the oath in all cases because touching is actual in it and the name of the thing sworn by remains, unlike if he swore not to touch cotton or linen and then touched a garment made from them, for he does not break the oath due to the removal of the name of cotton and linen from it, so it becomes like one who swore not to eat clarified butter (samn) or buttermilk (zubd) or not to touch them, and then ate milk or touched it.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on matn rulings about oaths regarding eating dates at different stages of ripeness. The author first explains that the name 'rutab' (moist date) and 'tamr' (dried date) are considered one species due to minimal difference, unlike grapes and raisins which are a complete transformation. Thus, if one swears not to eat unripe dates (busr) and eats moist dates, he is not guilty because the name does not apply. Similarly, eating moist or dry versions of nuts and figs does not break an oath because the name encompasses both. The core issue is about 'muthannab' - a date that has both ripe and unripe portions. Abu Hanifa rules that eating a muthannab breaks an oath sworn on either 'rutab' or 'busr' because it partakes of both and eating is done piecemeal. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it does not break the oath, analogizing to buying where the whole is considered. There is also a disagreement: Muhammad reportedly sides with Abu Hanifa in some books. The commentator then discusses the distinction between oaths on eating versus buying: in buying, the whole object is considered, so a mixture follows the dominant part; in eating, each part is evaluated separately. Finally, examples are given about touching cotton/linen versus eating butter/milk, showing that the name of the original substance is lost after processing, so the oath is not broken unless the name remains.

Key Terms
رطب (rutab): Moist or ripe fresh dates just picked from the palm.
بسر (busr): Unripe dates that are still hard and green/red.
مذنب (muthannab): A date that has both ripe and unripe portions, with a trace of the other at its stalk.
كباسة (kibasa): A bunch of dates on a stem; the whole cluster.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the two 'disagreeable' cases (oath on rutab/eating busr muthannab; oath on busr/eating rutab muthannab), what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa?
A) Hanth occurs in both cases.
B) Hanth does not occur in either case.
C) Hanth occurs only when the muthannab is mostly busr.
D) Hanth occurs only when the date is a pure muthannab of the sworn variety.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths on eating, a muthannab date breaks the oath because each part is separately consumed, even if the minority component is small.

Oath on Eating Meat: Fish, Pork, Human, and Organs
Explains custom-based interpretation of 'meat' in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given food item breaks an oath not to eat meat, and apply the Hanafi rule that oaths are interpreted by custom (`urf) rather than literal definitions.

Core Idea
Custom overrides literal blood origin: fish, pork, human do not break a meat oath.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical fiqh question: if someone swears off meat, do they break their oath by eating fish, pork, or organ meats? The author navigates between literal definitions (based on blood origin) and the overriding principle that oaths are interpreted by custom. You will learn the relied-upon fatwa and the intra-Hanafi debate on whether practical custom can restrict general wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that the Hanafi school interprets oaths based on custom, not linguistic or literal reality. The passage repeatedly emphasizes that oaths are built on `urf.
This principle overrides even Quranic usage that might otherwise define a term.
2
Examine the fish case
Even though the Qur'an calls fish meat (lahm), custom does not. The author critiques al-Hidayah's metaphorical interpretation, offering instead the argument that fish has no blood, so it is not meat literally. But the decisive factor is custom, as shown by the 'aliyah (fat tail) example.
3
Analyze the blood origin argument
The literalist view that meat stems from blood is weakened by the 'aliyah: it congeals from blood yet is not called meat customarily. Analogies with 'dabbah' and 'peg' in the Qur'an show that custom can restrict general terms.
4
Study pork, human, and organs
For these items, some Hanafis (like the author of al-Tabyin) argue that they are meat because they originate from blood. However, the relied-upon fatwa (from al-`Attabi and al-Kafi) holds that custom excludes them; the oath is not broken.
Regional custom varies for liver and spleen: in Kufa they are considered meat, elsewhere not.
5
Understand the usul dispute
Al-Tabyin argues that only verbal custom restricts general words, not practical custom. But Fath al-Qadir refutes this, citing Hanafi usul that habit (practical custom) can indeed restrict reality.
This dispute affects whether the customist or literalist view is adopted.
6
Apply regional variation
The passage notes that if the swearer is from Khwarazm, where fish is called meat, then eating fish breaks the oath. Similarly, organ meats depend on local custom. Always check the `urf of the swearer's community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً) أي لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً لا يحنث بأكل لحم السمك وإن سماه الله تعالى لحماً في القرآن للعرف، وقد قدمنا أن الأيمان مبنية عليه لا على الحقيقة وهو أولى مما في الهداية من التسمية التي وقعت في القرآن مجازية لا حقيقية لأن اللحم منشؤه من الدم ولا دم في السمك لسكونه في الماء ولذا حل بلا ذكاة فإنه ينتقض بالألية تنعقد من الدم، ولا يحنث بأكلها لمكان العرف وهي أنها لا تسمى لحماً. وأيضاً يمنع أن اسم اللحم باعتبار الانعقاد من الدم لا باعتبار الالتحام ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يركب دابة فركب كافراً أو لا يجلس على وتد فجلس على جبل أنه لا يحنث مع تسميتها في القرآن دابة وأوتاداً وهذا كله إذا لم ينو، أما إذا نواه فأكل سمكاً طرياً أو مالحاً يحنث . وفي المحيط : وفي الأيمان يعتبر العرف في كل موضع حتى قالوا: لو كان الخالف خوارزمياً فأكل لحم السمك يحنث لأنهم يسمونه لحماً، ولو حلف لا يشتري خبزاً فاشترى خبز الأرز لا يحنث إلا أن يكون بطبرستان اهـ.
قوله: (ولحم الخنزير والإنسان والكبد والكرش لحم لأن منشأ هذه الأشياء الدم فصارت لحماً حقيقة فيحنث بأكلها في حلفه لا يأكل لحماً وإن كان لحم الخنزير والآدمي حراماً لأن اليمين قد تنعقد لمنع النفس عن الحرام كما لو حلف لا يزني أو لا يكذب تصح يمينه، وكذا يدخل في العموم ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يشرب شراباً يدخل فيه الخمر حتى تلزمه الكفارة بشربها لكونها شراباً حقيقة، ووجوب الكفارة في اليمين ليس لعينها بل لمعنى في غيرها وهو هتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى ولا يختلف ذلك بين أن تكون يمينه على الطاعة أو على المعصية، وصحح الإمام العتابي أنه لا يحنث بأكل لحم الخنزير والآدمي. وقال في الكافي : وعليه الفتوى اعتباراً للعرف وهذا هو الحق، وما في التبيين من أنه عرف عملي لا يصلح مقيد اللفظ بخلاف العرف اللفظي ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يركب دابة لا يحنث بالركوب على الإنسان للعرف اللفظي لأن اللفظ عرفاً لا يتناول إلا الكراع وإن كان في اللغة يتناوله. ولو حلف لا يركب حيواناً يحنث بالركوب على الإنسان لأن اللفظ يتناول جميع الحيوانات والعرف العملي وهو أنه لا يركب عادة لا يصلح مقيداً .اهـ. فقد رده في فتح القدير بأنه غير صحيح لتصريح أهل الأصول بقولهم الحقيقة تترك بدلالة العادة إذ ليست العادة إلا عرفاً عملياً، ولم يجب عن الفرق بين الدابة والحيوان وهي واردة عليه إن سلمها. وفي الخلاصة : لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً فأكل شيئاً من البطون كالكبد والطحال يحنث في عرف أهل الكوفة، وفي عرفنا لا يحنث وهكذا في المحيط والمجتبى، ولا يخفى أنه لا لحماً يسمى قوله : (التصريح أهل الأصول بقولهم الخ) قال في النهر : وفي بحث التخصيص من التحرير مسألة العادة العرف العملي مخصص عند الحنفية خلافاً للشافعية كحرمة الطعام وعادتهم أكل البر انصرف إليه وهو الوجه أما بالعرف القولي فاتفاق كالدابة للحمار والدراهم على النقد الغالب. وفي
His saying: (And with fish, in (the case of) not eating meat) i.e., if he swears not to eat meat, he does not break the oath by eating fish meat, even though Allah called it meat in the Qur'an, due to custom. And we have already presented that oaths are based on it (custom), not on reality. And this is better than what is in al-Hidayah, that the name that occurred in the Qur'an is metaphorical, not literal, because meat originates from blood, and there is no blood in fish due to its dwelling in water, and therefore it is permissible without slaughter. For it is refuted by the fat tail (aliyah) which congeals from blood, and yet one does not break the oath by eating it because of custom that it is not called meat. Also, it is prevented that the name meat is due to congealing from blood, not due to being flesh. Do you not see that if he swears not to ride a 'dabbah' (riding animal) and rides an infidel, or not to sit on a peg and sits on a mountain, he does not break the oath despite their being called 'dabbah' and 'awtad' (pegs) in the Qur'an? All this is if he did not intend (to include them). But if he intended them, then he ate fresh or salted fish, he breaks the oath. And in al-Muhit: In oaths, custom is considered in every place, such that they said: If the swearer is a Khwarazmian, and he eats fish meat, he breaks the oath because they call it meat. And if he swears not to buy bread and buys rice bread, he does not break the oath unless he is in Tabaristan. End quote. His saying: (And the meat of pig, human, liver, and stomach is meat because the origin of these things is blood, so they are meat literally, so he breaks the oath by eating them in his oath not to eat meat, even though pig and human meat are haram, because an oath may be made to prevent oneself from haram, just as if he swears not to commit zina or not to lie, his oath is valid. Similarly, it enters under the general (term). Do you not see that if he swears not to drink any beverage, wine enters it such that kaffarah is obligatory upon him by drinking it because it is a beverage literally, and the obligation of kaffarah in an oath is not for its own sake but for a meaning outside it, which is violating the sanctity of Allah's name, and this does not differ whether his oath is on obedience or disobedience. And Imam al-`Attabi authenticated that he does not break the oath by eating pig and human meat. And he said in al-Kafi: And upon it is the fatwa, considering custom, and this is the truth. And what is in al-Tabyin, that it is a practical custom that does not qualify to restrict the wording unlike verbal custom, do you not see that if he swears not to ride a 'dabbah', he does not break the oath by riding on a human due to verbal custom, because the word customarily only refers to hooved animals even though in language it includes him. And if he swears not to ride an animal, he breaks the oath by riding on a human because the word includes all animals, and practical custom, which is that one does not usually ride it, does not qualify to restrict. End quote. It was refuted in Fath al-Qadir as being incorrect due to the statement of the scholars of usul: 'The reality is abandoned by the indication of habit, for habit is nothing but practical custom.' And he did not answer the difference between 'dabbah' and 'hayawan', which is an objection upon him if he concedes it. And in al-Khulasa: If he swears not to eat meat and eats something from the intestines like liver and spleen, he breaks the oath in the custom of the people of Kufa, and in our custom he does not break the oath. And similarly in al-Muhit and al-Mujtaba. And it is not hidden that it is not called meat. His saying: (The statement of the scholars of usul: etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And in the discussion of takhsis from al-Tahrir, the issue of habit (al-`adah): practical custom is a mukhassis according to the Hanafis, contrary to the Shafi'is, like the prohibition of food and their habit of eating wheat, it is diverted to it and that is the correct view. As for verbal custom, it is agreed upon, like 'dabbah' for donkey and dirhams for the prevalent coinage. And in...
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on oaths about eating, focusing on what is considered 'meat' (lahm). The author first addresses fish: even though the Qur'an calls fish meat (Surah al-Nahl 16:14), oaths are based on custom, not literal Quranic usage. Since people customarily do not call fish 'meat', an oath not to eat meat is not broken by eating fish unless the swearer intended to include fish. The commentator criticizes al-Hidayah's claim that the Quranic usage is metaphorical, arguing instead that the literal meaning of meat is that which originates from blood; fish has no blood, so it is not meat literally. He draws an analogy: if one swears not to ride a 'dabbah' and rides a human, he is not guilty because custom restricts 'dabbah' to hooved animals, even though the language could include humans. Similarly, an oath not to sit on a peg is not broken by sitting on a mountain, despite the Quranic metaphor. The key is whether the swearer had a specific intention to include such items. Next, the passage discusses pork, human flesh, liver, and stomach. According to the literalists, these are all meat because they originate from blood, so the oath is broken even if the substance is haram. However, Imam al-`Attabi and the relied-upon fatwa (in al-Kafi) differ, holding that custom excludes pork and human from the term 'meat', so the oath is not broken. The commentator then recounts a dispute about whether practical custom (al-`urf al-`amali) can restrict a general term. Al-Tabyin argued that only verbal custom restricts, not practical custom. This is refuted by the Hanafi usul position that habit (al-`adah) can delimit reality. The passage ends with a note that in Kufa, liver and spleen are called meat, so an oath is broken; in other regions, they are not. This illustrates that custom varies regionally and is always considered in oaths.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): Meat as the substance of animal flesh, but in oaths it is interpreted by customary usage, not literal definition.
عرف عملي (urf 'amali): Practical custom: habitual practice that may restrict the application of a term even if not verbally expressed.
عرف لفظي (urf lafzi): Verbal custom: a word's customary meaning in common speech, which can limit its linguistic scope.
ألية (aliyah): The fat tail of a sheep, which congeals from blood and is considered meat by origin but not customarily called meat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats pork, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because pork is literally meat.
B) He does not break his oath because custom does not consider pork as 'meat'.
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended pork specifically.
D) The oath is invalid because the action is haram.

Key Takeaway
Oaths are interpreted by custom, not literal definitions; what counts as 'meat' depends on regional `urf, not blood origin.

Interpretation of Oaths: The Role of Custom (Urf) in Defining 'Meat'
Ruling on what constitutes meat in oaths based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that oaths are interpreted according to custom, not literal meaning, and identify how regional custom and intention affect the ruling on eating fish after swearing not to eat meat.

Core Idea
Oaths are based on custom, not literal definitions – fish is not meat unless local custom says so.

When someone swears not to eat meat, does eating fish break the oath? The answer depends not on the Quranic usage (Allah calls fish 'meat') but on what people commonly call meat. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the Hanafi rule that custom governs oaths, and refines an earlier argument from al-Hidayah by emphasizing that the Quranic usage does not override common custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
What constitutes 'meat' (lahm) in the context of an oath? The swearer said 'I will not eat meat' – now eats fish. Does he break the oath?
2
Recognize the key principle
Hanafi law states that oaths are built upon custom (urf), not upon literal or Quranic definitions. The author asserts this principle explicitly.
The principle: 'al-ayman mabniyyah ala al-urf'.
3
Apply the principle to fish
In common custom, fish is not called 'meat' (lahm), so eating fish does not break the oath. Even though the Quran calls fish 'lahman', custom overrides.
Contrast with liver and tripe, which are considered meat because they originate from blood.
4
Understand the refinement over al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah claimed the Quranic usage is metaphorical. The author says it is better to say oaths are based on custom, not that the Quran is metaphorical. He gives examples (dabba, watad) where Quranic usage does not affect oath interpretation.
The author argues that the explanation based on custom is more consistent.
5
Note the exceptions
If the swearer intended to include fish in his oath, he breaks it. Also, regional custom can change the ruling: in Khwarazm, fish is called meat, so eating fish would break the oath. Similarly, rice bread is not bread unless local custom in Tabaristan says so.
Intention (niyyah) and regional 'urf can override the default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكبد والكرش لحم وبشحم الظهر في شحماً وبالية في الحماً وشحماً وبالخبز في هذا البر وفي أن لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً لا يحنث بأكل لحم السمك وإن سماه الله تعالى لحماً في القرآن للعرف، وقد قدمنا أن الأيمان مبنية عليه لا على الحقيقة وهو أولى مما في الهداية من التسمية التي وقعت في القرآن مجازية لا حقيقية لأن اللحم منشؤه من الدم ولا دم في السمك لسكونه في الماء ولذا حل بلا ذكاة فإنه ينتقض بالألية تنعقد من الدم، ولا يحنث بأكلها لمكان العرف وهي أنها لا تسمى لحماً. وأيضاً يمنع أن اسم اللحم باعتبار الانعقاد من الدم لا باعتبار الالتحام ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يركب دابة فركب كافراً أو لا يجلس على وتد فجلس على جبل أنه لا يحنث مع تسميتها في القرآن دابة وأوتاداً وهذا كله إذا لم ينو، أما إذا نواه فأكل سمكاً طرياً أو مالحاً يحنث . وفي المحيط : وفي الأيمان يعتبر العرف في كل موضع حتى قالوا: لو كان الخالف خوارزمياً فأكل لحم السمك يحنث لأنهم يسمونه لحماً، ولو حلف لا يشتري خبزاً فاشترى خبز الأرز لا يحنث إلا أن يكون بطبرستان اهـ.
Liver and tripe are meat. And by back fat, [if he swore not to eat] fat, [he does not break his oath]. And by dried meat, [it is] meat and fat. And by bread, [he means] this wheat [bread]. And regarding if one swears not to eat meat, he does not break his oath by eating fish meat, even though Allah called it meat in the Quran, due to custom. We have already stated that oaths are based on custom, not literal meaning. This is preferable to what is in al-Hidayah, that the naming in the Quran is metaphorical, not literal, because meat originates from blood, and there is no blood in fish due to its living in water, and therefore it is permissible without slaughter. This is contradicted by [the example of] the tail (alya): it coagulates from blood, yet one does not break his oath by eating it due to custom, because it is not called meat. Also, it is prevented that the name meat is based on coagulation from blood, not on binding together. Do you not see that if one swears not to ride a beast (dabba) and rides a human, or not to sit on a peg (watad) and sits on a mountain, he does not break his oath despite the Quran calling them dabba and awtad? All this is if he did not intend [otherwise]; but if he intended [meat], then he breaks his oath by eating fresh or salted fish. In al-Muhit: In oaths, custom is considered in every place. Thus they said: if the swearer is from Khwarazm, he breaks his oath by eating fish because they call it meat. And if one swears not to buy bread and buys rice bread, he does not break his oath unless he is in Tabaristan.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the definition of 'meat' (lahm) in the context of oaths. The author establishes the fundamental Hanafi principle that oaths are interpreted according to custom (urf), not literal linguistic meaning. Even though the Quran calls fish 'lahman' (meat), a person who swears not to eat meat does not break his oath by eating fish because in common usage, fish is not referred to as meat. This is supported by examples: a person does not break an oath not to ride a 'dabba' by riding a human, even though humans are called 'dawabb' in the Quran; likewise, sitting on a mountain does not break an oath not to sit on a 'watad'. However, if the swearer intended to include fish in his oath, he would break it. Furthermore, custom can vary by region: if the swearer is from Khwarazm, where fish is commonly called meat, he does break his oath. Liver and tripe are considered meat because they originate from blood, so breaking the oath. The same applies to dried meat. The passage also notes that if one swears not to buy bread and buys rice bread, he does not break his oath unless the custom in Tabaristan considers rice bread as bread.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or one of His attributes
العرف (al-urf): Customary usage of language among people, which takes precedence over literal definitions in oaths
لحم (lahm): Meat from animals that have blood; fish and tripe? etc. are excluded by custom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author consider the explanation based on custom to be preferable to al-Hidayah's metaphorical explanation?
A) Because the Quranic verse is ambiguous.
B) Because the tail (alya) is not called meat despite coming from blood.
C) Because fish has no blood.
D) Because the example of dabba and watad proves oaths follow literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to common custom (urf), not literal or Quranic definitions, and regional variations must be considered.

Pork, Human Flesh, and Oaths: Does Custom Override Literal Meaning?
Dispute on whether pork and human meat break an oath not to eat meat, resolved by custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether eating pork breaks an oath not to eat meat, and apply the principle that custom (urf) can override literal meaning in oaths.

Core Idea
Custom determines what counts as 'meat' in oaths, not literal origin from blood.

When someone swears not to eat meat, what counts as 'meat'? The literalist view says anything originating from blood, including pork and human flesh. But the preferred Hanafi view relies on custom: in common usage, these are not called meat. This lesson unpacks the dispute and shows how custom (urf) becomes a decisive factor in oath rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal view
State the literal position: pork and human flesh are meat because they originate from blood. This is supported by Zahir al-Riwayah.
The literal view does not distinguish between halal and haram meats.
2
Recognize the custom-based counter-argument
Imam al-Atabi and al-Kafi hold that custom (urf) overrides the literal meaning. In common usage, pork and human flesh are not considered meat for oaths.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
Understand the distinction between verbal and practical custom
Al-Tabyin argues that only verbal custom (urf lafzi) can restrict a general word, not practical custom (urf 'amali). Fath al-Qadir refutes this, citing usul scholars that even practical custom abandons the literal meaning.
The dispute within the school hinges on which type of custom applies.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the literal view, eating pork would require kaffarah for breaking the oath. Under the custom-based view, no kaffarah is due because the oath is not broken.
This is the core consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولحم الخنزير والإنسان والكبد والكرش لحم لأن منشأ هذه الأشياء الدم فصارت لحماً حقيقة فيحنث بأكلها في حلفه لا يأكل لحماً وإن كان لحم الخنزير والآدمي حراماً لأن اليمين قد تنعقد لمنع النفس عن الحرام كما لو حلف لا يزني أو لا يكذب تصح يمينه، وكذا يدخل في العموم ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يشرب شراباً يدخل فيه الخمر حتى تلزمه الكفارة بشربها لكونها شراباً حقيقة، ووجوب الكفارة في اليمين ليس لعينها بل لمعنى في غيرها وهو هتك حرمة اسم الله تعالى ولا يختلف ذلك بين أن تكون يمينه على الطاعة أو على المعصية، وصحح الإمام العتابي أنه لا يحنث بأكل لحم الخنزير والآدمي. وقال في الكافي : وعليه الفتوى اعتباراً للعرف وهذا هو الحق، وما في التبيين من أنه عرف عملي لا يصلح مقيد اللفظ بخلاف العرف اللفظي ألا ترى أنه لو حلف لا يركب دابة لا يحنث بالركوب على الإنسان للعرف اللفظي لأن اللفظ عرفاً لا يتناول إلا الكراع وإن كان في اللغة يتناوله. ولو حلف لا يركب حيواناً يحنث بالركوب على الإنسان لأن اللفظ يتناول جميع الحيوانات والعرف العملي وهو أنه لا يركب عادة لا يصلح مقيداً .اهـ. فقد رده في فتح القدير بأنه غير صحيح لتصريح أهل الأصول بقولهم الحقيقة تترك بدلالة العادة إذ ليست العادة إلا عرفاً عملياً، ولم يجب عن الفرق بين الدابة والحيوان وهي واردة عليه إن سلمها. وفي الخلاصة : لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً فأكل شيئاً من البطون كالكبد والطحال يحنث في عرف أهل الكوفة، وفي عرفنا لا يحنث وهكذا في المحيط والمجتبى، ولا يخفى أنه لا لحماً يسمى
His saying: (And pork and human flesh, and liver and tripe are meat) because the origin of these things is blood, so they become meat literally, so he breaks his oath by eating them if he swore not to eat meat, even though pork and human flesh are unlawful, because an oath can be made to prevent oneself from unlawful things, just as if he swears not to commit zina or not to lie, his oath is valid. Similarly, it enters under the general [term]: do you not see that if he swears not to drink a beverage, wine is included, so that he becomes liable for kaffarah by drinking it, because wine is literally a beverage. And the obligation of kaffarah in oaths is not for the act itself but for a meaning outside it, which is violating the sanctity of the name of Allah, and this does not differ whether his oath is on obedience or disobedience. Imam al-Atabi corrected that he does not break his oath by eating pork or human flesh. And it is stated in al-Kafi: And upon this is the fatwa, considering custom, and this is the truth. As for what is in al-Tabyin, that practical custom is not suitable to restrict the wording, unlike verbal custom — do you not see that if he swears not to ride a beast (dabba), he does not break his oath by riding a human due to verbal custom, because the word customarily only applies to hooved animals, even though linguistically it includes it. And if he swears not to ride an animal (hayawan), he breaks his oath by riding a human, because the word includes all animals, and practical custom (that he does not usually ride) is not suitable to restrict. End. Then Fath al-Qadir refuted this, saying it is incorrect due to the explicit statement of usul scholars: 'The literal meaning is abandoned by indication of custom', for custom is nothing but practical custom. And he did not answer the distinction between dabba and hayawan, which is contrary to him if he accepts it. And in al-Khulasa: If one swears not to eat meat and eats something from the stomach like liver and spleen, he breaks his oath in the custom of the people of Kufa, but in our custom he does not break his oath. Similarly in al-Muhit and al-Mujtaba. And it is not hidden that it is not called meat.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute regarding whether pork and human flesh break an oath not to eat meat. The literal ruling, based on the origin from blood, is that they are meat and thus break the oath. This is supported by the principle that an oath to avoid sin is valid and its kaffarah is due because the sanctity of Allah's name is violated. However, Imam al-Atabi and the author of al-Kafi give fatwa that one does not break his oath due to custom (urf): in common usage, pork and human flesh are not considered meat for consumption. The passage then contrasts this with the ruling in al-Tabyin, which distinguishes between verbal custom (urf lafzi) and practical custom (urf 'amali), arguing that only verbal custom can restrict a general word. Fath al-Qadir refutes this, citing usul scholars that even practical custom (i.e., al-ada) can override the literal meaning. The passage ends with a note from al-Khulasa that the ruling on liver and tripe also depends on custom: in Kufa it breaks the oath, in other regions it does not.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-urf al-'amali): Practical custom: what is commonly done, as opposed to what is commonly said.
العرف اللفظي (al-urf al-lafzi): Verbal custom: common linguistic usage, which is agreed to be a valid determinant in oaths.
الكفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath: feeding/ clothing ten poor people, freeing a slave, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-mu'tamad) in this passage, does a person who swears not to eat meat break his oath by eating pork?
A) Yes, because pork literally comes from blood.
B) No, because pork is not considered meat by custom.
C) Yes, because the oath includes all meat.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, custom overrides literal meaning in oaths; pork and human flesh are not considered 'meat' for oath purposes.

Swearing Not to Eat Meat: Does Raw Meat Break the Oath?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of 'meat' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether raw meat breaks an oath not to eat meat and identify the preferred view.

Core Idea
Raw meat does not break an oath to abstain from meat according to the preferred Hanafi view.

When a person swears not to eat meat, the default meaning typically includes cooked meat. But what about raw meat? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: does eating raw meat constitute breaking such an oath? The answer has practical implications for everyday oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its subject
The oath is 'not to eat meat.' We need to determine what qualifies as 'meat' in the context of oaths. Common usage ('urf) is key.
Meat is normally understood as cooked, but raw meat may be ambiguous.
2
Locate the indication from al-Asl
Imam Muhammad in al-Asl lists cooked, roasted, and dried meat as breaking the oath, implying that raw meat is excluded.
This is a qarinah (indication) that the default meaning does not include raw meat.
3
State the two positions
Abu Bakr al-Iskaf holds that raw meat does not break the oath (no hanth) — this is considered al-azhar. Abu al-Layth holds that it does break the oath (hanth).
Both are Hanafi scholars; the dispute is within the school.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preference)
The later authorities (al-Zahiriyya and others) prefer the view of no hanth, calling it al-ashbah. Thus the mufta bihi is that raw meat does not break the oath.
Tarjih is based on the strength of the indication from al-Asl and the endorsement of al-Iskaf.
5
Apply the broth ruling
The passage also clarifies that broth from meat does not break the oath unless the swearer specifically intended broth when swearing.
This is distinguished from meat itself; intention matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة وغيرها : لو جلف لا يأكل لحماً حنث بأكل لحم الإبل والبقر والغنم ،والطيور، مطبوخاً كان أو مشوياً أو قديداً كما ذكره في الأصل. فهذا من محمد إشارة إلى أنه لا يحنث بالني. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث عن أبي بكر الإسكاف أنه لا يحنث وهو الأظهر، ، وعند الفقيه أبي الليث يحنث. ولو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللحم شيئاً فأكل من مرقته لم يحنث إن لم يكن له نية المرقة .اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : الأشبه أنه لا يحنث بأكل النيء.
And in al-Khulasa and others: If one swears not to eat meat, he breaks his oath by eating camel, cow, sheep, and bird meat, whether cooked, roasted, or jerked (qadid), as mentioned in al-Asl. This from Muhammad indicates that he does not break his oath by raw meat. And in Fatawa of Abu al-Layth from Abu Bakr al-Iskaf: he does not break his oath, and this is the more apparent. And according to al-Faqih Abu al-Layth: he breaks his oath. And if he swears not to eat any of this meat, and eats from its broth, he does not break his oath if he did not intend the broth. End. And in al-Zahiriyya: The closest is that he does not break his oath by eating raw meat.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on raw meat in oaths. Imam Muhammad in al-Asl states that one breaks the oath by eating cooked, roasted, or dried meat, implying that raw meat does not break the oath. This is supported by Abu Bakr al-Iskaf and considered the more apparent view (al-azhar). However, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth holds that raw meat does break the oath. The passage also clarifies that eating broth from the meat does not break the oath unless one specifically intended broth. The final preference in al-Zahiriyya is that raw meat does not break the oath.

Key Terms
النيء (al-ni'): Raw, uncooked meat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes.
B) No.
C) Only if it is beef.
D) Only if he intended raw meat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths not to eat meat, raw meat is not included by default; only cooked, roasted, or dried meat breaks the oath.

Custom in Oaths: Animal Names
Detailed rulings on animal names in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom (urf) governs the interpretation of animal names in oaths, and distinguish between literal generic meaning and customary usage.

Core Idea
Custom overrides literal generic meaning in oaths—sheep does not include goat, cow does not include buffalo.

Oaths involving animal names can lead to confusion when a person swears by a generic term but eats a specific type. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the Hanafi school relies on local custom to determine whether the oath is broken. You will learn the rulings for sheep, goat, cow, buffalo, chicken, and other animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal term in the oath
Start by isolating the exact word used in the oath, e.g., 'shah', 'baqara', 'dajaj'.
The term may be generic or specific; note its lexical category.
2
Check literal generic meaning
Determine if the term is a genus (jins) that includes multiple subtypes in the Arabic language.
Literal meaning may be broad, e.g., shah can include both sheep and goats lexically.
3
Apply customary distinction
Investigate whether local custom (urf) differentiates between the subtypes. If yes, the oath applies only to the customary meaning.
The fatwa follows Abu al-Layth that custom distinguishes sheep from goat.
4
Determine if the oath is broken
If the forbidden food falls under the customary meaning, the oath is broken; otherwise not.
Same logic applies to cow vs buffalo, where custom excludes buffalo.
5
Extend to other animals
Use the same principles for chicken (dajaj covers both sexes), camels (generic terms cover all types), etc.
Note the special rule: head and trotters are 'meat' for eating oaths but not for purchase oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : حلف لا يأكل لحم شاة فأكل لحم عنز يحنث لأن الشاة اسم جنس فيتناول الشاة أي الضأن وغيرها وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في نوازله أنه لا يحنث، سواء كان الحالف قروياً أو مصرياً، وعليه الفتوى لأنهم يفرقون بينهما عادة. ولو حلف لا يأكل لحم بقرة لم يحنث بأكل لحم الجاموس لأنه وإن كان بقراً يعد في نصاب البقر ولكن خرج من اليمين بتعارف الناس .اهـ. وفي الخانية والرأس والأكارع لحم في يمين الأكل وليس بلحم في يمين الشراء .اهـ. وفي البدائع : حلف لا يأكل لحم دجاج فأكل لحم ديك حنث لأن الدجاج اسم للذكر والأنثى جميعاً فأما الدجاجة فاسم للأنثى والديك اسم الذكر، واسم الإبل يقع على الذكور والإناث، وكذا اسم الجمل والبعير والجزور، وهذه الأربعة تقع على البخاتي والعراب واسم البقر يقع على الأنثى والذكر كالشاة والغنم والنعجة اسم للأنثى والكبش للذكر والفرس لهما كالبغل والبغلة والحمار للذكر والحمارة والأتان للأنثى.
And in al-Muhit: If one swears not to eat the meat of a shah (sheep) and eats the meat of a goat (anz), he breaks his oath because shah is a generic noun that includes sheep (da'n) and others. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in his Nawazil that he does not break his oath, whether the swearer is a villager or city-dweller, and upon this is the fatwa, because people customarily distinguish between them. And if one swears not to eat the meat of a cow (baqara), he does not break his oath by eating buffalo (jamus) meat, because although buffalo is bovine and counts in the nisab of cows, it is excluded from the oath by people's custom. End. And in al-Khaniyya: The head (ra's) and trotters (akari') are meat in an oath of eating, but not meat in an oath of buying. End. And in al-Bada'i: If one swears not to eat chicken (dajaj) meat and eats rooster (dik) meat, he breaks his oath because dajaj is a name for both male and female. As for dajaja, it is a name for the female, and dik for the male. The name ibil (camels) applies to males and females, likewise jamal, ba'ir, and jazur; these four apply to bukhati (Bactrian) and 'irab (Arabian). The name baqar (cattle) applies to female and male, like shah and ghanam. Na'ja is a name for the female, kabsh for the male. Faras (horse) applies to both, like baghl (mule) and baghla (female mule). Himar (donkey) for the male, himara and atan for the female.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on the interpretation of animal names in oaths, based on customary usage. It highlights that the word 'shah' (sheep) in oaths generally includes both sheep and goats, but a fatwa from Abu al-Layth says that custom distinguishes them, so one does not break his oath by eating goat if he swore off 'shah'. Similarly, 'baqara' (cow) does not include buffalo by custom. The passage also notes that in oaths, the head and trotters of animals are considered meat for the purpose of eating but not for buying. Then it explains the gender and species classifications: 'dajaj' includes both chicken and rooster, while 'dajaja' and 'dik' are specific. For camels, 'ibil' is generic, while 'jamal', 'ba'ir', and 'jazur' all refer to camels regardless of type. 'Baqar' includes males and females. 'Shah' and 'ghanam' are generic, while 'na'ja' is female sheep, 'kabsh' is male. 'Faras' is generic for horse, etc.

Key Terms
الشاة (al-shaah): Sheep, but can include goats in some contexts; custom may differentiate
الدجاج (al-dajaaj): Chicken as a species, includes both male and female

Key Takeaway
Custom overrides literal generic meaning in Hanafi oath interpretation for animal names.

Swearing Not to Eat Fat: Back Fat vs. Belly Fat
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the classification of back fat in oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on back fat in oaths and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding its classification as fat or meat.

Core Idea
Back fat is meat to Abu Hanifa, fat to the Sahibayn – the oath is not broken.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, on whether back fat is considered 'fat' (shahm) in an oath not to eat fat. Understanding this dispute requires careful linguistic and legal analysis: what defines a substance as 'fat' versus 'meat' in Hanafi law? The answer has practical consequences for one's oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is swearing not to eat fat (shahm) and then eating back fat (shahm al-zahr). The dispute: does this break the oath?
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: no oath broken (back fat is meat). Sahibayn: oath broken (back fat is fat).
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa: back fat originates from blood, is used as meat, and provides strength; consensus that it breaks an oath on meat supports this. Sahibayn: it melts by fire, a property of fat.
4
Apply Qadi al-Isbijabi's refinement
If back fat means kidney fat (shahm al-kulya), Sahibayn's view is more evident; if it means fat attached to meat (shahm al-lahm), Abu Hanifa's view is more evident.
This nuance reconciles the two positions.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, supported by al-Tahawi, Malik, and al-Shafi'i (in the most correct view).
Fatawa are given accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبشحم الظهر في شحماً) أي لو حلف لا يأكل شحماً فأكل شحم الظهر لا يحنث فهو معطوف على قوله وبسمك وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا يحنث لوجود خاصية الشحم فيه وهو الذوب بالنار . وله أنه لحم حقيقة ألا ترى أنه ينشأ من الدم ويستعمل استعماله ويحصل به قوته ولهذا يحنث بأكله في اليمين على أكل اللحم إجماعاً كما في المحيط، ولا يحنث ببيعه في اليمين على بيع الشحم . قال القاضي الاسبيجابي : إن أريد بشحم الظهر شحم الكلية فقولهما أظهر وإن أريد به شحم اللحم فقوله أظهر اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : صحح غير واحد قول أبي حنيفة وذكر الطحاوي قول محمد مع أبي حنيفة وهو قول مالك والشافعي في الأصح. وقيد بشحم الظهر لأنه يحنث بشحم البطن اتفاقاً. وذكر في الكافي أن الشحوم أربعة : شحم البطن، وشحم الظهر، وشحم مختلط بالعظم، وشحم على الحواشي
His saying: (And by back fat, [if he swore] to fat), i.e., if he swears not to eat fat and eats back fat, he does not break his oath. So it is conjoined to his saying 'and by fish' and this is according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: he breaks his oath because the characteristic of fat exists in it, namely melting by fire. And for him [Abu Hanifa] it is essentially meat; do you not see that it originates from blood and is used as meat and gives strength? For this reason, one breaks his oath by eating it in an oath on eating meat by consensus, as in al-Muhit, and does not break his oath by selling it in an oath on selling fat. Qadi al-Isbijabi said: If by back fat is meant the fat of the kidney, then their view (Sahibayn) is more evident; if it means the fat of the meat, then his view (Abu Hanifa) is more evident. In Fath al-Qadir: More than one has corrected Abu Hanifa's view, and al-Tahawi mentioned Muhammad's view with Abu Hanifa, and it is the view of Malik and al-Shafi'i in the most correct. And it is restricted to back fat because one breaks his oath by belly fat by agreement. And it is mentioned in al-Kafi that fats are four: belly fat, back fat, fat mixed with bone, and fat on the edges.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute regarding whether back fat (shahm al-zahr) is considered fat (shahm) in oaths. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is not considered fat because it is actually meat (lahm): it originates from blood, is used as meat, and provides strength. Therefore, if one swears not to eat fat and eats back fat, he does not break his oath. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) consider it fat because it has the property of fat, i.e., it melts by fire. Therefore, in their view, he does break his oath. Qadi al-Isbijabi clarifies that if 'back fat' refers specifically to the fat of the kidney (shahm al-kulya), then Sahibayn's view is more evident; if it refers to the fat attached to meat (shahm al-lahm), then Abu Hanifa's view is more evident. Al-Kafi states there are four types of fat: belly fat (which is agreed to be fat), back fat (disputed), fat mixed with bone, and fat on the edges. The passage also notes that by consensus, belly fat breaks the oath.

Key Terms
شحم الظهر (shahm al-zahr): Back fat, which is disputed: some consider it meat, others consider it fat
شحم البطن (shahm al-batn): Belly fat, agreed to be fat in oaths

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He breaks his oath.
B) He does not break his oath.
C) He breaks his oath only if it is kidney fat.
D) He breaks his oath only if it is meat fat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, back fat is considered meat, not fat, so an oath not to eat fat is not broken by eating back fat.

Practical Custom Restricts General Oaths
Clarification that practical custom can restrict a general word in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that practical custom can specify a general word in oaths and state the ruling when a literal interpretation would conflict with customary usage.

Core Idea
Custom restricts the general — an oath on ‘animal’ only applies to riding animals.

When a person swears using a general term, does the literal meaning always prevail? Not necessarily. This lesson examines a ruling from the Hanafi school where practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali) overrides the literal scope of a word in oaths. We explore why riding a human does not break an oath that forbids riding 'an animal', and how this resolves a dispute among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general term and its literal scope
The oath contains the word 'animal' (hayawan). Literally, this includes all living beings that move, including humans. Recognize that the literal scope is very broad.
In Hanafi usul, general words (amm) are binding until specified (takhsis). Here, the specification comes from custom.
2
Determine the applicable custom ('urf)
The practical custom ('urf 'amali) among people is that when they say 'riding an animal', they mean riding beasts of burden like horses, donkeys, camels, etc., not humans. This custom is well-known and consistent.
The passage cites some scholars of Balkh who accept 'urf 'amali as a restrictor (muqayyid).
3
Apply the principle that custom specifies the general
In Hanafi usul, when the literal meaning is used but a metaphorical or customary meaning is prevalent, the customary meaning can specify (takhsis) the general. Thus, the oath is understood as 'I will not ride a riding animal'.
This principle is found in books of usul, as the passage mentions.
4
Derive the ruling
Since riding a human is not included in the customary meaning, the act does not break the oath. The swearer does not incur any penalty.
This resolves the earlier objection from Fath al-Qadir, which had considered the branch invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السعدية : إن العرف العملي يصلح مقيداً عند بعض مشايخ بلخ لما ذكر في كتب الأصول في مسألة إذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة والمجاز متعارفاً .اهـ. وهذه النقول تؤذن بأنه لا يحنث بركوب الآدمي في لا يركب حيواناً فإيراد الفرع على ما في الفتح كما في البحر غير وارد لأن العادة حيث كانت مخصصة انصرفت يمينه إلى ما يركب عادة فتدبر .
Al-Sa'diyya: Practical custom is suitable as a restrictor according to some scholars of Balkh, as mentioned in books of usul in the case when the literal meaning is used and the metaphorical is customary. End. And these transmissions indicate that he does not break his oath by riding a human in [the oath] 'I will not ride an animal'. So the branch applied to what is in al-Fath, as in al-Bahr, is not valid, because when custom is a specifier, his oath turns to what he usually rides. So ponder.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This final passage supports the view that practical custom (urf 'amali) can restrict a general word in oaths, contrary to the earlier argument from al-Tabyin. It cites the authority of some scholars of Balkh. The conclusion is that if one swears not to ride an animal (hayawan), he does not break his oath by riding a human, because customary practice restricts 'animal' to riding animals. This resolves the earlier objection raised in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-'urf al-'amali): Practical custom: consistent, widespread practices of people, not mere speech.
مقيد (muqayyid): Restrictor: something that limits a general ruling to a specific instance.
مخصص (mukhassis): Specifier: evidence that restricts the scope of a general term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does riding a human not break an oath that says 'I will not ride an animal'?
A) Because humans are not animals according to the Shari'ah.
B) Because the scholars of Balkh said so, and their opinion is always followed.
C) Because practical custom restricts the word 'animal' to riding animals.
D) Because riding a human is less harmful than riding an animal.

Key Takeaway
Practical custom can restrict the literal meaning of general words in oaths, so that riding a human does not violate an oath against riding an animal.
