Raw Meat in Oaths: Hanafi Position
Ruling on raw meat in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that raw meat does not violate an oath not to eat meat and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Raw meat does not violate an oath to abstain from meat — custom and the immediate understanding of the oath govern.

When someone swears not to eat meat, does eating raw meat breach the oath? The Hanafi school, relying on custom and the intent of the swearer, generally holds that raw meat is not considered 'meat' for oath purposes. This lesson examines the evidence and the differences among Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The scenario: a person swears 'I will not eat meat' (halafa la ya'kulu lahman). Determine what constitutes 'meat' (lahm) in this context.
The passage distinguishes between cooked, roasted, dried (cooked forms) and raw meat.
2
Locate the Hanafi ruling on raw meat
From the text: Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl indicates that raw meat does NOT cause violation. The sources cited (Khulasa, Fatawa Abi al-Layth, al-Zahiriyyah) confirm this as the preferred view.
The phrase 'فهذا من محمد إشارة إلى أنه لا يحنث بالني' is key.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Though the preferred position is no violation, some Hanafi scholars (e.g., Abu al-Layth in one narration) held that raw meat does cause violation. Identify the two views within the school.
The text mentions 'وعند الفقيه أبي الليث يحنث' for raw meat.
4
Understand the basis: custom (urf)
The ruling is linked to custom: what people customarily consider as 'meat'. The Kufan custom distinguishes raw from cooked. This principle extends to local customs (e.g., Egypt).
The passage opens with 'في عرف أهل مصر' showing the role of urf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The text prefers the view of no violation with raw meat, citing Abu al-Layth's fatwa and al-Zahiriyyah. Thus, for fatwa, raw meat does not violate.
The authority_weight is zahir_riwayah based on Muhammad's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في عرف أهل مصر أيضاً فعلم أن ما في المختصر مبني على عرف أهل الكوفة، وأن ذلك يختلف باختلاف العرف. وفي الخلاصة وغيرها : لو جلف لا يأكل لحماً حنث بأكل لحم الإبل والبقر والغنم ،والطيور، مطبوخاً كان أو مشوياً أو قديداً كما ذكره في الأصل. فهذا من محمد إشارة إلى أنه لا يحنث بالني. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث عن أبي بكر الإسكاف أنه لا يحنث وهو الأظهر، ، وعند الفقيه أبي الليث يحنث. ولو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللحم شيئاً فأكل من مرقته لم يحنث إن لم يكن له نية المرقة .اهـ. وفي الظهيرية : الأشبه أنه لا يحنث بأكل النيء. وفي المحيط : حلف لا يأكل لحم شاة فأكل لحم عنز يحنث لأن الشاة اسم جنس فيتناول الشاة أي الضأن وغيرها وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في نوازله أنه لا يحنث، سواء كان الحالف قروياً أو مصرياً، وعليه الفتوى لأنهم يفرقون بينهما عادة. ولو حلف لا يأكل لحم بقرة لم يحنث بأكل لحم الجاموس لأنه وإن كان بقراً يعد في نصاب البقر ولكن خرج من اليمين بتعارف الناس .اهـ. وفي الخانية والرأس والأكارع لحم في يمين الأكل وليس بلحم في يمين الشراء .اهـ. وفي البدائع : حلف لا يأكل لحم دجاج فأكل لحم ديك حنث لأن الدجاج اسم للذكر والأنثى جميعاً فأما الدجاجة فاسم للأنثى والديك اسم الذكر، واسم الإبل يقع على الذكور والإناث، وكذا اسم الجمل والبعير والجزور، وهذه الأربعة تقع على البخاتي والعراب واسم البقر يقع على الأنثى والذكر كالشاة والغنم والنعجة اسم للأنثى والكبش للذكر والفرس لهما كالبغل والبغلة والحمار للذكر والحمارة والأتان للأنثى.
Also in the custom of the people of Egypt, so it is known that what is in al-Mukhtasar is based on the custom of the people of Kufa, and that this varies according to custom. In al-Khulasa and others: If one swore not to eat meat, he violates by eating the meat of camels, cows, sheep, and birds, whether cooked, roasted, or dried as mentioned in al-Asl. This from Muhammad indicates that he does not violate by raw meat. In Fatawa Abi al-Layth from Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf that he does not violate and it is the most apparent, and according to al-Faqih Abu al-Layth he violates. If one swore not to eat anything of this meat and ate from its broth, he does not violate if he had no intention for the broth. In al-Zahiriyyah: The most similar is that he does not violate by eating raw. In al-Muhit: If one swore not to eat sheep meat and ate goat meat, he violates because sheep is a generic name encompassing sheep and goats. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in his Nawazil that he does not violate, whether the swearer is a villager or an Egyptian, and upon it is the fatwa because they customarily differentiate between them. If one swore not to eat cow meat, he does not violate by eating buffalo meat because even though it is considered cattle in the nisab of cows, it is excluded from the oath by people's custom. In al-Khaniyya: The head and trotters are meat for an oath of eating but not meat for an oath of purchasing. In al-Bada'i: If one swore not to eat chicken meat and ate rooster meat, he violates because chicken is a name for both male and female. As for hen, it is a name for the female, and rooster is the name of the male. The name 'ibil' applies to males and females, likewise the names 'jamal', 'ba'ir', and 'jazur'. These four apply to both the Bakhiti and Arab breeds. The name 'baqar' applies to female and male, like 'shah' and 'ghanam'. 'Na'jah' is for the female and 'kabsh' for the male. 'Faras' applies to both like 'baghl' and 'baghlah'. 'Himar' is for the male, 'himarah' and 'atan' for the female.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on oaths related to eating meat, emphasizing the role of custom (urf) in determining what constitutes a violation. The author begins by noting that the rulings in al-Mukhtasar are based on Kufan custom, and that custom can vary, so the same oath may have different results in different regions. The core ruling from al-Khulasa states that if one swears not to eat meat, he violates by eating meat of camels, cows, sheep, and birds, whether cooked, roasted, or dried. However, Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Asl indicates that raw meat does not cause violation. This is further supported by Fatwa of Abu al-Layth that the apparent position is no violation with raw meat, though there is disagreement. On broth, the rule is that eating broth does not violate unless one intended it. On specific animals: if one swears not to eat sheep meat, eating goat meat generally violates because sheep is a generic term, but fatwa is given otherwise due to custom. Cow and buffalo are differentiated by custom. The head and trotters are considered meat for oaths of eating but not for purchasing. Finally, on chicken, the term includes both male and female, so eating rooster violates an oath not to eat chicken. The passage also clarifies gender-specific animal names. The overall lesson is that custom and common usage heavily influence the interpretation of oaths.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): Meat; in oaths, includes meat of animals typically eaten, but custom and context may restrict or expand.
نيء (nayy): Raw meat; does not cause violation in oaths not to eat meat according to the preferred position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi position, if someone swears not to eat meat and then eats raw sheep meat, what is the ruling?
A) He violates the oath.
B) He does not violate the oath.
C) He violates only if he intended raw meat.
D) He must ask a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Raw meat does not violate an oath not to eat meat in the Hanafi school due to custom and the authoritative texts.

Broth in Oaths: The Role of Intention
Ruling on broth in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that eating broth does not violate an oath not to eat meat unless the swearer intended to include broth.

Core Idea
Broth is not meat — an oath not to eat meat is not violated by consuming broth unless the swearer intended it.

When someone swears not to eat meat, does drinking the broth or gravy from meat count as a violation? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the meat itself and its derivative liquids, but intention can change the ruling. This lesson focuses on the principle that custom and intention shape the interpretation of oaths.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the general ruling
The text: 'If one swore not to eat anything of this meat and ate from its broth, he does not violate if he had no intention for the broth.' The default ruling is no violation.
The key phrase: 'لو حلف لا يأكل من هذا اللحم شيئاً فأكل من مرقته لم يحنث إن لم يكن له نية المرقة'.
2
Understand the condition: intention (niyyah)
The ruling hinges on the swearer's intention at the time of the oath. If he intended the broth, then drinking broth would violate. If he had no such intention, it does not.
Intention is a critical factor in oaths (al-umur bi-maqasidiha).
3
Identify the basis: literal vs. customary meaning
Broth (maraqah) is not considered 'lahm' in common usage. The oath is interpreted according to the customary meaning, not a technical or extended definition.
This parallels the raw meat ruling: custom governs what constitutes 'meat'.
4
Apply to other scenarios
If a person swears 'I will not eat meat' and later drinks a soup made with meat broth, he does not violate unless he consciously included broth in his oath.
The principle extends to all liquid derivatives of meat.

Source Text

Custom and Animal Specifics in Oaths
Rulings on specific animals and parts in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules that custom and common usage determine whether eating a specific animal or part violates an oath about meat.

Core Idea
Custom determines whether an oath about meat includes specific animals and parts — not just the literal category.

What if someone swears not to eat 'sheep meat' but then eats goat? Or swears not to eat 'cow meat' but eats buffalo? The Hanafi school relies heavily on custom (urf) and the common understanding of animal names. This lesson explores several examples from the text, showing how custom can include or exclude certain animals and parts from an oath.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the sheep vs. goat scenario
The text: 'If one swore not to eat sheep meat (lahm shah) and ate goat meat, he violates because sheep (shah) is a generic name encompassing both sheep and goats.' However, Abu al-Layth in his Nawazil says he does not violate, and this is the fatwa because people customarily differentiate between them.
Two views: literal generic meaning vs. custom.
2
Analyze the cow vs. buffalo scenario
If one swears not to eat cow meat (lahm baqar) and eats buffalo meat, he does not violate because even though buffalo is considered cattle (baqar) in zakat nisab, it is excluded by people's custom.
Custom overrides the technical legal definition.
3
Analyze the head and trotters
Head (ra's) and trotters (akari') are considered 'meat' for an oath of eating, but not for an oath of purchasing. This shows that the context of the oath (eating vs. buying) changes what 'meat' means.
Again, custom and context matter.
4
Analyze chicken vs. rooster
If one swears not to eat chicken meat (lahm dajaj) and eats rooster meat, he violates because 'dajaj' is a name for both male and female. The text gives linguistic guidelines: 'dajaj' is generic, 'dajajah' is female, 'deek' is male.
Here the literal generic meaning prevails, not custom.
5
Synthesize the principle
Overall, the interpretation of an oath about meat depends on: (1) the literal scope of the animal name, (2) common custom (urf) that may narrow or expand that scope, (3) the context (eating vs. buying).
The passage repeatedly shows that custom can override a literal or technical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
شاة (shah): Sheep; linguistically may include goats, but custom can differentiate.
جاموس (jamus): Buffalo; considered cattle technically but excluded from oaths about cow meat by custom.
رأس وأكارع (ra's wa akari'): Head and trotters; ruled as meat for eating oaths but not for purchasing.
دجاج (dajaj): Chicken (generic, includes male and female); eating rooster violates an oath not to eat chicken.

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, if someone swears not to eat cow meat, does eating buffalo meat violate the oath?

Key Takeaway
